One ballade (and not a bad’un) in strict form as
under received from G. Innes, Richmond, Vic.—
to whom 10,s. (kl. has been sent:
Remarks :
Too like the interesting rattlesnake. Wants a few
sonorous polysyllables.
A rule of good Eng. verse is to let stress and quantity
go together : in other words, to drop your metrical
accent on your long vowel, and only for cause and ex-
ceptionally to slur a weighty syllable. In (e.g.)
Turn love’s triremes to the main
Turn Lowe’s might pass as broken metre ; but one breach
in one line is enough or too much. The fine word tri-
remes, following, is spoilt utterly—hurried past recogni-
tion ; and one trireme is plenty for “ lovers twain,” who
(in the office experience) prefer to sit close.
,JL urn .follows turves too closely ; and lovers who row on
the main in triremes should meet a watery death— i.e.,
there is a lack of metaphorical harmony, and the more
when the multiplicity of Love’s attributes is noted.
The ballade is a little too serious for the envoy.
To avoid the factory atmosphere, a ballade should have
continuity (as of narrative), consistency (ideas to theme,
as rays to sun), and a consecutive development of idea to
the clinching envoy, &c., &c.
Still, it’s a pretty fair result.
jt
And— p.s. —by the last post comes this, which
does not alter judgment, but is printed as tribute
to ingenious industry (Merv’s !) The author sug-
gests that ‘ ‘ conserves and deserves can be con-
sidered ” ; but No !
“ Ouida,” as Richard Burton’a. intimate friend,
tells The Fortnightly that Wright’s life fails
lamentably to do him justice; and she gives her
own thought of the man “so unique, so singular,
so many-sided, so extremely startling to all
commonplace people.”
“Did you really shoot, that Arab boyl asked him
once ; for the killing of the Arab boy was always beiii"-
cast up against him. ' °
“Oh, yes,” he answered. “Why not? Do you sup-
pose one can live :n those countries as one lives in Pall
Mall and Piccadilly ?”
And he laughed ; with contemptuous remembrance
probably, of the tolerance of his nation for the Deadly
Trades which make plutocrats of those who kill men and
women by the thousand before their thirtieth year in
their factories and furnaces.
Reside him most other men looked poor crea-
tures,” says Ouida.
In the eyes of women he had the unpardonable fault:
he loved his wife. He would have been a happier and a
greater man if he had had no wife j but his love for her
was extreme ; it was a source of weakness, as most warm
emotions are in the lives of strong men. Their marriage
was romantic and clandestine ; a love-marriage in the
most absolute sense of the words, not wise on either side
but on each impassioned. She adored him, and, like
most women who adore, she was not always wise. She
was of great courage and intelligence, and shone in society
even as she suffered solitude and met danger with forti-
tude. She was as happy in the great world of London as
in the ruined cities of Asia, and could adapt herself to
the most varying circumstance with equal spirit and pa-
tience ; she was exceedingly tender and humane to
animals, and of unswerving fortitude and resolution in
all kinds of peril. What made the one weakness in her
character was the religious superstition which is the rift
in the lute of so many a female soul. Like all her family
she was a devoted Catholic; this bigotry increased with
years, and after Burton’s death became so great that it
made hei actually burn the MS. of one of his most pre-
cious translations, because she deemed it of immoral
tendency. This. I confess, I could never pardon her •
and I never spoke or wrote to her after the irreparable’
act.
‘He could have lived on a shilling a day and a
few good cigars. -That might he Burton's epi-
taph ; for if it does not tell everything it tells
more.

J.U.A.
“THE SAVAGN TURNING..."
“ NE theory of the development of ideas
■ I suggests that, in inheriting the ances-
tral thought-machine, we inherit also
a number of latent ancestral thoughts.
That is, the hereditary brain-cells, though
they be composed of entirely fresh nerve-
plasma, are yet arranged after an ancestral
plan beyond our control, and can spon-
taneously generate thoughts in which individual
experience has no part. The theory is but an
extension to conscious thought of a power well-
known to reside in hereditary nerve-cells when
automatic impulse is concerned. Conscious indi-
vidual brain-acquirement, for example, can have
no share in the nerve-control of the fcetal heart.
N. S. a haler, Harvard professor of geology, who
writes of this and kindred subjects in Ins book
about The Individual, relates his endeavours to
capture the spontaneous thoughts that seem just
to rise to the level of consciousness —to be always
flitting over the edge of the conscious horizon ;
and finds that one s capacity to think in a way
apparently independent of his own experience
can be strengthened and increased by deliberate
effort.”
In June Nineteenth Century the Rev. Forbes
Phillips has a curious article in support of this
theory, and he gives experience in point.
Some ten years ago I paid my first visit to Rome. Again
and again within the city there came these flashes of re-
cognition. The Baths of Oaracalla, the Appian Way, the
Catacombs of St. Calixtus, the Colp/semn—all .seemed
familiar to me. The reason appeared obvious. I was re-
newing my acquaintance with what 1 had seen in pictures
and photographs. That might explain the buildings, but
not the dark underground windings of the Catacombs. A
few days later I was out at Tivoli. Here, again, suddenly
the whole place and country-side were as familiar to me
as my own parish. I found myself struggling with a
torrent of words, describing what it was like in the olden
days. Up to that time I had read nothing of Tivoli. I
had seen no views ; only a few days previous to my visit
had I heard of its existence, and here I was acting as
guide and historian to a party of friends, who concluded
that I had made a special study of the place and neigh-
bourhood ; then the vision in my mind began to fade. I
stopped like a man who for the time has forgotten his
part, and I could say no more. It was as if a mosaic had
dropped to pieces, leaving only a few remaining fragments
still in situ, and presently these receded from my grasp.
d*
On another occasion I was with a companion in the
neighborhood of Leatherhead, where I had never been
before. The country was quite new to me and to my
friend. In the course of conversation he remarked:
“They say there is part of an old Roman road some-
where round here, but I don’t know whether it is on this
side of Leatherhead or the other.” At once I said “ I
know,” and led the way with certainty in my mind that I
knew where we should find it, which we did; and
there was the feeling that I had been on that road
before, riding, and that I had worn armour. Such inci-
dents have caused me from time to time to pursue this
subject among my friends, and quite a number of them
can quote similar experiences. To the west, 3J miles
from where I live, is a Roman fortress in an almost
perfect state of preservation. A clergyman called upon
me one day and asked me to accompany him there for an
examination of the ruins. He told me he had a distinct
recollection of living there, and that he held some office
of a priestly nature in the days of the Roman occupation.
One fact struck me as significant. He' insisted on
examining a ruined tower which had bodily overturned.
“ There used to be a socket in the top of it,” he went on,
“ in which we used to plant a mast, and archers used to
be hauled to the top in a basket protected with leather
from which they picked off the leaders among the ancient
Gorlestonians.” We found the socket lie had indicated.
I urged him to publish many tilings he told me that day,
but he shook his head. “The time is not ripe,” he
replied.
And Phillips continues—
Whether we believe in apparitions or not, this world is
a haunted one. Our thought-world is full of deep under-
tones that roll in upon us from the past. As we lay our
ear to the din of the present, we find its accompaniment
to be the in measurable murmur of the ages, as the voice
of many waters. The commonplace expressions, the
ordinary words we use, are blocks of mind-stuff, wrought
into their present state by the ponderous inaee of time,
and cast and recast in many brains.
And the mind of man is a haunted one. Far-away-
generations of ancestors have cut deep the channels of
our memories until what was once a volition is now an
involuntary movement. We say a man has formed cer-
tain habits, but how often they have been formed for
him in the dim past.
“ Have you ever felt, on seeing a place for the
first time, that you have been there before ? This
is a favourite question of mine,” says Phillips,
“ and in quite 30 per cent, of the answers I get
something which bears directly on the theory of a
racial memory.” The subject is interesting, and
possibly some Bulletin readers can add illustra-
tions.
4.
“Punctuality”, writes Mrs. John Lane, “isan
unamiable virtue and very plebeian in everyone
but a king. It is always the punctual who lose
their tempers waiting for the unpunctual, and to
lose your temper is the thin end of the wedge for
the perpetration of the worst crimes. I suppose
the angels are always unpunctual, or they wouldn’t
be so sweet tempered. I don’t believe the punctual
are ever destined to be angels, for already on
earth they get so soured. So one cannot help
thinking that Heaven must be a rather unpunc-
tual place.”
d*
An article on ballades was printed here some
years ago, and apparently should have been
printed again. A ballade, in English, is not a
ballad. It is definitely a set of three eight-lined
stanzas and one four-lined, in any metre, with
the rhymes (1) a, b, a, b, b, c, b, c ; (2) a , b, a, b, b,
c, b, e; (3) a, b, a. b, b, c, b, c; (4) b, v, b, c.
The last line in every stanza is the same. No
syllable once used as a rhyme can be used again
(a fresh prefix or fresh spelling makes no differ-
ence). And it is a point of honour to play the
game, though in light or lazy verse players tire
often. To add, there are the ballade of ten-line
stanzas, the double ballade (as J.C.A.’s) and the
ballade wich double refrain ; but the common
form will serve endeavourers awhile. When
another refrain comes, we’ll try anew.
MEMORANDA
An interesting Melbourne enthusiast is Jameg
Caithness, vellum-binder and marbler with the
George .Robertson Proprietary. Book-marbling
or paper-marbling is a handicraft, but fully con-
sidered it is chemistry, and properly seen it is
an art.
What do you marble ? Book, edges and
book end-papers. (End-papers are the first two
and last two leaves that figure in or disfigure any
book, one of each pair being pasted or glued down
upon the cover.) How do you marble ? Thus:
You take a trough of appropriate size and fill it
with gum-tragacanth, a little below the consis-
tency of treacle. This is the matrix of the im-
pressions which you desire to make upon paper;
theft, from pots of colour—red, green, yellow, and
blue, or other—you take their respective brushes,
which, being flirted and fluttered above the
trough, leave the colour in streaks, or spots, or
spatters. Here is room for the artist, who combs
the superficial layer of colour into a paradise of
dainty devices that fall practically into notorious
patterns—Spanish, or Dutch, or shell, or antique,
and so on. Then a sheet of paper is laid upon tha
pattern, is lifted, and behold an end-paper: which,
when dry, may or may not be burnished.
The chemist is concerned with composition of the
paints. These are made from solid or moist colour,
but there is need of combination with gum, or with
turpentine, or with other ingredients, that the
result may be stable on a shifting surface, and
stable in the end. Here the economical man who
can use a red at 3s. Gd. a lb., and get a colour
equal to the pigments at 255. a lb., is the man
who shines. Caithness, whose firm binds for
Adelaide and Melbourne public libraries, is pure
fanatic in his profession. He talks marbling, he
dreams marbling, his marbling has won highest
praise from American experts. He is the one
man in the Australian Commonwealth to whom a
beautifully marbled end-paper is the most beauti-
ful thing in the world. Hence this meed of
curious praise.
*
Time and again Theodosia Garrison convinces
one that in her bosom throbs the pulse of poetry—
a pulse not too tutored, with ever and anon an in-
surrectionary surge. Her verses have the vitality
that one meets not often in American magazines,
yet less often in English (as becomes): and their
emotion is so genuine that just a touch of Landor’s
form or Poe’s would make them classic. In a
recent piece she tells of many children “sprung
of my heart and my brain ” —Content, and Etfse,
and Comfort, and Success, and Power; but one
child has fled:
Well do my children guard me, jealous of this their right'.
Carefully, soberly, ever by daylight and candlelight ,
But oh, for my Prodigal Joy of Youth
Somewhere out in the night!
J*
In New Guinea, according to A. E. Pratt, they
grow their own tobacco. It is very coarse and
rank.
“In fact, the natives themselves cannot inhale
much, as it makes them giddy ; and they are not
infrequently seized with severe fits of coughing
when the fumes have proved particularly
suffocating.
“The method of smoking is elaborate. They roll
a leaf into a little horn, and insert it in the
smaller hide on the side of the ‘ Bau-bau,’ within
this leaf is placed the charge of tobacco which
they light, and then placing their lips to the end
hole they draw. The little horn, or cigarette as
one may call it, is now removed from the holein
the side, and if the pipe is new they blow
away the first charge of smoke, by placing their
lips to the hole in which the cigarette was origi-
nally inserted.
“ Again the cigarette is placed in the small hole,
and the pipe is drawn from the end hole. This
time the smoke is intended to be used, so the
cigarette is removed from the small hole, and the
smoker applying his lips thereto inhales the whole
charge. Again the cigarette is removed, and the
pipe is filled by a long pull at the end hole, but
this time the smoker does not inhale the charge
himself, but removes the cigarette and politely
hands the charged pipe to his neighbour, who
punctiliously rubs the mouthpiece, and enjoys the
long whiff.
“Very often there is one drawer for an entire
party, whose duty it is to fill the pipe with smoke,
and pass it so filled to each of his companions in
turn. , .
“ The supply of tobacco is carried irr the armlet
or behind the ear—this last method being not
unknown to the festive Cockney, who, on Bank
Holiday, is seldom complete without a cigarette
so worn.”

Tracks in the Snow, by G. R. Benson, is a
realistic story of a murder and the pursuit of the
criminal —not half bad, and about GO per cent,
good. The realism is gained in the approved way
of multiplying details, and the book follows life
SO" photographically that it seems “as true as
death.” Some of the detailsare tedious, but some
of the details of life and death are tedious. We
think legal minds should follow the t me/.* with
attention.

THE BRIDE.
O, little face, so fair to kiss,
As clean and sweet as Heaven's dew ;
O, lips that only babble love ;
O, hoping eyes, I hope for you.
O, little hands, so white, sofair
To war in weary ways of toil:
O, little feet, so free, so wild
To run upon the thorny soil.
O, heart, with many loves to hold,
O, harper of the trembling strings,—
I doubt not, God may show you yet
The radiance of eternal things.
S.N.

You have suffered long and seriously. Why not let us assist you in the realisation of rapid relief and
complete cure? THIS WE CAN DO WITHOUT DOUBT if you will confide in us. Read the following
description or Keynote Symptoms, and if you have any of them you may rest assured you require the
remarkable treatment that has won name and fame for .the Freeman and Wallace Electro-Medical

FAILING VIGOR.
When this begins to go, you
will twitch and show signs ot
premature weakness. Fol-
lowing are some of the symp-
toms :
Your facial nerves twitch,
You are all run down,
Spots before your eyes,
The muscles twitch,
Pains in small of back,
Pains in head,
Shooting pains,
You feel fatigued,
You are weary,
Your head swims,
Your knees knock,
Your hand trembles.
NERVOUS TWITCH-
ING.
A nervous person easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitching.
Here are some symptoms :
Your eyes twitch,
Your hands tremble,
You feel shaky,
You walk haltingly,
You are melancholy,
Ringing in the ears,
Blood is poor,
Your eyes weak,
Your eyes bleary,
Your head swims.
DEBILITY
This affliction destroys ambition,
organic strength, energy, and
hope. Following are symp-
toms :
You feel weak,
You feel cold,
Your back aches,
You lose flesh,
You sleep poorly,
Are low-spirited,
Your eyes sunken,
You have hot flushes,
Your memory impaired,
No real vigor,
You have sick headaches,
Nausea after eating,
Hands or feet moist,
Brightness leaves the eye.

OF DEATH. AVOID IT !

DR. ELIZABETH WHITE, M.D., Specialiste in Diseases of Women and Children.
AND AT MELBOURNE.
In the Public Interest.
One of the most famous and scientifically replete
medical institutions in the Southern World is The
Freeman & Wallace Electro-Medical and Surgical
Institute, which has been established in Sydney
for upwards of 20 years, and is known far and
wide throughout Australia by virtue of its colossal
mail methods and themany remarkable cures which
are effected by the medium of its parcel post
system of medical treatment. Were it not for the
fact that this system is a most effective and rapid
one, it is questionable whether the numerous
patients who are fortunately treated by the Insti-
tute would recover as rapidly as they testify.
Men residing in all parts of Oceana, even as far
as Thursday Island to the north, New Caledonia
to the east, South Africa to the west, and Tasmania
to the south, have at various times consulted
the Specialists attached to the Medical Staff of
the Institute per medium of correspondence. The
system is so effective that a letter posted from
West Australia is received and replied to in a few
days with that promptitude of despatch at all
times associated with an effective medical service.
There are thousands of men in Australia to-day
who are grateful for having used this service, and
who would, had they not done so, no doubt, from
the character of the desperate diseases from which
they suffered, have been in their graves.
The medical statistics of the Institute prove
that, within the last 10 years over 10,000 men have
been permanently cured of diseases which are in-
timately associated with the Male Sex. Testi-
monials are received daily from all parts of Aus-
tralia from men who have not only for years
suffered, but who have, at the hands of incompe-
tent, unskilful, and unscientific practitioners, had
their maladies aggravated instead of cured.
The general practitioner is not, in a sense, a
Specialist; his work is too general and his prac-
tice too confined to allow him to have that
thorough knowledge which is absolutely essential
to the eradication of certain forms of disease,
which demand a special skill, special study, and a
special knowledge. One of the most famous of
Russian pathologists recently, in a book which he
has published, and which lyis startled the medical
world, admits that although it may be the policy
of the practitioner to cure, in many instances he
most emphatically kills his patient: He admits,
in this essential work, his many errors, also the
errors of his scientific brethren, and alludes
pitifully to the faith which the general public
accords to men who, by virtue of the scientific
altitude to which they have risen and the scientific
honor which they have reaped in the fields of ex-
periment, desire to pose before the world as
medical saviours of mankind. Although this
book deals principally with the Russian school
of medicine, still fact generalises the truth of
this pathologist’s writings, and most emphati-
cally can such a system of incompetence be
localised to the medical schools of Australia. From
time to time, through the columns of the Aus-
tralian press, much may be learned as to the
incompetence of the general surgeon and of the
systems governing many localhospitals. Specialism
is in itself a profession, and even the phenomena
of a single disease are sufficient to call forth the
study of a single lifetime. There are certain
classes of disorders which afflict the male popula-
tion which the Specialists of the Institute have
studied completely, and are in a position to speak
emphatically upon the probability and the impro-
bability of cure.
The Specialists have a few private words to say
direct to suffering men. If you are ailing and feel
that you are not what you should be at your age;
if you are run down, ill at ease in your mind,
nervous, irritable, and despondent you should
consult Dr. Richard Wallace, M.D., the Physician-
in-Chief of the Institute, who is Registered by
the Australian, English, and American Medical
Boards. He is a Specialist of high order in the
treatment of Men’s Disorders, and in all Private,
Nervous, Skin and Blood Disorders. He may be
consulted personally, or by letter, in all affections
of the Nervous System, Blood and Skin Disorders,
Bladder Troubles, Rupture, Varicocele, Diseases
of the Prostrate Glands, and in all Chronic
Troubles of Men and Women, such as Asthma,
Rheumatism, Gout, Bronchitis, Piles, Chronic
Indigestion, Catarrh, Lumbago, Sciatica, Liver
and Kidney Complaints, etc.
These lines should be read and memorised by
all Young, Middle-aged, or Old Men. It will be
of infinite value to them if they lack Vital Force,
Energy, Muscular Power, Mental Determination,
or Nerve Strength, or in cases of young men who
have failed to attain perfect manhood. Many
Middle-aged men lack Perfect Strength and Vigor,
whether by reason of care, privation and expo-
sure, over-work, brain-strain, or other causes, and
find their physical powers declining at an age
when Nature intended them to be in the Prime
of their Power and Strength. Amongst old men,
their condition of waning vitality is attributed by
them to the result of old age or senile decay,
whereas it may be simply the result of want of cor-
rect Medical treatment, rendering the body power-
less to reproduce its vitality unaided. To all such
afflicted ones the Specialists’ system is offered as
a means of affording, not temporary alleviation,
but positive removal from many disabilities. Men
of any age whatever should note that the
Specialists may be consulted Free of Charge
where their opinions may be deemed of value in
serious cases demanding expert opinion. Thou-
sands of men have been cured through having
written to them from far and distant parts, where
medical men were few and inexperienced. Spe-
cialists’ modern scientific methods having been
followed by adoption of their systems of home
treatment, the sufferer soon improved, and ulti-
mately regained his health. During the last fifteen
years the Institute’s records prove that countless
men would now have been buried had they not
communicated with the Medical Staff, and placed
themselves under treatment for serious afflictions
admitting of no neglect or tampering. The reader
should study carefully the advertisement on this
page, which is calculated to bring comfort and
nope to the most despondent sufferer.
Read the Following Testimonies of Eminent
Citizens who have been Cured by the
Freeman and Wallace Treatment
INSPECTOR F. FOWLER, Chief Officer commanding:
the police division at Burwood, wrote :—“Since adopting

suffered from I seldom ever experience. As you are
aware, I feel so much benefited that I have recom-
mended the treatment to many of my friends.”
Mr. PHILIP NEWBURY, Australia’s Favorite Tenor,
says : —“ I have tried a course of your Electro-Medical
treatment, and it has benefited me to a great degree.
For some years I suffered from Uric Acid in the Blood,
causing Rheumatic tendencies, but, thanks to the
wonderful efficacy of your ELECTRO-MEDICAL treat-
ment, I am now completely cured.”
Mr. JOSEPH MEGSON, the well-known Cyclist of

tional break-up more than once. The merits of your
treatment received by me are beyond question.”
Mr. G. H. BERNARD, of Ganmain Station, via Wagga,
wrote:—“l am getting on steadily and surely. My
nerves are in a much better state now. lam not now
subject to those severe frontal headaches. My brain is
quite capable of tackling any reasonable amount laid
upon it.”
Again he says at a later date :—“ I must say that for
years I have not felt so well as I am now doing. The
half-yearly balance was robbed of all its trials, thanks
to the admirable trim in which you had my nerves and
brain.”
Mr. GEORGE E. KNIGHT, of Simpson-street, Wei
lington, says : —“ Your good work in my case is now com-
plete, and you have done all that is possible to be done,
and I will ever remain yours gratefully.”
Mr. ALFRED RUMING, of Stonehenge, thus describes
his condition : —“ I must certainly say that I cannot now
complain of any pains or aches whatever ; in fact, I con-
sider myself a new being. At one time life was a misery
to me, now it seems Paradise, and I was never better in
my life than now.”
Mr. .T. BOULDEN, of Camberwell, Victoria, who
suffered from rheumatism for years, after a three-months’

testimony:—“For years I suffered from rheumatism,
which was of synovial and articular character, and tried

You are at liberty to publish this testimony, also my
portrait, in the interest of the public.”
Mr. 11. CUMMINS, of Hobart (Tasmania), who suffered
from Nervous Prostration, declares:— 11 I wish to convey
to yon my heartfelt gratitude for your skilful assistance
rendered to me in my afflictions. I had been for years a
great sufferer, and at times nearly despondent. I had
been treated by the most able and skilled doctors that
could he found in this colony. I expended large sums of
money with several doctors, and was coolly told that
there was no hope for a cure. One of your pamphlets
fell into my hands which treated upon my case. You so
fully described and explained all my symptoms that I
felt satisfied you understood my complaint thoroughly, so
I determined to pay you a visit. I was thoroughly
examined by you and pronounced curable. I placed my-
self under your care. Within a week of commencing the
remedies you prescribed I felt better. To-day I can con-

S£S THE I.ABEU ON THE SELYEDQE.
Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The ponds in Sydney’s Centennial Park
are fast going into the possession of the gluttonous
shag. It is futile to attempt fish acclimatisation
with myriads of these birds about. Now that
young trout are being introduced to N.S.W.
rivers, the shags should be looked to. One time,
there was 6d. a head on them, but instead of that,
the Government now provides them with a safe
breeding-ground, from which they can scour the
country.
“ The Hack ” tells some extraordinary
stories :
Re the truth of the tradition that the mate of a married
snake which has come to an untimely end will, within
24 hours thereafter, be found on the spot marked by an
x (x marks the scene of the tragedy vide Sydney Weekly
Crime). Some years ago I was strolling through the
scrub outside Portland (Vic.), when the most horrible
sensation I ever experienced went through me, and I
pulled up just in time to avoid treading on a black snake
which lay stretched across the narrow cattle-track. It
was the first snake I had seen outside a cage, and, being
anxious to distinguish myself, I hunted round till I found
a stout piece of driftwood. Then I stole cautiously back,
and smote that snake the hardest smite I was capable of,
The snake didn’t offer any objection, but, to prevent acci-
dents, I continued to assault and batter it for five
minutes. Then I turned it over with my cudgel, and
found to my disgust that it had been dead all the while,
for it was minus its head. Some three yards further
on, however, I almost trod on another snake—this time
a live one. It glided away before me, but I succeeded
in killing it. A few mornings later, I killed another black
snake, and hung it over a fence. .Returning some four
hours afterwards, I was surprised to see a live snake
curled round the rail alongside the dead one. Biking to
Port Fairy (Vic.), a year later, I came across a recently-
killed black snake right in the centre of the road, with a
live one coiled beside it. Latter raised its head as I rode
up, but made no effort to escape. I killed it with my
bike-pump.
Remember, also, being told a remarkable story of this
snake-peculiarity by the lighthouse-keeper at Cape Nelson
(Vic.). He o: ce (so he alleged) killed a tiger-snake, and
hung it up in the lighthouse to scare his mate. Mrs.
Tiger-snake arrived before liis mate, however, and nearly
scared the life out of him by suddenly springing at him
through the doorway' of the room where her husband’s
corpse was suspended. He had hardly disposed of her
when her eldest son arrived and took up the quarrel.
During the following month, he avers, the other members
of the family arrived one after another til! the whole of
them were exterminated by the slaughter of a tenth-
cousin of the first murdered. I won’t vouch for the truth
of this yarn, however.
Re snakes. Are your other readers affected by them
as I am? They always fill me with an extraordinary feel-
ing of horror. 1 don’t think the feeling is one of fear, for
I have always attempted to kill them when I have seen
them, even when I might have avoided them. Nor have
I experienced such a feeling regarding any other danger,
though, in my brief career, I have had a number of
narrow escapes. For instance, was caught, while a
youngster, in a quicksand off Sumner Beach (Maoriland);
fell from a bridge into a ravine 20ft. deep (Maoriland);
was a passenger on the ill-fated Wairarapa on the trip
during which it caught fire in mid-ocean ; was bogged in
a suburban clay-hole and nearly drowned (Melbourne) ;
have been swept quarter of a mile along an open sewer
(Melbourne);-lost in the bush for two days (Mt. Gambier);
upset in the Yarra three times boating (couldn’t swim at
the time, either); tossed by a bull (Redesdale); bitten by a
tarantula, poisoned by meat from a cook-shop, and twice
knocked down by bolting horses ; was crushed under a
fence round the* Equitable building (Melb.) during a
crane collapse in which, I think, three men were killed ;
have fallen 30ft. from a gum tree, had 20 tons of books
and shelving collapse on me, been wakened by a fireman
smashing in my bedroom window', was a passenger on the
Cintra on the trip during which it was burnt out in Port-
land Bay (Vic.), also once climbed the cliffs right behind
the Portland rifle-butts just in time to be greeted by the
first volley of the afternoon’s practice (another man was
shot dead in the same plaee through doing the same
thing about a year later), and have had about thirty
bicycle Yet I’d sooner go through the whole
lot again than see a snake wriggling in the grass at my
feet. Why
My Lady Silence.
The side sun on the mountains makes
Red the decaying gums,
When through the bushes and the breaks
My Lady Silence comes.
All day she curls her white limbs there,
In shadow-folded dells;
But never a wanderer wanders where
My Lady Silence dwells.
Fast in her silken net she holds
Dead butterflies of sound ;
All song and speech she takes and folds
Into her sleep profound.
Sleep is her coronet, her domain
Spans the wide country, fills
The grey-lit town of want and pain
With peace from folded hills.
Sounds of still waters are her voice,
Spent winds sleep in her hair ;
The cries of dead men who rejoice,
Enshroud her, slumbering there.
She holds the dream of coming bliss,
The half-forgot desires;
The blue mist on the mountain is
The smoke of her far fires.
But when the train shrieks to the cloud,
And ploughs the silent deeps,
Amongst the bushlands in her shroud,
My Lady Silence weeps.
When new days light the township roofs,
I hear—the morn begun—
The sound of her free horses’ hoofs
Rush, fearful, from the sun.
VIC.
FURNLEY MAURICE.
The Singer.
I watch from out my curtained room
The anxious crowd press down the street;
Below, their faces white the gloom ;
Above, the dull roofs almost meet.
They never pause, they hurry by,
The smoke, the din, are everywhere,
A narrow strip of clouded sky
Is all I have of light and air.
But sometimes at the break of day,
When stars are fading into blue,
A Singer wanders down the way,
Singing as if the world were new.
“/ know a hidden place” he sings,
“ Where dew drops hold the earliest light,
And silent, happy, growing things
Blossom in crimson, blue, awl white ;
The dawn-wind, stirs the snowy thorn,
And high in air its fragrance flings —
There wakes a rainbow-tinted morn
In my far-hidden place,” he sings.
And sometimes when the moon is high
(At each mid-month she’s overhead),
My careless singer loiters by
And sings of days remembered.
He sings : “ Oh distant eve, awake
Green-silvered thro ’ the orchard bloom !
Leave, Nightingale, for love’s dear sake
Your silence and sequestered gloom!
Far up, the last of daylight dies, ■
Down orchard aisles the far song rings
Wood-bowered, a farther yet replies
Across the quiet dark,” he sings.
Then to my stifled heart there comes
A waking all too wildly sweet,
I lose the mutter of the slums,
The dust and thunder of the street.
The healing silence wraps me round.
The sunshine makes me whole again ;
Oh dreamful ease ! Nor sight nor sound
Can call me back to toil and pain.
What matter if the way be long,
The blessed end or far or near
While echo-faint I hear the song
As fairy music fine and clear !
He sings; “ Beyond the dreary town
I know a deep untrodden way
That wanders greenly up and down
Toward the clear birthplace of the day.
The shafted sunlight creeps and clings
Athwart that moss-grown way and dim, —
Come follow, follow me,” he sings.
And I—l rise and follow him.
QUEENSLAND.
A.W.
The Widow.
Sitting alone in the shadow,
While the glad children play
Out on the bright-eyed meadow
Ever by day and day.
“ She is the Widow,” the neighbors
Say, if'you ask her name ;
Morning and noon she labors,
Stitching away her shame.
All the young children love her,
Clamber upon her knee ;
She is the children’s “mother”—
Only one child had she.
Back from the love-fields weeping,
Once in the long ago.
Came a young mother reaping
Harvest of bitter woe.
Blue is the sky of morning—
Blue as the deep-sea wave—
Blue is the daisy awning
Over a tiny grave.
Sitting alone in the shadow,
While the glad children play
Out on the bright-green meadow
Ever by day and day.
Think we of them, my brothers—
We who are all to blame —
The white-souled, ringless mothers
Stitching away their shame ?
TAS.
G. LOUIS RANDALL.

A VIVID ACCOUNT.
FOR YOUR BENEFIT.
THE CASE OF MR. S. SHAW.
(By a Sydney Heporter.)
A vivid account appears belows of the extraordinary experiences of Mr. Samuel Shaw, of No. 208
Liverpool-street, Sydney, where he has been living for the past fifteen years.
“ It is over half a century ago since I came from Manchester to this country,” Mr. Shaw observed
to a reporter, “and up to a certain period I had nothing to complain of about my health. Then my
luck changed, and for fully ten years I had an annual visit from a complaint that seemed to have got
hold me for good.”
“ What was the nature of it ? ” enquired the pressman.
“It was neuralgia that paid me those yearly attentions, but in addition to that I was greatly
troubled with indigestion. Indeed, I blame the latter for causing the former, as it was when I
became.unable to digest my food, that I first noticed how sadly my general health was falling off.
My system seemed to go to pieces bit by bit, and then my nerves had just about broken down alto-
gether, when my physical tortures were at their worst. The pains in my head were so terribly severe
that the softest pillow felt as if it were filled with sharp edged stones that cut right into me. All
about my jaws and gums I was so sore and tender that 1 could not bear anything to touch me, and I
would flinch from the application of a piece of wadding as though it was a hard, rough substance of a
penetrating character.”
“ I suppose your sleep would be greatly interfered with ? ”
“Oh ! don’t mention anything about sleep, for I positively dread to recall the numberless nights
that I put in without getting any at all—simply turning from side to side in such agony that often I
wished myself dead instead of existing in such misery. Sometimes the pains would take one side of
my head and face, and on other occasions the opposite side would be affected. My eyes, round the
back of my neck —goodness ! I don’t know where the pain did not exist. It was awful, and you can
easily imagine how much suffering can make one’s life a burden to him. I know mine was, for I was
dreadfully melancholy. There was always some fearful thing going to happen, according to my mind.
Troubleineveiy direction, and the strange part of it was that, although the calamities did not occur when I
imagined they would, still I could not help worrying over the same notions on subsequent days.
That will show you what an unhappy frame of mind I found myself in owing to my excessive
weakness, and it was surprising how irritable the least thing used to make me. The slightest
noise coming suddenly threw me into a state of excitement and caused my nerves to shake quite
visibly, and if I exerted myself a little bit the trembling was just as bad. The trouble was that
the longer my sufferings continued the worse they became. My organs of digestion were getting
more incapable of doing their work every day, and although, owing to the poorness of my appe-
tite, I took but very little food, still it was always enough to greatly increase the torments I was
already enduring.”
“ You had made efforts to overcome those afflictions, I expect ?”
“Repeatedly I had tried, but it all seemed useless. Years ago I had consulted a doctor in Victoria,
and again in Sydney I had placed myself under the care of several medical experts, who could not
cure me, and naturally I thought my load of suffering would eventually drag me down to the grave.
My attempts to get better did not cease when the doctors’ medicines failed. No ; I tried many others,
only to be again doomed to disappointment, for the heavy feelings in the chest were still there after
taking a little food, the annoying pains between my shoulders had not moved an inch, and the
aching sensations across my loins were just as troublesome as ever. I still got up with a coated tongue
and a nasty taste of a morning, the sudden giddy turns had not yet been checked, and the appalling
sense of utter feebleness and dejection was bearing down upon me more forcibly and more alarmingly
than ever. One thing was certain—that a change one way or the other would soon take place, and it
was a decided change for the better that came, and the cause of it all was nothing but Clements’
Tonic, and that is the reason why I have since recommended that remedy to hundreds of other people,
who have always thanked me for my advice afterwards. Do you know that I had quite given up hope
of ever being cured, and had lost all faith in medicines because of having tried every one of them
without any good resulting, and would never have taken Clements’ Tonic only that I happened to
pick up an old paper and saw in it the names of some influential people, friends of mine, who were
testifying to its efficacy. That day I got some Clements’ Tonic, and the very first night I obtained a
little ease.”
“ How long ago is that ?”
“Eight years or more, and I am indeed grateful to be able to say that ever since my health has
been all that I could wish, for by continuing Clements’ Tonic for a while I succeeded in throwing off
all the ailments that had made me thoroughly sick and tired of my life. Little by little the neuralgic
pains that for years had nearly driven me mad abated. It really seemed too good to be true, but there
was no doubt about the complete manner in which Clements’ Tonic cured me. Not only did my head
pains ultimately die away and never occur again, but I gained in health all round. It was something
new for me to fall asleep soon after settling down in bed and not wake again till breakfast time, and
didn’t I feel the better for it! Truly, it is only fair to say that Clements’ Tonic made an
entirely new man of me, for in every way I felt different. In a few weeks I was taking food
as heartily as ever, but what I appreciate more was being able to digest all I ate. Yes; Clements’
Tonic put my digestion right and no mistake, and I often think what a wonderful medicine it must be
in view of my many previous failures with other so-called remedies. My doctors told me my liver and
nerves were out of order, so I am sure of not making a mistake when I advise my friends to take
Clements’ Tonic for those complaints, as it cured me thoroughly. After all 1 had endured it was
delightful to again feel fresh and vigorous, to forge ahead and find enjoyment in everything that I
came in contact with. So different from being weak and shaky, melancholy and irritable, like I had
been ; but I soon had every cause to be cheerful, for Clements’ Tonic had driven away each pain and
discomfort that had clouded my life so long. My system was splendidly toned and strengthened
throughout, and ever since I have enjoyed good, sound, robust health ; so you may publish these facts
in any way it pleases you.
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, SAMUEL SHAW, of 208 Lirerpool-street, Sydney, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth o: Aus-
tralia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully read the annexed document, consisting of five
folios and consecutively numbered from one to five, and that it contains, and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements’ Tonic, and also contains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements—which I give voluntarily without receiving any payment ; and I make this solemn declaration con-
scientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900.
(Sgd.) SAMUEL SHAW.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney this fourth day of November, one thousand nine hundred and four, before me,
WALTER J. GROGAN. J.P.

AN ILLUSTRATED PAR.
“I must stand on my principles!” said G. Reid at Maryborough (Q.). But it is one thing to stand on your
principles, and quite another to jump on them.

By sending direct to Tins Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have Tiik Bulletin posted
regularly to you every 'week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
llotv to Address Tetters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
•methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
■invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor 'will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 icords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. ivhen the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is fomvarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufiicient

will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which lids to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JULY 19, 1906.
A “REFORM” PARLIAMENT.
Short of a dissolution, with a disqualification of
any member of the “Reform ” Parliament (what
a fine satirical name !) standing as a candidate at
the ensuing elections, there seems to be little hope
of promptly restoring decent government to N.S.
Wales. That extreme remedy is of course out of
the question, and the almost certain inefficacy of
anything short of it adds to a deplorably
scandalous position the sting of hopelessness. At
the present time a vote of censure on the existing
N.S.W. Government, in the name of Mr.
M‘Gowen, is pending. That it will not be carried
is almost certain. The Garrutheks Government,
which draws its support from sections so widely
apart as those represented by Mr. Wade and
Mr. Crick, Mr. Norton and Mr. David Fell —
and has all those gentlemen as brothers-iu-arms
in its defence —is entrenched too strongly. If it
were to happen that a pang of conscience, a
sudden realisation of the serious responsibilities
of their duty, forced enough members from the
Carruthers side into Opposition and gave Mr.
M‘Gowen a majority, it is hard to see what
benefit would result, unless a dissolution were to
promptly follow and a chance given to the
electors to purge the House. For the evil is not
confined to the Government. Certainly the Gover-
nment represents either the most impudent gang
of political schemers that ever disgraced a legisla-
ture, or is set of blundering fools fit only for a
political idiot asylum ; and no Administration in
any Australian State has ever so fully deserved
censure and dismissal. But, the Government done
with, there seems no likelihood, indeed little pro-
bability, of asucceeding Administration being able
to restore much dignity and decenc , to the Legis-
lature. The debates of the session, and the acts
which this Parliament has tolerated, pillory it as
incurably bad. If there existed in the House any
group of honest, sensible, and courageous men
there would be some hope; if indeed there were
on the roster of members, one—just one—
individual with strength of mind, wit and
perseverance, he would long ago have been
Premier forcing the House to follow him, or he
would be now Premier-designate by the voice of
the country, the electors waiting only the oppor-
tunity to acclaim him leader and give him power.
But there is not one. Some honest folk there, of
course, are. It would be extraordinary if among
90 men all should be bad. But when there is
honesty there is weakness; and members group
themselves into two divisions, the stupid and the
scandalous.
* * * *
If the Labor party, which forms the Opposition,
had any clear claim to public confidence, Mr.
Carruthers would have been out of power a
year ago. If in the Carruthers following—which
includes nearly a score of men who openly profess
to be ashamed of him as leader, and to be kept from
seceding to the Opposition only by the fact that
the Opposition is “just as bad”—there were one
or two members of real character and ability, they
would long ago have ousted him from office. But
the mournful fact is that Mr. Carruthers is
easily the ablest man in the House, and also is the
man whose character and temperament most
nearly represent those of the majority of members.
He has no dignity ; nor has the House. He lacks
courage; that fits the Assembly. He has no
definite principles; that provides no point of
difference. His ideal is to drift along, sneaking
out of this difficulty, scraping through this
escape hole, content with contemptuous tolera-
tion when he cannot command support and
respect; that too is the ideal of the House
which he leads. It would indeed be some-
thing of an offence against the fitness of
things, conveying an impression of inartistic
incongruity, if anyone but Mr. Carruthers led
the N.S.W. Parliament. Mr. Wade is suggested
by some as an alternative, Mr. Ashton by others.
Both are putty men compared with their unre-
vered leader, mere wax figures, whose reputation
for rugged strength, gained on the football field,
or by a long series of refusals of office, comes in
handy to Joseph when he is dressing his political
shop-front. To change him for one of them would
be deposing King Joseph to enthrone in his stead
Joseph’s little finger.
No, Mr. Joseph Hector McNeil Carruthers is
the “natural leader” of this NiS. Wales Parlia-
ment, the fittest man to keep it to its muddy ruts.
When that is said, there is really nothing more to
add of condemnation. Were Mr. M‘Go wen to
succeed him, it is probable that a far more honest
attempt would be made to grapple with the
scandals which, like a fowler’s mesh, surround the
Parliament. But it is doubtful that the public
would believe so. The Labor party is indelibly
stained by the fact that, dazzled by the Owe’Sul-
livan, it consistently supported the original Lands
Scandals administration, and that in the present
Parliament it has shown itself thoroughly
insincere on the subject of financial reform,
and resolute only in Opposition when it
came to stonewalling Local Government or
Liquor reform. Further, one fact seems to
be admitted on all sides out of the murk of the
recent scandalous accusations and counter-accusa-
tions, that the Labor party—or some of its repre-
sentatives—applied to a Sydney tote-man for
financial support. The scandal of W Ren’s rela-
tion with a section of the Victorian Labor party
is an open sore which all may see. There seems
to be a canker of the same sort in N.S. Wales.
Nothing can more discredit a political party than
the suspicion that it is willing to reinforce its
funds from the coffers of men who live on the
follies of the workers, and who, being depen-
dent for their existence on the law’s toler-
ance, are naturally generous with gifts with
which they may hope to purchase political
aid in time of trouble. That suspicion
attaches itself to the N.S. Wales Labor
party now, not so much from Mr. Norton’s charge
that a tote-man was levied on, or sought to be
levied on, for the. Labor party funds, as from the
practical admission of the fact from the other side.
There is, thus, some reasonableness in the stand
of those who are unwilling to replace Mr. Car-
ruthers with a Labor Government. But there is
no reason in their refusal, on that excuse, to dis-
miss Mr. Carruthers from office, for his dismissal
could be , promptly followed by that of Mr.
M‘Go wen, and a scandalous Parliament then sent
for judgment to its masters. But a scandalous
Parliament, for the reason that it is scandalous,
has no wish to face that judgment; so Mr. Car-
ruthers remains in office.
* * * *
There is little of comfort in studying the pro-
gressive degeneration of N.S.W. Parliament
since Mr. George Dibbs was defeated at the polls.
Mr. Reid, who followed him, only gained the
office by a “palace conspiracy,” stepping into
power over the murdered body of Heny Parkes ;
and only kept office by the shabby tricks of the
demagogue. Mr. Lyne, who followed, with Mr.
Owe’Sullivan as his chief colleague, did nothing
to better things, so far as Parliament itself was
concerned. True, his record of useful legis-
lation was good, but the degeneration of
the Assembly as a deliberative body con-
tinued, and with it the policy of keep-
ing power by buying support. Mr. John See,
who followed, won his electoral warrant largely by
the exposure of the scandalous relations of the
Lyne Government with the Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, relations in which he, as a colleague of
Mr. Lyne, had necessarily been a participant.
The See Government, which was really a Ciuck-
Owe’Sullivan Government, was followed by a
Waddell Administration, which only went to
the country to be massacred. At that general
election a strenuous effort was made to
give a real issue to the contest and on a
platform of financial honesty to rally a party
of honesty and good government. The Labor
party refused to stand on that platform, as
did the two other parties (the majority of
those few men who professed a sympathy
with the honesty issue, subsequently proved
to be insincere): and the attempt failed.
The election was fought mainly on the
sectarian issue: and it quite fitly resulted
in the return of this “Reform” Parliament,
marking about the lowest level to which the
N. S. W. legislature has as yet reached, and
having for its natural, fit and proper leader Mr.
Joseph Carruthers. A new election might
mend matters. So far as the range of human
speculation can go, it could not make them
worse. But Parliament, as it stands, is hopeless.
No mere change of Government would be of any
material use.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Reid Goes to Queensland.
No one expects much from George Reid in the
way of political propriety. But the people of
Australia are entitled to expect, even from him, a
moderate amount of decency and honesty. They
are not getting it just now. His action ingoing
on a political tour to Queensland, with his absurd
anti-Socialistic show, in the middle of an impor-
tant session, is nothing short of an outrage. To
put the thing on the lowest ground, he is paid
£4OO a year to attend to his Parliamentary duties,
He doesn’t earn his money by talking rubbish
about Socialism in remote parts of Queensland,
It may be quite true that, if he were in his place
in the House, he would only be talking rubbish
about the Anti-Trust Bill. But that doesn’t
alter the complexion of his present performance,
which involves deliberate and wilful contempt of
duties which he is paid to attend to. Ever since
he has been in the House, Reid has consistently
placed his private interests before his public
duty. Except when he was in office, the House
never saw him when he had a ghost of a chance
of earning a fee by staying away. There may have
been some colorable excuse for this, in that a man
must support his family, and while the Parliamen-
tary allowance is so grossly inadequate, it is very
difficult for a member of Parliament to give proper
attention to both sets of duties. But whether the
excuse is good or bad, as applied to Reid’s absence
from the House for professional reasons, it does
not help him in his present performance. But
that is not the worst aspect of Reid’s action. Even
if he has not enough honesty to recognise that he
ought to do what he is paid to do, he might, as a
man who has been for a long time in public life,
and has held the highest offices in the people’s gift,
be expected to understand this rudimentary truth
—that the electors put a man into Parliament, not
for fun, but to get from him the best he can con-
tribute to the national policy. A Bill, according to
Reid and his noisy following, is before the House
which is going to sound the death-knell of com-
merce in Australia. If one-half of what Joseph
Cook and Co. are saying of the Anti-Trust Bill is
true, it is the most extravagantly wicked and
foolish measure that was ever introduced into a
civilised Legislature. And yet Reid’s con-
ception of his public duty allows him, at a
time when the whole commercial interests of Aus-
tralia are in deadly peril (according to him),
to conduct a useless political campaign at the
remote ends of the Commonwealth. Under the
circumstances, it is not surprising that the Syd-
ney Telegraph should find it necessary to apolo-
gise for its political hero. But Reid is peculiarly
unfortunate in his apologist. “ If he satisfies his
own party by his movements,” says this apostle
of lofty politics, “ that is quite enough for his
look-out.” Under the absurd party system, as
everyone knows, the interests of the country are
too often sacrificed to the interests of party. But
one doesn’t often find such a frank avowal of the
poisonous theory that a man is sent into Parlia-
ment solely to serve the interests of his own
party.
Audit in W.A.
Finance in Westralia is still a very casual busi-
ness. When the new Auditor-General was
appointed, two or three years back, he mildly
suggested (he had come out of a mercantile house)
that it wouldu’t be a bad idea if the business
departments of the State knew whether they
were paying or not. One or two of them said
that, now they had come to think of it, it might
be as well; but most of them merely sniffed.
Auditor-General Toppin repeated the mild sug-
gestion later on, and said something about busi-
nesses being run on business lines ; and the sort
or reply he got can be reckoned up from this
quotation (Auditor-General’s Report, 1905, just
published):—
A diversity of opinion exists as to accounts to be kept.
Where the nature of the business should require the suc-
cess or otherwise of the undertaking- to be disclosed, in
some instances endeavors are made to show the position ;
in others, it is contended that the Government system does
not require such information.
So the lordly public servant sits on his stool, with
the taxpayer to fall back upon, and with Cohen
behind the taxpayer, and asks what it matters
whether the blessed thing pays or not ! Of
course it would soon be made to matter if Cohen
shut off supplies, and the taxpayer had to part
up every cent that was spent or frittered away ;
but while the pawnshop is open, an awful waste
can go on, and the taxpayer never notices it.
But his children, to whom the bill is being passed
on, will notice it. Meantime the Labor party,
which is the party of State enterprise, the party
whose programme and prospects will suffer most
when it is discovered that State enterprises are
conducted in a sloveniy way—this party winks
at even the most prodigal borrowing. And so
its members are not only preparing a rod to
whip their own children, but they are building a
cross to crucify their own cause.

Jam Follows the Beef.
Those people who object to Pure Food legislation
on the ground that it is an outrageous interference
with Private Enterprise—and who in N.S. Wales
reinforce that bad argument with the worse one
that it is an outrageous interference with State
rights—chose an unhappy time to call with a pro-
test on N.S.W. Premier Carruthers. For it was
the day the English Jam was found out. Thought
of the iniquity of the American Beef had partly
faded from the public mind, when the cables
reported the “filthy condition” of British jam
factories, and a fresh wave of nausea put
the community in a mood to stomach any
degree of State interference rather than allow
Private Enterprise the freedom of its interior.
The case for allowing the dairy, the butter factory,
the abattoirs, the jam factory, and the bakery
the full “liberty of the subject” is hopeless, and
Private Enterprise may as well succumb to that
fact. The public is willing to tolerate a good deal
of interference with other people’s actions to
ensure the purity of its food supply; and State
Socialism will surely extend its domain to the
supply of all the alimentary necessaries of life
within a very short time. State or municipal
butcheries, bakeries and dairies already exist in
many parts of the world. Very shortly
they will exist in all civilised countries.
Meanwhile the food industries, while remaining in
private hands, must submit to an increasing vigil-
ance of State inspection. For it is clear that only
by State interference can decent conditions be
enforced. Modern industrialism and modern
means of transit and preservation have effected
startling changes in regard to food supply. Of
old—before the days of big cities, of cold storage
and of tinned goods—the supervision of food
supplies could be close and effectual since it was
purely local, and no village had to put up with
more dirt in its milk, bread and meat than it
actually eared for. (Sometimes it cared for a good
deal, but that does not effect the argument.) Now
food is put up in all parts of the world for other parts
to devour. Those who prepare have no kin with
those who will eat. In many cases, they are the
servants of grasping joint-stock companies which
have no gospel but that of dividends. There is
thus no neighborly feeling, and no consideration
of conscience to protect the consumer against
adulteration. And since adulteration is so vastly
profitable, some power must step in to impose a
check. That power can only be the State,
acting as trustee for the consumer. Within a
very short time no civilised country will allow
to be consumed within its boundaries any
food products which its inspectors have not
scrutinised, or which are not guaranteed by the
reliable State inspection of the exporting country.
This close State supervision will inevitably tend
to State ownership of all food preparation indus-
tries. But that will be Private Enterprise’s
proper punishment. As a food purveyor Private
Enterprise has proved such an unspeakable hog
as to deserve almost any kind of penalty.
The High Court.
It is proposed by the Australian Government to
add two Judges to the High Court, and thus
raise it to the strength originally proposed and
always necessary. But there is talk of opposition
and an effort to come to a “compromise decision ”
of a Bench of four. Such penny-wise-and-pound-
foolish economy is deplorable in a National
legislature. Were it a fact that three Judges
could, by strenuous industry, just manage to do
all the work of the High Court, it would be still
advisable to have a stronger Bench, partly because
a Judge can’t as safely be kept at high pressure of
work as a clerk or a laborer, for if he
yields even a little to the pressure, and slips into
carelessness, the results are liable to be serious :
partly because in the number of councillors
there is wisdom, and a three-judge Bench, in
which two can carry the day, is liable to become
a one-man Bench, with the strongest man, carrying
the weakest man with him, laying down the law.
But it is quite clear that three Judges cannot
carry through the present work—arbitration and
original jurisdiction cases have to await a
strengthening of the Bench ; and fairly clear that
even four Judges would not be sufficient in view of
the growing stress of work. The Bulletin believes
that in matters of State, as in matters of private
business, a scrupulous and scrutinising economy is
advisable. But there is no real economy in try-
ing to save a few pounds at the price of injuri-
ously affecting the most important work of the
Commonwealth. For the same reason the Legis-
lature should take the occasion of the necessary
amendment of the Judiciary Act to provide for
judicial pensions. A Judge’s work is too serious
to allow it to be ever clouded by senility, and the
inevitable result of leaving the judicial positions
without pensions will, in time, be senile Judges
clinging optimistically to the faith that they are
still earning their salaries as well as drawing
them.

N.S.W. Local Government.
When the N.S.W. Shires Bill was before the
Legislature it was announced as a Local Govern-
ment Bill to provide for local works from focal
revenues. “Let the men who vote the money for
public works find it. Do away with the Roads-
and-Bridges member. Abolish the expensive
Works Department. If the country member
wants a bridge against somebody’s door, let his
constituents find the money.” Purity of politics
was to result from this Local Government, and the
cash saving was to be immense. The Government
in return for those blessings was to part with the
land-tax. So the Shire Councils were brought
into existence. They are doing nicely, and adver-
tising for clerks at wages of from 255. to £3 a
week. And this appears in the press :
Messrs. Briner and M‘Farlane, Ms.L.A., interviewed
the Minister for Works with reference to the need of con-
structing a bridge over the Coldstream River, a branch of
the Clarence River, near Ulmarra. The Minister said
that he had reduced the estimate for the work from £7500
to £3BOO. This sum would be placed on the Estimates.
If the work were carried out, it would he by the Govern-
ment, and not by the Shire Council.
Note : This sum will be placed on the Estimates.
The Government will pay for it, not the Shire
Council. N.S.W. thus has Local Government
plus, not minus, the Roads-and-Bridges member.
Was anything else to be expected from Car-
ruthers, anyhow ?

The History.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
Our youth grows short and the time draws near
When a firm pen we must take,
To fill in the outlines, bold and clear,
Of the history we’ve to make
The task has never been faced before,
But the task’s not one to shirk) —
No history long of words and war,
But a history wide of work.
We’ll nurse the presents that Nature gave
With care in the arms of toil,
We’ve mother and friend and faithful slave
In one, in the smiling soil.
Our land is ever the best ally
And its force to the world will give
No tale that is wrought of men that die,
But a tale that men may live.
We’ve never harked to the tocsin’s note
Or the grim great drama played,
When the brother strives for the brother’s throat
At the breast of the barricade.
Work served instead of the rolling drum,
A wage for the victor’s spoil,
And we’ll gain our greatness in days to come
As we gained our rights—by toil.
We girded our loins but once to strike,
And then in the cause of shame;
But next we’ll strike on the side of right
When the spears of Asia flame.
No feud of a sordid squabbling clan
Is the fight that to-morrow knows,
And our flag will fly in the wide white van
When the ranks of J aphet close.
Our history lies not yet behind,
No pangs of the past are writ
On the yellowed scroll, or the warrant signed
That the flames of Hate be lit.
Though lands that jealousy parts and cleaves,
Our greatness only gauge
By a meagre volume of empty leaves
With a blot on the title page,
Our greatness is where the gold reefs are,
Where the foundry hammers swing ;
In the groaning dray, and the creaking spar,
Are our land’s awakening.
In the axe that gleams in the new-cut track,
In the comb that the shearer plies,
In the hearts that conquer the bush Out-Back,
Australia’s history lies.

disorganise or crush Australian industries and
throw* Australian workers out of employment,
then inquiry may be made by a Justice of the
Court, and if unfairness is proved, the
dumped goods may be prohibited, and an
injunction obtained to restrain their further
importation. Thus the wire nails that are
sold to compete at a lower cost than that at
which the iron and steel companies will supply
Australian factories with the raw material; the
salt that came out in the ship Bessfield to be sold
at a price that was less than even the ordinary
rates of freight; the machines whose wholesale
admission means the death of an Australian in-
dustry : all these are liable to be held up until a
Judge is satisfied that they are being honestly
sold by fair traders, whose object is to carry on
their business at a profit, and not to throttle
Australian factories in order to make Aus-
tralia an empty field for foreign exploitation.
To this measure the large, heavy Dugald Thom-
son moved an amendment, suggesting that
the penalties of the Bill should apply to the
goods of every country in the world except
Britain, but that “imported goods” should be
held “ not to include goods imported from, and
the product of, the United Kingdom.” Dugald
divided his speech into two parts, in the first of
which he said that no strangling, throttling com-
petition came to Australia from Britain; and
secondly, that Britain should be allowed to choke
our baby industries if she wanted to, “ because She
was Our Mother.” Attorney-General Isaacs
nailed Dugald down on this dilemma. If British
competition was conducted honestly and fairly,
then the Bill made no attack upon it ; if dis-
honestly and unfairly, then Isaacs saw little dif-
ference (so far as the baby industries were con-
cerned) between strangulation by a mother’s ten-
der hands and the callous fingers ot a stranger
from America, Germany or Japan. The baby
was just as dead in either case. But Dugald
still sobbed phrases of devotion to the Smother-
land, and declared that no man loyal to the
Empire could oppose his amendment. Yet, in
fact, every determination that Australia makes to
keep up a high standard of life and wages within
her shores tends to strengthen the Empire. The
Empire contains, in addition to some 300,000,000
black and brown subjects, who must inevitably
revolt some day against the yoke of a few
whites, nearly 00,000,000 of white people in
the British Isles, Canada, Australia, and South
Africa. Those in the British Isles (by far the
greater number) are becoming less and less fitted,
through over-crowding, under-feeding, and under-
paying, to tight for the Empire when her day of
trouble comes. “The colonies” have an oppor-
tunity, by maintaining the high standards they
have begun, of creating a big,_ virile, healthy
population that it will be difficult to attack.
Persons of the Dugald type resent any departure
from the wages and the living standards of
Britain, where 12,200,000 of the people tremble
on the verge of starvation. They demand, in
effect, that the highest level of the British race
shall be the lowest level that it drops to in the
factories of England. It is, examined carefully,
a bitterly unpatriotic and anti-imperial demand.
“ Up Against the Labor Party.”
The sin of successive "V ictorian Governments
in permitting Wren to flout the Law with
his Collingwood Tote was made glaringly ap-
parent at Port Melbourne last week, when
Judkins, the anti-gambling crusader, attempted
to address an unsympathetic crowd of working
men. Circumstances have enabled the Judkins
party of Rechabites and snufflebusters to take up
a position which should have been filled by bigger
and broader-minded citizens, but, in the absence
of more weighty champions, Judkins and co. de-
serve consideration. The fact that they don’t get
it is a trifling matter compared with the fact that
Government inaction has permitted the “cause’
of Judkins to take the form of a political party
question. A deplorable state of things is revealed
in the scornful remarks hurled at Judkins by his
thoughtless antagonists. “You are up against
the Labor party this time, old boy,” shouted one of
the opposition at the Port Melbourne meeting, and
the other interrupters expressed the same
sentiment. To denounce the gambling evil as a
canker which, owing to the lawless enterprise of
AVren, is eating into the heart of the working
classes in Melbourne is to be “ up against
the Labor party.” Struggling workers talk
nowadays of one gambling law for the rich and
another for the poor, as they never talked in the
time when the betting law was more strictly
enforced, and Wren had not arrived at omni-
potence. In short, Wren is being misrepresented
as a brave upholder of the working man’s right
to lose his money at a shilling tote or a shilling
betting club in the same recognised way as
the non-laboring man loses his money under
more “ classy ” conditions. And this undoubted
new danger to the community is merely the
result of the Government’s neglect to nip Wren
in the bud. Anti-gambling laws in all countries
must ever be in the nature of class legislation, un-
just according to logic, but quite justifiable by
the rules of expediency. The men who prey
upon the gambling instinct of needy toiling fools
are a much greater evil thau such as prey upon
the same instinct in rich fools. No reasoning
person can deny that a bricklayer chronically im-
poverished by Wren’s tote, or any kindred faci-
lity for “doing in” his wages, is a more pitiable
spectacle than the plunging heir to a rich estate
who reduces himself by gambling to the status of
a “ broken-down ” person, living on the last rem-
nant of his fortune, or on a comfortable pittance
granted by his wealthy relatives. It’s a poor
look-out for the Victorian Labor party if it is to
commit itself to a policy of free gambling facilities
for the poor.

The “Good Man” Candidate.
To those old ladies, of both sexes, who are pre-
paring to raise for the next Federal elections a
“Good Man” cry The Bulletin would like to
give a note of friendly warning. It is not a re-*
petition of the old familiar argument that the
good man is not necessarily the good politician ;
that a thorough training in the Seven Deadly Dull
Virtues argues no skill in economics, and none of
that strength of will and mind necessary to drive
reforms through Parliament ; that experience
shows that the man who is willing to be labelled
“ Good” is very often, like the woman who boasts
of her virtue, a sepulchre with a lie on the
tombstone, and something unpleasant and
obscene beneath. (When Walter Besant wrote
his story entitled “Such a Good Man,” lie
drove those facts home in a way that caused
the whole tribe of Good Men to hate his
very name.) All this has been argued before,
and not understood by the old ladies, of either
sex. It might be argued, again and again,
with just as little effect. The fault is not with
the argument. It is with the old ladies’ mental
spectacles, which are adjusted only for the nar-
rowest of vision. “I can give my friend the
reasons,” thundered Gladstone, on one of the
few occasions when lie eundescended to say what
he meant in a sentence under two columns in
length, “but only the Almighty can give him the
comprehension to understand them.” On the mam
issue of the Good Man cry the old ladies, of both
sexes, must be left to the Almighty, but
there is a side issue which they may be able
to understand. Whilst it is broadly true that
the “goodness” of a man—taking goodness in the
sense as understood by old ladies who form
Societies for the Prevention of Premature Bunal
and for the Discouragement of Early Marriages
among the Heathen Poor—has nothing to do with
his chances of political usefulness, it is also true
that some forms of wickedness argue strongly that
a candidate is unfitted for public life, if a man
is dishonest in his private affairs ; if he shows a
cruel and brutal disposition in his family lite ; 1
he makes of his drunkenness or his salaciousness
a matter of pride and boast—it seems probable
that in public life he will be more apt to peculate,
more likely to be a bad ruler generally ; and, any-
how, even if his public acts were unobjectionable,
there is a reasonable sentimental bias against, say
the Hon. the Premier, being taken out of the gutter
drunk because he hasn’t enough decency to get
drunk in private. But the effect of a Hooa
Man ” cry is just to put men of that sort into
public life. When a catmittee of old ladies of
both sexes begins to poke a prying nose into the
private lives of candidates, and take account ot
the sins which are not in any way public pro-
perty, the sense of disgust which fills the public
mind gives a chance to the other sort of candi-
date, who flaunts his badness as an ontiamme,
and is not a “Good Man,” or an ordinary man,
but a boastfully bad man. There have entered
Australian public life at different times several
of this type, and on the whole they have
hardly adorned politics. Even when they have
done nothing publicly scandalous to match their
private objectionableness, they have usually
assisted to waste much time by posturing m
the lurid limelight as villains, ha-ha, of the
deepest dye, and have covered many irrelevant
pages of Hansakd with discussion on their
drinks, their debaucheries and their devilment
generally. To the “Good Man” cry Australia
owes their presence. They represent the natural
swing back of the pendulum. The electors, dis-
gusted at being asked to apply the gimlet-hole
test to Plunk in the interests of Goofool, are
moved to rush to the support of Blade, who
wears his wickedness as an aureole, and whose
life couldn’t get sufficient investigation from a
dozen shipwright’s augers, let alone a gimlet, lo
the old ladies, of both sexes, who worry about
“Good” candidates then, The Bulletin begs to
suggest, “drop it.” The effort to keep out a
healthy Bread, Beef and Beer man in the
interests of a Bun and Cold Tea candidate only
helps the cause of the Chicago Sausage type ot
anthropoid animal.
