Patents.
“ Wimmera ” on patents :
Six thousand inventors have applied for patents since
the Common wealth office opened about two years ago.
How many of these will be heard of m a few years time .
And, in spite of fair promises, the Commonwealth office
is not doing all it might to help the inventor. Under'the
Australian Patent Act a search must be made to see it
the invention is new. For a time this was not done, the
excuse being that “ N.S. Wales was holding on to its
State records,” although that needn t have prevented the
office from making search in the excellently classified
and indexed records of the English which are
kept in the Melbourne office. It is for that work that
the high fee of £8 is paid, and if the office, has not a
sufficient staff then it is time it had. Otherwise the in-
ventor can be exploited by all kinds of agents who will
procure him a patent regardless of whether it has any value
or not. In the United States, Germany, Austria and
even in old-fashioned Britain, a search is now made in
order to give the inventor warning if his invention is no
good and thus prevent him from spending his siller on a
dream that he is an Edison. If anyone will examine the
British Illustrated Official Journal issued by the ratent
Office, and will note the number of worthless ideas which
are “protected” by patent, he will see the need for an
examination of this kind. You can t rely upon private
enterprise” to do it. “ Private enterprise vvan , ts ., th .®
job of taking out a patent in foreign countries, and it is
only too ready to flatter the natural conceit of the mex-
stoutly held out against the preliminary ex-
amination for a long time. Britain was individua'ist. It
said : “ Let the inventor take out his patent. Even if it
is invalid it is his own risk. It won’t hurt Us (i.e.,
officialdom). But when it was seen that patents fie-
uuentlv failed when tested in the law courts, and when
the better-informed English inventors began the practice
of taking out a German patent first in order to see if the
idea were new, and if that part which was new
were any use, people began to say things. Now a
search is made, and both the inventor and the public
benefit * the inventor, for the reasons already stated,
and the public, by the elimination of a large number of
confidence-trick patents, which had no validity, and weie
not worth the paper they were written on, except for the
illegitimate purpose of extorting backsheesh out of manu-
facturers who feared to contest the matter in a court of
law Under the new system the novel elements in a
patent must be set forth in a simple manner, and if the
whole idea is evidently old, the office either lefuses to
grant the patent or insists that it shall be endorsed with
the number of the previous patent. That is all right as fai
as it a-oes. Of course, it does not guarantee validity abso-
lutely for a search is only made of the previous British
patents. But even so much is good. In Germany they
search English, German, Austrian, American and French
patents. Sometimes they search periodicalsand text books.
In America a search is made in the American and English
records, and the patent officer can go outside them if he
deems it advisable. The Australian Patent Office should
at least search the Australian and British patents, as
publication in either of those would certainly invalidate
the patent, and it is not desirable that the inventor
should get a worthless document. The office should be
the guardian of the inventor and the public alike. If
invention is worth encouraging at all, let us give the
inventor full information, and save him from loss and
disappointed vanity later on. Of course, the inventor of
the infatuated type will still exist, hut sane men at least
will be saved from their ignorance of the patent laws, and
will no longer spend large sums in pushing valueless
patents in foreign countries.

Canada.
“ Austra ” writes from London :
The most prominent thing here in the North of
England (after beer and betting) is Canada. Returned to
this part the other day, and on going to purchase my
daily packet of rags was asked, “ Would you like one of
these’” It was anillustrated Canadian pamphlet. Iti
a public bar I found a string of pipe-lights—it was a
shipping co.’s sailing list—steamers for Canada. W ent
to a sale at a farm ; the auctioneer’s office-boy was dealing
out Canadian literature. At each railway station aie
maps and pictures of Canada. In each town of any size
at all are agents who regularly receive and are responsible
for the distribution of posters, pamphlets, facts, lies, etc.
These men are usually in the agency business, with other
lines, and in other cases the hill-posting cos. act. By
this means the Canadian boom is worked cheaply and
effectively. Probably there are travelling inspectors to
see that all do their work properly. But it is the half-
neii n v papers that really do most for Canada. That they
are inspired is very obvious. Advertisements appear,
tellin ur each reader that he is the particular person
required, and that there is just room to squeeze
on to the next boat, while the news columns
daily have something to say. Every little local preacher,
school teacher, or loafer, has his projected departure for
Canada chronicled. Each day’s sailings or arrivals are
wired. Allusions to Canada are made in the persona.l,
local, financial, and sporting columns. Even a few words
against the country are allowed to appear ; this gives an
opening for half-a-dozen letters next day, all of which
denounce the dissatisfied one as a waster and a squib.
So unanimous a;c the letters that they appear to he in-
spired by one person. Even sketches and column-length
tales extolling life in Canada are published, whether as
such or at advertising rates writer knows not, but he has
suspicions. A weekly paper, Canada, published in Lon-
don, beautifullv illustrated, finely got up, and nearly the
size of The Bulletin, does not seem to be on sale any-
where ; it is quietly and regularly dropped into public
reading rooms, hotel smoke rooms, and railway station
waiting apartments. The one thing all this information
does not tell you is that after you get 160 acres, more or
less free, what more land you want has to be got through
land or railway companies. That information would not
assist the object in view, as the people of this country
know even better than Australians what monopoly of
land by railway companies or private individuals
means, and would not care to go from it to more of it.
At times it is difficult to get pamphlets from the agents,
they are rushed out to such an extent; while apparently
the only places in the United Kingdom where similar
literature of the Australian States can be got are the
Agents-Generals’ offices in London. They are almost
packed with what ought to be in the public reading
rooms, etc. ; so much so that the writer has had to fairly
burrow his way in to get a glimpse of Australian papers.

The European mail contract, which the Deakin
Government has arranged, represents an excellent
bargain. The Commonwealth obtains a much
improved service as regards speed, regularity, and
cargo-space, at a sum practically equal to that
paid now ; and the new boats will fly the Com-
monwealth flag, and will be in most respects Aus-
tralian. Apart from all other considerations, the
fact of a great fleet of first-class steamers travelling
the high seas as “ the Australian line” will be of
notable value as an advertisement in Europe of
the potentialities of this continent. The new
agreement, too, brings nearer the possibility of
Australian naval defence. Presumably it will be
possible to arrange that the Australian mail
line will train Australian seamen as a naval re-
serve, and will provide some ships convertible
into cruisers in the case of war.
Senator Staniforth Smith, at the Prime Minis-
ter’s request, has furnished a very ample and
valuable report on his trip to the Malay States
and Java. It is of vital interest to Australians
from different standpoints, bearing in mind also
Germany’s resolve to possess the Dutch colonies.
Senator Smith gives a comprehensive survey of
the plantation industries suitable for New Guinea.
The Malay Peninsula has 880,900 population, of
whom 400,000 are Chinese. Tin mining employs
half of them. The South African system of
getting Chinese labor was entirely borrowed
from the Malayan. Java is only one-four-
teenth of the Dutch possessions in these waters,
but its annual revenue is £7,790,000, out of
£10,196,006 for all Netherlands India, on the
whole of which there is usually an annual
deficit; but Java yields a considerable surplus.
One thing that rouses the Germans is the,
enormous plunder that Java yields to officials.
The Governor-General gets £ll,OOO a year; Vice-
President, £3000; Lieut.-General, £2680; Vice-
Admiral, £2500; four councillors, each £2400;
secretary, £2OOO ; six directors, each £2OOO. The
big pay goes down to a host of others, and it is
palpable that the place could be worked for a
fraction of the money. Two heads of the
Mahomedan faith draw from the State respectively
the huge sums of £73,750 and £39,387 per year.
A hint for land reformers is that all the land in
Netherlands India, except a small area of Java,
is owned by the State.

Sugar growing, as practised in Queensland, is
still a most wasteful business ; if other Australian
industries threw as much on to the rubbish-tip,
and were bled as much by middlemen, very few of
them would pull through without crying out for a
cheap Kanaka. Last year, as far as the statistics
go—and they don’t go all the way—there were
5,904,518 gallons of molasses available; that is,
just upon 100,000,0001 b. Of that total about
600.000 gallons were used to feed stock ; a very
little of it was turned in as manure ; of the rest,
3,000,000 gallons, in addition to all that was not
recorded, went to absolute waste. Now, molasses,
especially when mixed with ground bagass
(another sugar-mill by-product), makes fine stock
fodder; yet this prodigious waste occurred in a
country whose stock dies by the million for want
of stored fodder. The answer always thrown in,
when this and similar waste is mentioned, is that
the thing won’t pay ; and it looks as though, so
long as the industry had T. T ANNA to lean upon,
it never would be made to pay. _ It is simply one
more instance of cheap labor being deadly to any
brains that happen to be in its neighborhood.
Some polite correspondence has been passing
between the Westralian Auditor-General and
the Bailway Commissioner of the great sandy
State. The Audit Department asked for par-
ticulars of certain items, which particulars the
Commissioner declined to supply. Whereupon
the A.-G. suggested that if the required infor-
mation were not promptly forthcoming the items
would be disallowed. Then the Railway Cominis-
sioner got riled, and used strong language. The
A.-G. was formerly accountant in the Railway
Department, and knows exactly what goes on
there. The Commissioner squirmed at the thought
of being compelled to knuckle down to a man who
had only recently been one of his subordinates.
So he rose and vigorously cursed the A.-G. and
all his works, winding up a letter to the head of
the Government in these terms
My opinion of the whole trouble is that my staff are
being unduly harassed and interfered with, solely because
in my righteous indignation I told Mr. Toppin a few
straight truths, and if he had carried on while with me
as he has done since, I should have sacked him—and he
knows it.
Nothing daunted, the A.-G. carried out his threat
and disallowed the items which embroiled the
Commissioner with the Treasury Department,
and things are becoming lively all round. The
Commissioner, commonly known as Bully
George, has now sent in the following ultimatum
to the head of the Treasury Department:—
This harassing action of the Auditor-General will have
to end. I have no time, nor have my officers, to bother
about such tomfoolery.
There now !
Proceedings in the Federal and State Parlia-
ments show that in proportion to the diminution
of alcoholic business in the refreshment-rooms the
catering business grows less remunerative. Most
Australian Parliaments report regular deficits on
the refreshment-room, and the tendency all round
is financially down-hill. This condition of
things provides a fair opportunity for testing
the sincerity of many solid-looking Tories.
A motion to abolish the Parliamentary refresh-
ment rooms, and let members dine at the neigh-
boring hotels or boarding-houses, should elicit the
warm approval of men who talk loudly about the
beauty and loveliness of private enterprise. The
hotelkeeper, boarding-house-keeper and coffee-
stall-holder represent private enterprise, and
should be supported by Toryism on a cash basis —
its own cash and its own basis. If this motion
were carried, provision could be made for
members who took in their meals on their
persons; an apartment could be set apart as
an eating room for the men who carried a
bundle of sandwiches wrapped in newspaper
and a soda-water bottle of _ cold tea. 1 hen
those members who laugh derisively when mention
is made of serving free meals to hungry school
children could also laugh at the idea of supplying
semi-free meals to members of Parliament men
who now eat twice as much as they pay for and
make the country liquidate the balance. It s a bit
of Socialism to" which The Bulletin doesn t
object; the poorly-paid politician deserves these
little perks. But it’s also a kind of Socialism
that deserves the bitter hostility of Anti-Sosh.

A case illustrating the maze that has to be
travelled through when dealing with the N. S.W.
Land laws has been decided by the Land Appeal
Court. A public reserve in the Braidwood dis-
trict became available for selection, and in the
rush for the land three applications were lodged
simultaneously for the same block. The Land
Board tossed up for it—that is, sent the appli-
cants to the ballot. The unsuccessful gamblers
went to the Land Appeal Court, which argued this
way : Under Act 1884, section 27, the Land
Board was right in sending them to the ballot.
But section 27 of the 1884 Act was repealed by
section 11 of the 1889 Act. This section was then
set aside by section 6of the 1899 Act. But the
case could not be settled by section 6, 1899, be-
cause section 20 of the 1903 Act repealed it.
Further, section 5 of the 1905 Act settled the
hash of section 20, 1903, and the Court would
have decided the matter on section 5, 1905, but
for the fact that the section had apparently by
accident forgotten to allow for such a case as the
Braidwood one. The Court nearly lost itself
by this time, but it managed to decide that the
local Land Board was wrong to settle the thing
by tossing up, though it did not say what should
have been done instead of the toss-up.

If a certain undesirable alien named Ll Wan
Quai is finding the money himself to tight his
way into the Commonwealth —which he probably
isn’t—he will have made a large hole in his
savings by the time lie’s finished. Li originally
reached Victoria in the Changsha in October,
’O4. When the crew was lined up prior to de-
parture Li was missing, and lie remained so for
12 months, when the police laid bands on him.
P:M. Panton sentenced him to 14 days and de-
portation, On appeal, Judge Eagleson quashed
this conviction, which was for entering the Com-
monwealth as a prohibited immigrant. In
January, ’0(1, the State happened on Li once
more and charged him with “ being a prohibited
immigrant found within the Commonwealth.
Panton once more convicted Li, and ordered him
to be gaoled for seven days, and then deported.
Another appeal, to Judge Molesworth, resulted
in the conviction being sustained. Then Li—and
his backers —went to the High Court, and last
week that tribunal decided that Panton and
Molesworth were right, and that Li should be
sent back to the Yellow land as quickly as might
be. These things total up two trials and three
appeals, with expensive lawyers all the way
through ; so evidently a Chow sailor and market-
gaidener gets higher wages—anu more prompt
payment—than, say, the white mariners of the
Melville Island.

Some of the wholesale houses in Sydney have been
prosecuted for selling “ egg-powders, ” which are
advertised as a “reliable substitute for eggs.
The defence put up was that the purchasing
public is perfectly aware that it is not buying
either powdered egg, or anything in the nature of
an egg. The magistrate before whom the cases
were heard convicted the defendants, but punish-
ment is deferred pending some sort of settlement
with what is vaguely referred to as the “autho-
rities.” The defence seems a pretty large and
cheerful assumption. How can anyone say that
all “the purchasing public,” which is a very large
section of the community, knows that the term
“egg-powder” has no relation, however remote,
to eggs’ Wasn’t the name carefully devised to
prevent the public knowing that ? And if it had
but known that the only resemblance to egg lay
in the coloring—which seems to be the case—vvould
it have spent its money on egg-powders? It
hardly seems likely.

“P.”: If there is in Australia a man for whom
the muddy tear of pity should be allowed to drop,
that man is Cook, deputy-leader of the Opposition
in the Reps. He is a galley slave and a working
bullock. The sharp, fierce, querulous little weasel
was yoked by G. Reid in the Opposition team
when Federal politics began, and tugged as hard
as the thickest and fattest animal of them all.
Reid at last dragged his cart out of the mud to a
greasy, uncertain Prime-Ministership. He took
with him the lamentable Pumpkin Smith and the
heavy-witted Dugald Thomson, but he left
Joseph out. The party slid back into the mire
again, and Reid, who had long since revolted
against working his brain to pieces without re-
ward, left all the “bullocking” work to Joseph.
During the whole of last session, and for as much
of this session as has already passed, the toil of
obstruction, and the ear-piercing scream of
opposition have been kept going by this
hard-worked speck of flesh and vinegar.
Nominally, Joseph is the Opposition’s deputy-
leader ; actually, he is its scullion and man
of all work. “I am under no obligation to
the importers' ; I have never received any benefit
from them,” Joseph often declares. That is pro-
bably true. The calico-man is just as ready to
sweat his political servants as to sweat his clerks
and seamstresses. And Joseph is breaking down
under the task of doing the perspiring and
straining for a lethargic and foolish Opposition.
There has been received in Australia from 0. C.
Beale, of Sydney, a copy of a report furnished by
him to Herbert Gladstone, British Home
Secretary, regarding the effects of proprietary
medicines and remedies with secret formulie.
How murderous some of these medicines are, is
shown by Mr. Beale’s quotations from F. .T.
Smith, M.D., pathologist for 15 years to the
London Hospital, and editor of “Taylor’s
Medical Jurisprudence.” Says Smith (con-
densed) :
To my certain knowledge there is debasement as
can only be called debauchery, especially among women,
resulting from the use of poisonous drugs sold under
secret formula;.
Widespread and lasting injury is done to infants and
young children by the indiscriminate use of narcotics
(manysold as “soothing-powders”). More than injury,
death is inflicted, but is invariably ascribed to some other
cause. For nurses or mothers to administer these drugs
is a wicked practice.
So-called “ headache powders,” etc., are dangerous to
the heart, and cause many fatal accidents. They should
never be administered except under strict medical super-
vision.
I am quite convinced that the amount of suffering and
the number of deaths are grossly out of proportion to
the publicly recorded cases. . . I should be pretty certain
that not above one per cent, of the mischief is recorded
or even suspected by the average layman..

‘‘ Tusi Ata ” writes :
Judging by the song of exultation that is struck up
when Japan orders a few bales of wool or half-a-dozen
raw hides, and the corresponding wail of concern when it
is rumored that Monkeyland is displeased with us, one
would imagine that the welfare of Australia depended
solely on the good graces of Mr. Yosiiiwara. Quite apart
iron, the fact that it is the manufactured article that
should be exported, all the profit Australia can ever hope
to make from trade relations with Japan is very harmless.
” a P will only buy those goods which he cannot do
without, viz., certain primary products, and he will buy
them, like everyone else, in the cheapest market.
But if Australia wants a market for some of its dairy-
ing and agricultural products, why not capture the one at
its own back door—the Malay Archipelago, with its
teeming millions of population (Java alone has over
30,000,000 inhabitants)? A considerable sum of the
£10,000,000 that is spent yearly on imports by that
colony, is accounted for by provisions, such as flour,
butter, tinned fruits, and milk, meats, and biscuits,
most of which, if a small quantity of butter exported by
a Melbourne firm is excepted, are supplied by America.
It is true that the Chinese, who are the principal retailers
in the Archipelago, as well as the Dutch themselves, are
very conservative, but when once the barrier of prejudice
is broken through, and it has been shown that the Aus-
tralian article can hold its own in open competition, a
valuable Customer will be found in the Dutch East
Indies. Much the same remark applies to manufac-
tured goods, especially of iron and leather, which
Australia should be making. Give Australia a
healthy Protective tariff, and it will soon be able
to supply not only its own requirements, but also
to get its share of the Indian island and mainland com-
merce. The field is large enough, and there is no need to
whine for a twopenny-ha’penny trade with a race which
will never be anything but a competitor for Australia’s
prospective Asiatic customers. If Australia must export,
then the Straits Settlements, Siam, Burmah, French
Indo-China, etc., should receive first consideration.
“ T.F.M.” from Melbourne :
I am making no complaints about the velocity of Tait’s
trains, for I am still haunted by visions of the risks I ran
when Private Enterprise—the Hobson’s Bay Co., to wit-
used to transport me to and from my mansion in the
suburbs. No doubt Tait is slow, but Private Enterprise
was slower and dearer—and not nearly so sure. Big divi-
dends were the chief concern of Private Enterprise.
Nothing was thought of the convenience, or even the
lives and limbs, of the passengers ; the permanent way
looked after itself mostly; the “carriages” were ugly,
dirty and decrepit; while the engines suffered terribly
from asthma, seuile decay, and numerous other ailments.
When Private Enterprise saw that the Government would
be compelled to buy the H.B. lines in order to connect
the Gippsland railway with Melbourne, all the defi-
ciencies of the plant were carefully varnished over, and
the bargain was completed, enormously to the advantage
of the co. Then the Jolimont catastrophe occurred —
just when Private Enterprise was reckoning up the pro-
fits it had made out of its successful knavery—and the
Government realised to the full how it had been rooked.
That accident, which killed several people and maimed
others, was distinctly traceable to the worn-out wheels of
a Hobson’s Bay Co.’s vehicle, and a careful inspection
showed that the whole of the rolling stock, which
Private Enterprise had declared to be in excellent condi-
tion, was so rotten as to be only fit for the scrap-heap.
An expenditure of half-a-million sterling had to be
incurred to place the lines in the condition they ivere
alleged to be in when handed over to the State at what
Private Enterprise affected to consider an extremely
moderate price. In addition to that, it cost fully £50,000,
in compensation, etc., in connection with the Jolimont
affair. No, l am not complaining about Tait myself.
Things may be badly arranged under his regime, but
“Disgusted Traveller,” “Season Ticket Holder,” and
others who are wailing about slow trains should try and
imagine how things were in Private Enterprise days.

The parable of Fitz J. (Westralia): —
Paid a neighbor of mine a visit last week. After the
usual neighborly “ Hovvdedo,” &c. :
“Expect my boy Jim home next week.”
“Oh?”
“ Yes ? Y’know he’s a bit of a‘ mechanic is Jim, an’ e’s
been over the other side on the look-out fora billet.”
“ So I heard.”
“Thing is pretty bad over there, I b’leeve. Nothin’
seems to be doin’ at all, so he’s off home.”
“That’s bad.”
“Yes, he’s in Adelaide now, lookin’ round. Y’ve ’eard
of this ’ere George Eeid, I s’pose. He’s Preemyer over
there, I think. Jim was telling me *ow he went to ’ear
’im at the Theatre or soniewheres. He’d be the sort of
cove to vote for, I reckon. Freetrader, ain’t he ? ”
“ Something like that ” I said.
“ That oughter be alright, I reckon—Freetrade for a
free country. Jim was saying as ’oiv this ’ere Esin was
telling them about Freetrade being the best thing for
Australyer.”
The old man was putting up a fence—dog-proof it was.
I picked up a hammer ; the brand was “Cheney, U.S.A.”
Then a pair of pliers, “made in Germanv.” Ditto a pair
of wire snips. The wire-netting had an-English brand,
likewise the plain wire. A brace and bin bore Mathkson’s
brand. A crowbar came from Sheffield ; an axe (which
had more than one gap in the blade) was “Kelly,
U.S.A.” A grubbing axe was plainlv branded “Iron
City,” presumably U.S. A. The shovel'might have come
from Palestine for aught I know. It was called “ Samson
No. 2.” The only item that had any Australian hand-
work attached to it was a wire-plug (actually one solid
iron wire-plug), for which the local smithy had charged a
bob. No wonder Jim, who was a bit of a mechanic, couldn’t
get a job. Oh yes, Freetrade is the thing for Australia
all right!
From the Commercial Intelligence Bureau,
London (an organisation devoted to the extension
of Imperial trade and commerce): —
\V e have read with much interest your article advo-
cating a strong naval policy for Australia, and in refer-
ence to .your view that Australian trade should be carried
on by Australia and Australian-manned ships, we beg to
point out to your readers how the achievement of that
laudable ambition is menaced by foreign enterprise, prin-
cipally German. The main line routes from Germany to
the United States, South America, the Far East and Aus-
tralia are naturally the backbone of the mercantile marine
of that power, but ramifications have been, or are being
proceeded with in many other directions by the institu-
tion of German sailings between one set of foreign ports
and another. If Australians do not take your hint and
provide themselves with a well-equipped mercantile
marine, large enough to cope with the expanding com-
merce of their country', they will soon see much valuable
shipping trade appropriated by foreigners, German ships
not only trading between Australian ports, but acting as
the chief carriers of Australian produce to Europe, thanks
to the advantage they will derive from subsidiary or local
lines. Is Australia content to allow much of her valuable
shipping trade to be captured by the Germans? That is
the danger with which she is menaced.
“U.”: That “Labor church” suggestion which
some Victorian Laborites have put forth was
bound to come. For years past various Labor
leagues have had a sprinkling of members (mostly
imported) who are reproductions in many ways
of the objectionable kind of churchgoer—the man
who always complains when anything practical is
done, and who, when asked what he would sub-
stitute, spouts mistily concerning “the deepening
of the spiritual life,” just as the “advanced”
Laborite talks about vaguely-pretty things with
the same meaning. The Socialist Stiggins (im-
ported) is always prominent and voluble. Any-
one whom he suspects of being a Laborite is liable
at any moment to be attacked with some such
stereotyped mass of words as:
“Well, brother, you, I take it,
are with us, like all good com-
rades, in the great cause of
endeavoring to bring
about the economic free-
dom of the workers a
by the institution
of a co-operative
commonwealth ? ”
“Brother” and
“comrade ” are
becoming sick- i
eningly fre- /
quent terms
of address
from persons
who believe
in “rend-
ing the
thral 1,”
“ bursting
the prison,”
“breaking
the chains,”
and so on.
Some of these
men are esti-
mable enough
in their own
way, but one V
does not want '
to be “ broth-
ered ” by them
(or by anyone
else) about five
times per minute.
Another church
parallel is the unrea-
soiling distrust of those
who “don’t go to our
church,”, as it were—men
who are undoubtedly in
sympathy with the Labor
movement, yet are not “pro-
fessin’ Christians ” or professed
Laborites to the extent of “sit-
ting under” the Rev. Tom
Mann, or some would-be auto-
crat of the P.L.C. Most of the
persons who are doing their best
to spread narrow sentiments in
Labor circles come from a country that is
decades behind this land in real political
advancement, and necessarily has largely different
ideals. Yet the blatant Reformers force on Aus-
tralia their ready-mixed political remedies almost
before they have had a look at the patient. Un-
fortunately, they find a certain limited number of
Australians to take notice of them.
“0.K.” : Bulletin par. (5/7/’O6) re the
shocking liberality with which Bent proposes-to
treat the Vic. beer industry appears to be based
on the assumption, unhappily erroneous, that the
Vic. Licensing Act doesn’t expressly provide for
compensation to the owners of de-licensed pre-
mises. The Act of 1885, slightly amended
and consolidated in 1890, was carefully de-
signed for the protection of the proputty-
owner, and provides for compensation. All
the liauor legislation in Victoria has been
directed by capitalists for capitalists, and al-
though Bent proposes (if rumor speaks truth) to
give Mr. Publichouse 10 years’ notice of the ter-
mination of compensation rights, the blessed
Legislative Council, where the majesty of Beer is
strongly represented, may demand an extension
of the notice to 100 years.

“With Flying Colors,” revived at Sydney
Royal by Bland Holt last Saturday, puts a good,
sensational story—with no very marked improba-
bilities—in an excellent setting; adorns it further
with clever and mostly earnest acting; and the
result is good. That part of the public mind that
is touched by the yellow back of a Miss Braddon
creation, or the Sherlock Holmes detective
yarn, will find in “ Flying Colors ” ex-
actly the sort of pabulum it likes. The
hero’s course of true love is interrupted
by the villain (a very villainous villain with homi-
cide on his conscience), who has him accused of
cheating at cards in a naval officers’ mess. The
hero of course is degraded, and, equally of course,
the heroine doesn’t marry the villain. But in
the course of time, through many sensational inci-
dents, the story comes to the triumph of virtue and
true love and a wedding, with a hint of a cradle in
the background. It’s a robust, happy, healthy
story, such as Bland Holt delights to tell in four
acts and nine handsome stage pictures. No room,
however, is left for comment. The usual people
are good in their usually charming and blundering
way, and the usual people are wicked, and Bland
Holt and Mrs. Holt are, as usual, comical
humorists who relieve the tension of the story
with a laugh.

Gerard Kean, the tenor about whose
discovery ” there has been so much beating of
drums, will be all the better for the cold, healthy
criticism which it is to be hoped he will get in
Sydney. So far lie lias only appeared in places
where either criticism for press purposes is un-
known or where the critic,' in the face of the
vigorous booming of the “star,” hasn’t had confi-
dence enough in his own judgment to stand out of
the chorus. At Brisbane the public did not take
the singer half as seriously as he took himself.
At his last concert—the Centennial Barn was
thronged—Kean sang tenor in the first part, and,
so the friendly papers said afterwards, soprano in
the second part of the programme. Possibly they
were natural notes he sang ; but they sounded
falsetto. Yet it is hard to believe that a teacher
who has any hope of a singer’s future would allow
him to sing one single note out of his register.
In any case, many of Kean’s notes are apparently
so forced as to be unpleasant; and the Brisbane
audience audibly punctuated some of these good-
bye songs with guffaws. Kean’s first Sydney
concert will be at the Town Hall on the 25th.

The newest eccentricity at Sydney Tivoli
is “ Vasco,” who ties himself into knots and plays
on divers instruments before he unwinds himself,
the most satisfactory of his items is a flute solo,
played while the soloist squirms on his back and
jangles bullock bells with his toes. He blows into
brass instruments also; and that needs forgive-
ness. Iwo plump people, one of whom is Bertie
hang on to nothing in particular, and get much
applause ; and Little Cliff seems likely to out-
grow his present pretty clothes before his popu-
larity wanes.

At the Palace, Spencer’s American Theatre-
scope reels off series upon series of pictures of
Frisco before the solid earth rocked under
•S - saine P lace vy hen the ’quake
and fire had left it a blackened mass of tottering
ruins. However, lest the spirit of gloom should
sit too heavily on the soul at the sight of this
sudden passing of earthly glory, there are comic
and humorous interludes in the shape of spooning
couples and other frivolities. Mankind is a
strange creature, and he steps forth from the pic-
tuie of a wrecked sky-scraper and dead bodies
the side-walks to smile cheerfully at
the spectacle of a man kissing somebody else’s
wife under a weeping willow. J
At Her Majesty’s, Sydney, William Collier
has been establishing for himself a high place
among the men who have played in Sydney in
that class of piece—the class that hovers unde-
cidedly between farce and comedy. This week-
end “On the Quiet ” gives place to “ The Dicta-
tor,” the first thing in which Collier played after
he had got through the ’Frisco shock. It’s a yarn
of a Spanish-American revolution ; and just at
the moment when Guatemala and Honduras and
San Salvador have set out to knock one another
off the country where they hang the Monroe doc-
trine, the story is right on the spot.

There was clearly a demand for the two
extra concerts given in Sydney Town Hall by
Miss Narelle and “Chanter” Fentress, for there
resulted a good supply of audience. At the first
of two concerts the amazing Dr. MacCarthy
taught the piano how to take a joke, for lie
played the accompaniment to the “ Black Bird,”
an air of bardic origin, which the programme
said was about 2000 years of age.
* -* * *
A new feature of the Bostock and Womb-
well circus is a lady wild beast tamer, who makes
the Bengal tigers lay their stripes at her feet,
and rides the Nubian lion like Una of old.
The Lukishima Japs and Japesses have some
new and equally startling performances. But
this week the circus relies upon the occult for
its main attraction. Last Thursday Professor
Bonner, a tall, youthful-looking man, hypnotised
Mr. Williams while a party of gentlemen hid
something in David Jones’s huge establishment.
When the party returned from hiding the article,
the hypnotised man fastened on to one of the party
and, although blindfolded, rushed the whole
crowd away through the city till he came to
Barrack-street, which he ran up and down
once or twice, and then, diving into the
ladies’ paradise, produced the hidden article
from the receptacle in which it had been
hidden. This week, the young man will
be buried alive in a sheet-iron coffin
and left in the cold mould for five days and nights.
A tube will give access to the tomb so that he will
not be suffocated, and through it any member of
the public can see the hypnotised man lying on
the bed in the ground. During that time he
will take neither food nor water, arid will not
leave the stretcher. Professor Bonner has an
arrangement of pulleys by which he can draw rugs
on and off the buried man as required, according
to the testimony of the thermometer, which
he will lower down the tube. It is a
curious idea of amusement, and the near-
est approach to that startling trick of the
Hindu fakirs, who, stopping the ears and nostrils
of a man with a certain kind of clay, bury his feet
deep in the naked earth, and shutting off all air
supplies, keep him there till the grass has grown
over his “grave,” When they dig him up and
restore him unharmed to his startled relatives.

The Fisk Singers announced their “last
night” Saturday last, but reconsidered their
intention, and are still playing nightly in the
Centenary Hall, Sydney. On Saturday next they
anticipate a move to the Lyceum Hall, Pitt-
street.

“The Squaw Man,” which was somewhat
rough and unready in its first night performance
at Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, has now settled
down to a very prosperous career. Looking
critically at this American matter, one would
expect the cheap sentimentalism of the mixed-
marriage situations to prove a weakness in White
Australia ; but it seems that the hero’s loyalty to
his red skin spouse, who prowls moodily around
in charge of a pail, and his devotion to his half-
breed offspring, impress Melbourne even more
favorably than the convincing cow-town character
sketches and the artistic stage pictures for which
Coleman has supplied some admirable fixings and
backgrounds. An English father, hugging his
tinted youngster to his heart whilst consenting to
send him “home” to be educated as a future
English peer, satisfies a popular yearning for
pathos. Also, when the heroic parent produces a
folded paper that has “ never left his body”
during eight long years of toil and perspiration,
and the family lawyer reads the document at a
glance, everybody in front of the curtain rejoices
to think that the hero has proved his in-nocence.
Yet a similar incident in an Andersonian drama
would be received with smiles by the average
William sonian audience. “The Squaw Man,”
although its story bristles with unrealities, asks to
be taken seriously, like a really high-class melo-
drama, and it is taken seriously. Some of the
stage pictures have a lot of persuasive eloquence.
The besetting sins of orthodox English melo-
dramas are conspicuously absent. With the
exception of a vapid johnnie character, quietly
played by Cyril Mackay, there is no “comic
relief-” agent in the.story, nor any luridly wicked
person, bar the drunken rustler, who meets his
doom with a revolver in either hand.
■**■*■*
Charles Waldron’s tall, good-looking im-
personation of the hero improves on acquaintance.
He has an air of ingenuous youthfulness and
feminine manliness ; in fact, the actor’s method
of softening his voice inflections and in-
clining his head towards the person he ad-
dresses is strangely reminiscent of Nance
O’Neil’s masculine femininity. The statement that
he has a considerable Yankee accent should be
taken to mean that, as an English military officah
in the first act, he sounds unmistakably American
m contrast with Plimmer and others. And as a
hero generally Waldron puts a strong American
restraint upon himself, so that his alleged conflict
of feelings is left pretty much to one’s imagina-
tion. All of which remarks apply with four-fold
force to Miss Ola Humphrey, the Countess Diana
who needs to borrow a little of Mrs. G. B. Lewis’s
dramatic ginger. In her praiseworthy attempt
to cut a pathetic figure as Nat-U-Rich, the moody
squavv, Miss Florence Gleeson is handicapped by
her littleness, but this comes in handy at the
finish when Chief Tabywana (Hardy Kirkland)
brings her dead body along in his arms to testify
that his son-in-law has become a widower with
full power to marry Diana. The old Chief is one
of the pillars of the show, likewise Utah White
(who, having taught him to speak the Ute lan-
guage, interprets his utterance of it), and Big
Bill, the cow-puncher. Bill’s accent and bearing
are racy of the ranch in the same high degree that
Ca,sh Hawkins (George Bryant) is reeky of the
drinking saloon, and Nick, the bar-tender, is

“The Man from Mexico,” furnished with
a new songandance item, is getting near the end
of its fourth and last week at Melbourne Princess’s.
One more week remains at the disposal of the
Ward-Willoughby Co., and this will be devoted
to “ The Talk of the Town,” a farcical comedy by
Eille Norwood, whom Australia dimly remembers
as a member of the Brough-Boucicault forces. The
six nights’ limit for “ Talk of the Town ” is an un-
alterable arrangement, R. G. Knowles being due
at the Princess’s on the 28th.
By the courtesy of Willoughby and the
grace of Ward, a complimentary matinee will be
given to Leila Doubleday at the Princess’s on
Thursday next. The musical talent of Leila lias
been much talked about since she appeared at the
Town Hall some weeks ago, and the forthcoming
matinee concert will give a number of Melbourne
people a first opportunity to hear her, if they get
in ahead of the numerous admirers who want to
hear her again.
3}?
The present melodrama at the Royal will
pass away after Friday, deeply regretted by all
who can appreciate its audacious absurdities.
“ The Assassin ” never verges on dull probability
like some of the more blood-stained productions
of Anderson. The performers who try to be
funny are fairly successful; those who endeavor
to be not funny are exceedingly droll, and the
stage manager takes the biscuit with a series of
eccentric illuminations which come to pass, like
the writing on Belshazzar’s wall, for the purpose
of warning Willard that he must Prepare to Meet
his Doom. The Bill for next Saturday (21st) is
an original mystic drama, entitled “Dangerous
Women.” The word “mystic,” in its Ander-
sonian bearings, is full of promise.
* * *- *
Comedy sketches are the rule at the Opera
House just now. A while ago there was an
epidemic of j ugglery and acrobatics at the house
of Rickards, but the various professors of the
silent arts have all departed, and a number of
animated conversationalists remain in their stead.
Seymour and Dupre, who jump and dance, are
the least talkative of the sketchers. The latest
arrivals, Blake and Granby, have almost as fine a
flow of language as Homer Mason and his
Marguerite, the lady member of the firm being
foremost in the flow. All things considered,
Blake and Granby amount to something less than
they might have been at any other time than the
present.

Two of the most densely populated show
places in Melbourne last Saturday were the Town
Hall and Fitzgerald’s building. At the Hall
organist Lem are gave another recital, and at the
Building a number of fine, unimported artists
chopped and sawed wood in beautiful competi-
tion. Seemingly the Tasmanian performers were
first chop, and therefore they got the lion’s share
of the stakes, but if the virtue of the other con-
testants was its own reward they didn’t come off
badly. Seeing that Bent is throwing a lot of
chips in various directions, he might very well
spare a subsidy for an Annual Axemen’s Carnival
and set apart a gold medal for the soloist who sup-
ports himself on one leg and a crutch whilst
engaged in a wonderful underhand chop.

The remarkable support always given by
Adelaide to the Broughs seems to have had some-
thing of the personal element in it, for the busi-
ness done in the Brough-Flemming season now
ended was only moderate. Miss Temple and Miss
Major have put in some clever work in small
parts, and Philip Leslie is an artistic humorist
who deserves bigger opportunities. Miss Beatrice
Day and Miss Winifred Fraser shine most where
they can play up to each other in contrasted parts,
as in “ Quality Street ” and “ The Walls of
Jericho.” The co. played itself out with
the funny but foolish “Passport” on Friday,
and “The Little Michus ” is to follow.
Meanwhile, the Tivoli has started on another
dramatic season, at prices ranging from sixpence
to the giddy height of a florin. The Richter co.
puts up “The Socialist” for a start, and goes in
for politics, relying strongly on a “moral tone,”
but not disdaining the free use of firearms.
Notices stating that “ a petition in favor
of the abolition of the income-tax may be signed
here ” have lately been splashed about Melbourne
and suburbs. It is a significant fact that many of
them are on view in the windows of pawnbrokers
and other money-lenders.
The most potent ally, “humanistic” or
“ Seddonistic ” legislation possesses in its efforts
to enlighten and enliven mankind is D.C L.
Whisky. When the wise man turneth to his
D.C.L. he gets the best sort of stimulant to the
best qualities of the mind. Also, it illumines
with the arc light of cheerfulness the gloomiest
spots of thought.*
Bruce Smith to Sydney Stab, concerning
the progressive land-tax: —
In Australia the several Governments have in the past
sold their land in the open market, and have used the
purchase money for public purposes. Therefore, this
spirit of confiscation was a sort of public crime, and would
not be denuded of its dishonest nature by having legis-
lative sanction.
Does B. Smith really believe that “ Australian
Governments have in the past sold their lands in
the open market ” ? Did B. Smith never hear
of the methods by which some of the estates passed
originally from the Government to private hands?
A short course of Australian history would make
B. Smith a much wiser man. (On second thoughts
it wouldn’t. Nothing could make B. Smith a
wiser man. He can acquire information, but not
wisdom.)
How to Grow a Mocstache.— Scores of people are
asking for a good recipe for producing a moustache.
Well, here’s the best moustache producer ever heard of—
Craig and Ait-ken’s Capillarine. This pushing firm sells
this preparation at 10s. 6d. a jar. It is a wonderfully
successful article, and, as a proof of its reliability it has
been sold in Australia for nearly a quarter of a century.
If it were not a good article it could never have lasted
and the firm could not have afforded to advertise it all
this time. It is a secret formula, guaranteed pure, and
will not injure the skin in any shape or form. There is
just one thing it will do and do it well—better than any
other article of its kind—and that is: grow a full
luxuriant moustache. The agents, Craig and Aitken, of
654 George-street, Sydney, send Capillarine post free
under sealed cover for 10s. 6d.*

round Stratford-on-Avon. She found him apathetic at

Madame. “Ah !” said Arditi. “Ze librettist ! ”
A severe loss to Australian music is the death of
Heaps, the Sydney violin and ’cello virtuoso.
Said Mrs. Merry-go-round: “I like the life. I’ve
lived in tents for 26 years, and travelled with the merry-
go-round for 17, principally in Queensland. As you say,
it’s agreed with me right enough ; and—l suppose it’s use
—I miss the tent. Houses don’t seem home-like. It’s a
good payin’ game if you have a bit of money to start with.
You could start in a small way for about £200; but it runs
into a lot of money if you have waggons, tents, &c., and
a traction engine for shifting camp and working the show.
We build our own turn-outs, barring the “ horses ” ; and
this one cost about £lOOO, engine and all. The wooden
steeds are American and Herman made ; but we’re getting
our next lot made in Sydney.”
Harry Leston has for some time been seriously ill,
appendicitis being the disturbing element. He is very
slowly recovering, and was last week admitted into the
Walker Convalescent Hospital.
The stage infant phenomenon is sufficiently wearisome,
but considerably more monotonous is the juvenile utterer
of heavy moral sentiments. Melbourne has recently been
afflicted with a specimen in the person of “ Master Roy
Painter, Australia’s boy temperance orator.” The infant
hurls his platitudes under the admiring chairmanship of
the parental Samuel Painter, a frequent candidate for
Parliament on the “ good man ” ticket.
T. Bent let himself go very freely at the Melbourne
City Newsboys’ gathering t’other night. He made what
he aptly described as a series of “ rambling remarks,” and
when he wasn’t remarking ramblesomely he perpetrated
snatches of song, the contrast between the 18 stone
Premier and his 18 ounce singing voice being too funny
for words. On these festive occasions Bent is oblivious
to the fact that he is half-a-century behind the times in
his frivolity. The other night he quite seriously exhorted
the boys to join him in ditties which had died with their
grandfathers—ditties concerning flies and bluebottles, the
refrain whereof is “ tiddle-iddle-ee.” An ordinary old
man notes that most of the popular babbles of his boy-
hood survive only in his failing memory, but these are the
very relics that Bent submits to the rising generation as
things of enduring interest. Many people give Bent
credit for being designedly absurd in order to join in a
hearty laugh against himself. As a matter of fact he is
unconscious of his own foolishness. He attaches great
importance to the choruses he learned when he was
young, and he reckons that the stamp of his infant
approval has conferred a lasting distinction upon “ tiddle-
iddle-ee.” He speaks of ancient happenings as things
which “ everybody remembers,” because he regards Bent
as pretty nearly everybody.
Apropos “The Squaw Man”’ An American humorist
asserts that the stage Indian belongs to “an extinct
tribe that never existed.”
“ Lil ” : The gallery-boy who is paid by the manager to
take the villain’s machinations seriously (8., 5'7/’O6) is a
comparatively harmless type. A really objectionable
stage hanger-on is the hoodlum who pesters members of
minor troupes into keeping him and his pals in beer
money, oh the ground that any performer who doesn’t
contribute will be howled down. Many a poor serio-girl
who has stood out against blackmail has been reduced to
tears by these miserable loafers. Painful as the serio’s
performance sometimes is, she doesn’t quite deserve that,
and occasionally she has an aged parent to support.
The substitution of Ada Crossley for Clara Butt, at the
recent Handel Festival, was necessitated by an interest-
ing domestic matter. On May 29th Miss Butt (Mrs. Rum-
ford) presented her husband with a small boy (who is to
have Princess Christian for his godmother, if you please),
and since then the baby has been singing louder than
momtner. The Butts— i.s., the Kennerley Rumfords—are
not due in Australia until next year, but in the meantime
the Tait Brethren have their soprano songstress to boom.
Nevin Tait writes from London that Dolores will reach
Sydney (D.V.) in the first week of August. She will come
via San Francisco, unless another earthquake intervenes.
Dolores has been very busy during her four years’ absence
from Australia. She has sung in almost every capital city
in Europe, and was in fine voice the last time she warbled.
A. W. Arnold and his wife, two ancient Thespians,
dating from the early digging’ days, have just celebrated
their golden wedding at Avoca (Vic.). For two years
they played with G. V. Brooke, and Arnold also played a
part in the Eureka Stockdale affair. His eldest son is
on the staff of Adelaide Chronicle.
“ O’Fipp ” : Hugh Ward’s early experience with a min-
strel troupe (recorded in Bulletin 5/7/’O6) reminds that a
big percentage of notable American actors started in the
same way. Joe Jefferson “jumped Jim Crow” with a
blackened face at the age of four. Tragedians Edwin
Booth, Lawrence Barrett, and Edwin Forrest all worked
their way from nigger minstrel business to the top of
their profession. Also Phineas T. Barnum was a Brudder
Bones early in his career, but he carefully smothered the
guilty secret lest it should compromise his dignity. A
dime museum man’s dignity is a fearful and wonderful
thing, and not to be tampered with lightly.
After closing the Melbourne season, the Willoughby
Ward Co. goes on to Geelong and Warrnambool, and opens
in Adelaide on August 4,
Madame Oalv6, in her first appearance as the page
Cherubino, in Mozart’s “ Nozze di Figaro,” thought her
calves were decidedly not good enough. So she padded.
At the first interval, the manager said, “ That was absurd.
Your own figure would be the exact thing.” So she went
on in the second act without pads, and could not under-
stand why the manager was angrier than ever. The
manager thought there was too much variety about the
page Cherubino.
“O’Fipp”: The officials of the South Melbourne musi-
cal and elocutionary competitions have arisen in their
wrath. They declare, with all the pent-up fury induced
by dealing with competitions for more years than oiie
likes to think of, that they’ll be jiggered or something if
they’ll listen till the bitter end of a song or a recitation
after the performer has shown right at the beginning that
he hasn’t even Buckley’s chance of securing a prize.
Also, they think variety of choice should be allowed the
performers, instead of making all in one division toil
through the same piece. Before this epoch-making de-
claration it was no uncommon thing for 23 girls to arise
one after another, and plod steadily through the seven
verses of “I’m a merry Gitana ! Ha, ha! Ha, ha!”
Then 19 fair to middling bassos (mostly middling) would
severally describe themselves as “The monarch of the
storm,” each asserting 18 distinct times (342
times in all) that “ I chuckle and laugh, ho! ho!
ho! ho! ” But all this conscientious mirth was
as nothing to the efforts given to an ungrateful
world by another set of geniuses. After numerous pain-
ful repetitions of “ The Speech of Lucius Junius Brutus
over the Dead Body of Lucretia” had done their best to
scatter a few more corpses o’er a smiling land, the bright
under-35 division favored, about 61 times, with good old
stodgy G. R. Sims’s “ Billy’s Rose ” and “ Christmas Day
in the Work-’ouse.” Then there was usually a teri-
minutes interval for the audience to retire while the care-
taker swept up the dropped pathos and conveyed it to
the ash-bin. The officials seem to think they will avoid
all the untempting repetition by their new rule as to
selection of pieces. But they little know the precedent-
following mind of the victim to the competition habit.
As soon will Brer Rabbit cease to burrow as the competi-
tion fiend relinquish his passion for the one restricted
round of imported Sims and imported “ Parrhasius.”
The famous old-time actress, Dora Jordan, mother of
William IV.’s 10 children, comes into notice once more
with the auction sale in London of a big bundle of her
unpublished love-letters to the unworthy object of her
affections. The letters, curiously enough, were preserved
by William’s legitimate (and childless) wife, Queen Ade-
laide. Probably she kept them in an ice-chest to throw
up at her Silly Billy when there was a domestic row on.
William’s shabby treatment of Mrs. Jordan tarnishes what
little gilt there was on his character. It is a long and
sordid tale, ending with Dora’s death in poverty, exiled
from England by debt and robbed of her children by sweet
William.
“ Tho Dairymaids,” Robert Courtneidge’s musical
comedy, now running- at the Apollo Theatre (Lon.), with
Carrie Moore in the lead, is being strongly recommended
by physical culturists. Although the aestheticism of
athleticism is somewhat satirised in the scene of Miss
Pyechases’ Gymnasium, where Carrie sings “ The Sandow
Girl,” the demonstration is very fetching, and it is re-
garded by professors as a first-rate ad. for their schools.
Muscular development of beauty in the she-sex has
become a matter of serious public interest since the less
enlightened time of Pinero’s comedy, “ The Amazons.”
The Countess’ three daughters were put through a daily
course of physical training as part of their education in
mannishness, but the scene in “ The Dairymaids ” intro-
duces dumb-bells as aids to beauty and implements of
girlish grace.
“ Philo ” : An Australian artist who has done well
abroad is Nat Phillips. Failing to obtain recognition by
the Australian vaudeville houses, he left, not quite three
years ago, with another Australian named Tommy
Armstrong, for the land of the Beef Trust and the earth-
quake. He did well in U.S.A. and Canada. Then with
his wife, Miss Daisy Meritt, of Sydney, he started East-
ward, as “Merritt and Phillips, the Whistling Girl and
the Dancing Dude.” Again did well in U.S.A., and
went on to England, and appeared with success in
London. After working the English provinces, they
were booked by Richard Warner for a tour of Europe.
After that they will return to London for a season at the
Alhambra. Nat Phillips comes from an old theatrical
stock. He is a grandson of Raphael Tolano, a one-time
incandescent light in the Australian theatrical business,
who was lessee of the old Prince of Wales and Victoria
Theatres, and the first manager to produce “ East
Lynne ” in Australia.
Collier’s most popular pieces in America are “The
Dictator” and “The Man from Mexico.” Hugh Ward
had bousrht the Australian rights of “ Mexico,” and
might have secured “ The Dictator ” likewise before there
was any thought of Collier coming to this country.
George Bryant, who plays the quarrelsome cattle
duffer in “The Squaw Man,” and plays it very well, is
only new to this country as a member of the imported
section of the co. In his young days, 20 years ago—he
hasn’t visibly aged in the meantime—George Bryant was
a member of Williamson, Garner and Musgrove's Dramatic
Co. what time the firm was running Melbourne Royal.
He went to America to try his luck, and got very good
inducements to stay there.
A DUAL CONDEMNATION.
Bung and Stiggins join hands in barring a
bar gt the new Sydney railway station.
Late boss showman Bailey once possessed the first baby
elephant born in captivity. Barnum offered him 20,000
dollars for it, but Barnum’s offer was in itself worth more
to Bailey than that. He toured the country exhibiting
a flaming' poster, headed, “What Barnum thinks of my
Baby Elephant,” and Barnum’s offer underneath. Then
Barnum recognised a kindred spirit, and went into
partnership with Bailey.

“ Quan ” : Bulletin (28/6/’O6) quotes the
case of the charwomen employed by the N.S.W.
Taxation Department, each of whom has to clean
and scrub about 3500 square feet of flooring for a
miserable£so per annum. Little enough, I’ll allow;
but I know a teacher in a seventh class N.S.W.
public school who sweeps the school and weather-
shed every day, scrubs them every week, and also
cleans the windows weekly for £2 12s. Id. a year.
(He’s legally entitled to £2 12s. 1 5-7 d., but the
Education Department systematically robs him of
the fraction.) The floor space of the school ex-
ceeds 1000 square feet. The weather-shed amounts
to nearly 400 square feet, and there are 11 win-
dows, eight of them containing 191, square feet of
glass each.
Odol is so instantly refreshing in use, so
pleasant of odor and taste, and so lasting in its antiseptic
effects that it ought to find its way into every house
where good teeth and healthy mouths are valued, which
should be everywhere. Decay is prevented or arrested by
its use, and the teeth last twice as long' as they otherwise
would do. Bad teeth not only detract from one’s
personal appearance but have such a serious effect upon
the functions of digestion that chronic ill health often
follows.*

The prices averaged at the recent Sydney
stud-sheep sales give some idea of the way land-
holders are rushing in to stock up and get 2os. for
their fat sheep and top prices for their wool (up
North only a few years ago thousands of fat bul-
locks were sold for 255. a head). The Hill-Clark
firm ran through 545 merinoes which averaged
£23 3s. 2d.; their little Tasmanian lot crept up to
an average of £63 13s. 3d.—which is pretty dear
mutton chop.
The N.S.W. Sheepbreeders’ Show, re-
cently concluded to the relief of Sydney news-
paper readers, on whose nerves the blessed affair
was getting, gavesome £BOO in prizes, of which Sam
M'Caughey and J. S. Horsfall took nearly half,
winning between them all the decent prizes. The
members of the association were grumbling at the.
futility of competing with the long-pursed pair of
squatters when a whisper went round that the-
association, which is representative of the back-
bone of the country, would take it as a favor if
the prize-winners would kindly return their
cheques as a donation to the funds. Some angry
ones blurted out that if the association was to be
run for the glorification of M'Caughey and
Horsfall, the least they could do would be to
finance it, whereupon it was announced that only
those who wished to return their prizes in a
purely graceful, gentlemanly way would be ex-
pected to do so, and the meeting dispersed in a
■trained kind of wav.

SOCIETY
A moody Wairarapa farmer lately adopted
an effective way of ending a weary life. He
collected a stack of dry wood in the centre of a
paddock, set fire to the four cardinal points of the
heap, and when he was satisfied that it would
blaze all right, jumped on the top, and shot
himself with a rifle.
* * * *
An unusual and extra-certain sort of suicide
was that of Carlo Rusconi, a Castleinaine (Vic.)
charcoal-burner, who lately took a revolver in
each hand and fired into each temple, the bullets
meeting about the centre. But goodness knows
why lie should have taken such trouble when there
was a nice, easy, painless death lurking at hand in
his charcoal.

In tlie street, the other day, a big
woman went past. Said Williams: “There
goes Mrs. , from the pub. at the corner, and
if ever there wa3 a lady she’s one. Why, many’s

‘‘Hamer” : A short experience of Auck-
ind (M.L ) gave me the impression that it is a
city of inflexible women. A friend of mine took
possession of me one night and the first inflexible
woman we met was the wife of a publican, who
insisted that we should leave the hotel at 10
o clock. We subsequently went with some other
friends to. supper and when we were loath to
leave, the inflexible manageress of the restaurant
informed us that she had put our hats outside,
and we went out to find them When I reached
my boarding-house I found the bolt 011 the door ;
and my inflexible landlady refused to open the
premises she said she wasnt going to have her
boarders coming home at all hours. I sought my
friend’s house, and found that the inflexible
woman he was married to had handed his blankets
out through the window and told hint he -right
as well finish the night outside. So we sat
together and lamented on an inflexible doorstep.
The trained nurses of Melbourne are con-
fronted by the discouraging fact that, whereas
their numbers are increasing at an absurd rate,
the health of the city and—in a lesser degree—of
the suburbs is improving steadily. The sewering
ot the metropolis has affected a remarkable
diminution in the death-rate. For 1905 it was the
lowest on record. Typhoid cases are an incon-
siderable quantity; and young men of the type
that formerly suffered from lung and chest com-
piaints are now adopting the “Sandow” cure,
and the fresh air cure, and the ministering angels
in cap and apron are getting very few patients
"ho want to marry them. The medical profession
also is overstocked, but the doctors suffer less
than the nurses from scarcity of work, for there
are always plenty of people determined to be
rather sick if they can’t be seriously ill. These
are no good to the nurses. The doctors want all
the money that can be got from such cases.
Perhaps if the truth were brought fairly
home to the public there would be more nurse
a i i' l the sick-rooms. Except in
palpably serious cases a nurse would be summoned
to the patient before a doctor was consulted and
the bright young woman with the cheerful “bed-
side manner would give her opinion as to
whether a doctor was an urgent necessity. More
often than not, he wouldn’t be required, and the
person—patient or otherwise—who paid the
nurse s fee would rejoice over a saving of money.
Half the time of the detectives investi-
gating the so-far-insoluble St. Kilda (Vic.)
murder is wasted in pondering over theories
offered by amateur detectives. The deceased
reached the hospital with a deep gash, as
from a hatchet, over his right eye, and his
right ear half severed. Some amazing “sleuth
hounds” have advanced the theory that it must
have been a case of suicide. Others demonstrate
to their own satisfaction that deceased acci-
dentally fell into a deep drain, comjiletely ignor-
ing the blood-splashed wall of the lavatory.
Another original theorist demonstrates that the
dead man must certainly have been kicked by a
horse. There being no horses in the reserve
round the lavatory, the animal presumably came
from his stable for the express purpose of assault-
ing O Rourke, and having smitten him a few
times, twisted himself out of the lavatory,
jumped a 20ft. drain, and returned to his manger.
But the quaintest theory is that of a Brighton
genius, to the effect that deceased was struck by
a meteorite. “ A meteorite,” says he, “must fail
somewhere ; why not on a man’s head ? ” The
theory is all right, as far as it goes ; but it doesn’t
go to the end. It doesn’t say what became of the
meteorite after it had wounded O’Rourke. A
theory that, to account for the slaying of an
ordinary gardener, brings a huge meteorite out of
the heavens, makes it smite him in two places
and then leaves it nowhere, is only a half-baked
theory, after all.

made to pronounce words “ Dike English gairls”
by their snob teachers. The “ bleat ” thus instilled
is far worse than the much-talked-of Australian
accent.

WHEN THE BUTCHER WANTS HIS
MONEY.
MRS. BLANK: “ Before you married me you used
to say you were well off."
MR. B LANK: "So I was; but I was a fool , and
didn't know it."
The Jap who lives in Queensland doesn’t
wear any tail feathers to speak of ; but the visit-
ing Jap is a whole menagerie. Decently, after
the small, gold-braided person from the Monkey
warships had been there and been fussed over as
usual, the municipal people who run the Cairns
(N.Q.) tramway were met with a demand that
Japanese should be permitted to ride in the
“white” section of the local tram. The local
Jap didn’t want to ride “white ” ; he lives in the
discolored part of the town from choice, and pigs-
in there with Ah Chin, Tommy Tanna, and the
rest of the miscellaneous collection of Chinatown,
and he would prefer to travel with the same
people that he prefers to live with. But the bump-
tious visiting Monkey, swollen after his scrap with
the unready Bear, fired in his demand, and as the
Cairns tramway bosses didn’t know what to do
about it, they passed it on to the State Govern-
ment ; and the State Government drew back from
the prickly subject, and, in a pained voice, asked
the Cairns people if they couldn’t manage their
own tramway traffic. Both State and local
authorities have spent some months trying to for-
get all about the matter; but, every time they
think they are forgetting nicely, a memo, comes
from the Japanese Department of Frills and
Feathers asking them what they have done about
it. They might suggest that the Jap should
devote the next- million years to whitening his
face, and then he could come back and ride white.
* * * *
A correspondent:—
The allotment of the Rhodes Scholarships by the
shocking University Senate wants looking into. The
first N.S. Wales scholar was Barton, and though the
choice was not the best at the time, it was passable as a
compliment to Barton’s father. Since then two other
appointments have been made, and both of them gro-
tesque. Rhodes laid it down that candidates must excel
in manly sports, but the only claim of Rogers in this con-
nection was that he once won a handicap at a college car-
nival; and that of M’Callum that he once played some
“B grade’’lawn tennis with small success for the Uni-
versity. On second thoughts, however, the latter had
another claim inasmuch as his father was a mem-
ber of the Senate. But bad as these appointments
were, a much worse one is in prospect. A son of
another member of the Senate is being coached labori-
ously in several sports, for which he has never shown
any liking or aptitude, with a view to his selection as the
next Rhodes scholar. He is a studious youth of some in-
telligence, and it is possible that he might do N.S. Wales
credit in the schools. But it was not youths of this
academic sort that Rhodes wanted—he stipulated for
muscle and pluck as well as for brains, and, whether his
wishes were right or wrong, it is only decency to respect
them.

An interesting work going forward is the
improvement of the Saltwater River (Melb.) by
subscriptions from the inhabitants of Flemington
and Essendon, with an eye, of course, to honest
boodle. The river comes to the racecourse from
Maribyrnong, and here the Essendonians are
going to rival Henley-on-the-Yarra, which won’t
be difficult. It is pointed out by deep-sinking
local antiquarians that Wedge and party came up
to Maribyrnong by boat before the other arm of
the river, the Yarra proper, was discovered,

Last week’s weddings in Australasia were
rather sensational. One bridegroom, sauntering
round Ballarat just before the ceremony, was
robbed of all the cash he had saved up for honey-
moon expenses, and he had to moon on tick.
Another bridegroom was arrested for forgery
while in the church waiting for the bride, and
having a wooden leg, and a revolver in that leg,
shot himself dead before he reached the lock-up.
In the third case the couple managed to get
united all right, but, just as they were going
away, discovered that the bridegroom’s father had
hanged himself in the back-yard. Thereupon the
guests dispersed. On the way home a boat-load
of them capsized while crossing a stream, and did
a shiver on a convenient snag in the river until
daylight.
A RIGHT OF MAN’S.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T meet reformers day and night ;
The meddlers snatch at each delight.
They seek to trap me unawares,
And change my favorite sins for theirs.
I take a drink, and it excites
The lank and sallow Rechabites ;
1 gamble in a bob or so,
And Judkins hunts me to and fro ;
I hug a girl, and find it good—
Out jump the shrieking brotherhood 1
Grown weary now, I ask my clan
To barrack for the rights of man.
Especially the best of all,
The right that came through Adam’s fall.
Inalienable, treasured long—
The right to do a little wrong !
vie.

For the best part of a fortnight after his
funeral the grave of Richard Seddon was guarded
by troops. Uneasy feelings got abroad that body-
snatchers were feared, but an official announce-
ment stated that the soldiers were there to guard
the mats and greenstone relics and other curios
sent by the Maoris as tokens of respect for the
departed leader. The Maori custom is that these
shall lie upon the grave for a oertain number of
days, and, the white curio-hunter having no respect
for the law of tapu, it was thought necessary to
employ guards.

It was left to the Prahran branch of the
Vic. P.L.C. to draw attention to the insane prac-
tice of the “ authorities ” in forwarding to a city
laundry dirty linen from the Broadmeadows Con-
sumptive Sanatorium in the same railway vans
with butter and milk. We’re not so far ahead of
Chicago, after all.
* * * •
This seems to be a grievance of some kind:
That thoroughly decorous nook of Melbourne Bohemia,
Fasoli’s Pension Suisse, has again been dragged from its
normal routine of cheap meals, smart talk, and harmless
unconventionality. This time by the Age, which pub-
lished much inept comment on the little cafe and its
habitues. If the reporter was picturesque about Fasoli s,
Fasolians are vitriolic about his work, and waste a good
deal of meal-time arguing hotly on the question of Press
privilege. The “ regulars,” who vary from State Premiers
and puisne Judges to foreign gentlemen who play classic
melodies by the kerbstone, don’t appreciate the cheap
publicity occasionally given to their haunt. It causes an
invasion of their pleasant preserve by worthy gentlemen,
who bring along part of the family, with a view to seeing
the life from the inside. The simpering Miss, who
imagines she is being wicked, also turns up ; and, worst of
all, the devil of a fellow who talks “ pidgin English ” to the
second oldest customer, who is eloquent in two languages
and f 1 lent in five. Even Fasolians have feelings, and they
aon’t care to be regarded as zoological curiosities by subur-
ban S- mday-school inspectors. Such intruders disturb the
gorge of the oldest customer. He longs for those days
when no impudent ink-slingers came along to write unkind
comments on the people who advised as to the merits of
the garlic salads, and dropped a gentle hint regarding the
tactlessness of the casual diner’s usual tribute to the
place—“ so Bo’emian.” The 0.0. thinks of those nights
when “ the bovs ” —amongst them the Lindsay brothers,
Randolph Bedford, Phil May’s “ Parson,” and brilliant
old Hammond—entranced the hours with jests that
were original; phrases that were memorable; and ideas that
seemed unique as they budded in that din of laughter
and wit. He ponders on a magnificently empirical doc-
trine often expounded at that table by Clement VVragge ;
on that weird French youth, who, after a large dinner,
would sing Hawaiian dirges as well as any native. He
thint i of charming Manaoli, who was recalled
from a banana shop here to the command of an
Abyssinian expedition numbering seven hundred
men, chiefly selected from the graduates of Italian
Universities j of his brave heart and passionate brain.
Memory excites ghosts of Bedford’s objurgations, and
they hover disgustedly about the heads of those inquisi-
tive Philistines who, prying there, find in that dingy
room merely “ such quaint people, and such odd food.”
And the oldest customer softly says “ damn copy-
hunters,” and goes home early.
THE DANGEROUS “POTTED PHRASE.”
LADY (to applicant for work): “But your
trousers are very shabby.”
T OURIST: “ Shabby pants is nothing, mum. They
often covers a warm heart.”
The Maori King, a huge ocean tramp
which lately left Sydney for Japanese ports, is
reputed to be a vessel with a hidden history. It
is alleged on bad authority that, when the late
Baltic fleet was on its erratic way from Russia to
its grave in Tsushima Strait, the Maori King was
a sort of pilot vessel, and, under a French captain,
led the way for the motley line of warships with
their peasant crews and ignorant commanders till
the fleet was well past Singapore, and any day
might bring the smoke from Jap funnels above
the horizon. Then it did an unobtrusive “get,”
and went back to its trade of tramping for freights
—so the bad authority says.

“ Ageratum ” : Can anyone tell me why
catmittees always take charge of the morals of the
community? Once, in a rash moment, I was
cajoled into joining a branch of the Braille
Society, to convert books into Braille type for the
blind, and I went to a committee meeting. It
was mostly an ancient she-male and unmarried
committee, and its one aim seemed to be to
choose the good little book that ought to have
died, for the use of the Indignant Blind. But this
dav some frivolous and influential outside sub-
scriber had had the temerity to ask them to put
Dean Alford’s “Queen’s English” into Braille,
and they were suspicious about it. The secretary
said, “ I don’t know the book at all,” in a tone of
dark meaning. The rest of the committee said
“ No.” Then the outside subscriber produced a
copy and handed it round, and that catmittee
touched it gingerly, as though it would bite. One
of them even opened the cover, but shut it again
in a hurry, and the outside subscriber began to
look annoyed. At last, the secretary blurted out,
“But—is —it well—is it a nice book? Is it a
book—one could er well, have in the
house?” The owner seized the “Queen’s
English,” and walked to the door. “No,” she
said, sweetly, as she went out, “I scarcely think
it is. You would probably not understand it.”
* * * *
Wealthy Chinese in Australia have long
been accustomed to ship the remains of their dead
relatives to China. Handling dead Chow is not
a nice business for the undertaker, and had to be
paid for heavily, and the coffin-gear necessary, and
the freight, were also expensive items ; so recently
the Chows have made an offer to some of the Vic.
Benevolent Asylums to pay the railway fares of
aged Chinese inmates to Melbourne whence they
are shipped in a job lot as live freight to China.
Of course the Asylums jumped at the offer, and
John is pleased, too, at making a good deal.
* * * *
Tommy Bent went fairly mad over the Mel-
bourne papers misreporting a song he squeaked
before a crowd of helpless newsboys the other night.
“My songs are not the songs of the ordinary
music hall,” he said. “They are the pqetry of
the days when Shakspeare was the favorite poet
in the land.” Then he supplied the correct ver-
sion. Here it is :
Which goes to show that a fairly successful poli-
tician can be made of a man whose literary tastes
are not superior to those of a kanaka.
* * * *
The revenge of the great Powers is a fear-
some thing. Recently H.M.S. Prometheus pow-
wowed with the French cruiser Vaucluse to
discuss the best method of avenging three French
sailors, killed and eaten by Malekula natives
some months back. A joint expedition landed
and marched with great fierceness to the guilty
village. The chiefs were sternly ordered to pro-
duce the murderers ; the chiefs humbly promised
to produce them, went away, and stayed away.
Then the armed representatives of two great
Powers killed 30 pigs, and triumphantly returned.
* * * *
Archdeacon Oakes, of Kelso, who proposes
work for the unemployed on condition that they
should attend church on Sundays, merely repeats
the formula applying to ticket-of-leave men in the
convict days. The convict received his ticket
with the stipulation that he should attend church
on Sunday, and if the parson reported him for
non-attendance, the ticket was withdrawn and he
was sent back to the gang.
