Dear B.—Your remarks (21/6/’ot>) about
the preacher-man and his need of more-or-less sin-
ful relaxation fit the parson, who oughtn’t to be
one, to a hair, but don’t seem to suit the born sin-
shifter. The latter never goes to an island chasing
brown women—unless to “save” them and their
benighted brethren. He lives to preach. He is
twin-brother to the actor-man. The limelight is
an absolute necessity to both. Take the preacher
away from his mothers’ meetings and bun-scrim-
mages, his -anniversaries and Bible-classes, and
you banish him from what is dearer than life
itself. When a preacher leaves the church he
goes on the stage or the platform. Send him
(or the actor) where he cannot be seen or heard,
and you send him to purgatory. Rev. James,
the Biddy, is not a type ; he is the deception that
proves the rule.—L.O.
■K * * *
“Lil ” : The common cat ( Felis backyardi)
is a frequent habitue of the shops of grocers and
other tradesmen. I saw one reposing peacefully
in a bin of eggs the other day. The cat is allowed
to make herself offensively at home almost any-
where ; piles of bread are specially favored as
resting-places, and some produce vendors seem
to regret having to shift tabby as she reposes on
the bacon-cutting board and nestles up against
the cheese. It is “up to” the health authorities
to see that the animal is made to stay in its own
yard.
* * * *
The electric trams in Sydney brought bad
times amongst the cabs, and the all-night trams
struck another blow at them. The cabman who
used to scorn anything that was not gold after tram
hours has lost the run of those hours now.
You notice the result more in the country than
the city. Hansom cabs are appearing everywhere
—in Tam worth, for instance, there are 18 of
them ; and what business a cab can do in a town
of one short street is a mystery.
* * * *
Be “In Memoriam” notices: Observe
how often the most gushful sentiment goes
with the shrewdest of business methods. The
particularly sobful notice, introducing half-a-
dozen inches of bathetic home-made verse, nearly
always come from a drapery Crcesus, or afish-and-
chips capitalist, or a lady who has made a fortune
out of the Aunt Sally profession. A wail that
shows some attempt at restraint is usually paid
for by one who would do extremely well in busi-
ness if he or she could only be a little more gush-
ful. As for the benighted persons who don’t
advertise in the tearful columns at all when they
have a chance, they’ll never die rich.
TWO SOLILOQUIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She.
What do I in secret dream,
As beneath my lids I glance—
Deep my eyes as any stream ?
What do they in secret dream
As they through the lashes gleam ?—
That I’m your first love perchance.
No ; I more ambitious dream—
That I am your last romance.
He.
What is it I most regret
’Mongst the girls with whom I tarried?
Who, amongst the girls I met,
Who is she I most regret?
Can it be that I will fret
For that wicked one who harried,
Or that good one whom I let
Bore me—or the one I married ?
N.S.W.
HELEN JEROME.

No Heart for Anything
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“Up till five years ago I didn’t know what it
was to be even one day sick,” said Mrs. Fanny
Smith, who has a fruit and confectionery business
in Union-street, Adamstown, Newcastle. “Then,
little by little, my health started to break up.
My blood grew weaker and thinner, till at last it
was little better than water. In summer I was fit
for nothing. I could hardly drag my legs after
me. For two and three days together I had to lie
up in bed. The doctor treated me for nearly
twelve months, but I wasted away in spite of ail
he could do. I don’t know what would have
happened if it hadn’t been for Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People. I looked as if I
couldn’t live a month when a friend got me to
give them a fair trial. I started them just before
Christmas, and I didn’t leave them off till March.
By that time I didn’t have a single thing the
matter with me. My health was perfect, and it
has been perfect ever since. Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills didn't merely build me up—but they cured
me for good.
“Before coming up here to Newcastle I had a
laundry business at Kirribilli Pt., North Sydney,”
said Mrs. Smith. “ That meant a lot of work
and a lot of worry —and the doctor said that work
and worry would be the death of me. I went to
bed tired, and I got up the same way next
morning. There wasn’t a bone in my body that
didn’t ache. Everything was a bother to me.
Some days my feet and legs ached till I simply
had to give in and rest. I was utterly down-
hearted, and never wanted to talk to anyone. I
tried hard to tight off my sickness, but it was no
use. Everyone noticed me going down hill. It
often took me the best part of a day to turn out
work that I used to do in half an-hour. My face
had a care-worn look, and not a scrap of color in
it. My lips turned almost white. Round my
eyes were heavy black rings. All my blood
seemed to be drying up. My hands were always
clammy, and my fingers stone-cold. I kept
taking the doctor s medicine, but I grew thinner
and weaker.
“ There seemed no hope of me ever getting
strong again,” Mrs. Smith added. “My appetite
got more and more faddy. Soon I could not fancy
my food at all. I found fault with the nicest
dinner. Whenever I did force anything down, it
turned sour, and made mo feel sick. But all I
could bring up wus wind and mouthfuls of
horrible bitter water, every now and then. My
tongue was horribly dirty. Every morning I got
up with a taste like brass in my mouth. I was
so miserable that I didn’t care whether 1 lived or
died. .
“ Month after month, I was gettiner weaker.
It was as much as I could do to stand with the
dragging pain in my back. Often my head felt
as if it would burst. There were days when I
could hardly keep my eyes open with the pain
across my forehead. Even when I went to bed
my temples kept throbbing. It was seldom that
I ever slept well. For hours I twisted and turned
from one side to another. Many a time it was
daylight before I dozed off. 1 got up as limp as a
rag, and I simply could not shake off an utter
down-hearted feeling. All day long I was
nervous. If a knock came to the door, I felt it
was some bad news for me. I used to work myself
up to an awful pitch. A cold sweat broke out on
me, and a horrible dread of death came over me.
It was all I could do to keep from fainting.
“When the doctor’s medicines failed to cure
me, 1 got all sorts of mixtures from the chemist,
but they did me no good,” Mrs. Smith went on.
“ I didn’t know what to do for the best when a
friend brought me some Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
for Pale People, and begged me to give them a
good trial. When I had been taking them for a
couple of weeks everyone said how much better I
was looking. Certainly, I was eating three times
as much. When I was doing so 'yell I said I
wouldn’t leave off Dr. Williams Pink Pills till
they cured me —even if I had to take them for a
year. For fear of missing a dose I used to carry
a box about with me, no matter where I went.
Gradually the color came back to my cheeks, and
my lips got a healthy red. The headaches mm
backaches eased up. My nerves grew steady,
and I started to sleep well at night. Every
morning I got up full of life and ready for my
day’s work. Since the time I left off Dr. Williams
Pink Pills I have never fainted once—my heart is
now sound and strong.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured Mrs. Smith by
going down to the root of the trouble in the blood.
That is why they never fail. Bad blood is the
cause of all common diseases like anaemia (blood-
lessness), eczema, paleness, headaches, neuralgia,
sciatica, rheumatism, lumbago, general weakness
and the special ailments that only women-folk
know. Bad blood is the one cause—and Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are the one cure, because
they actually make new blood. They do just that
one thing but they do it well. They don’t bother
with mere symptoms. They don’t cure for a day
they cure for good. But, of course, you must
get the genuine Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People—in wooden boxes—never in glass,
bottles. Price 2s. 9d. a box, six boxes 15s. 3d.—
from retailers, or, post free, from the Dr. Williams
Medicine Co., Sydney.*

The capital is £90,000, £65,000 of which is represented lw
preimses ; and for the part of the premises not usld in th'l
business, gross rents of £265 were received in the half
year. At only 3 per cent., the premises should retmn,'
on the book value, £1950 a year ; deduct £530 received as
rent, and the half-year’s proportion of the balance of
£14:10, plus rates, repairs, etc., must be added to the
expenses shown above to arrive at the true
position. But even then the expenses are low, and
the profits are excellent. At 2£ per cent, on the
corpus of an estate and 21 per cent, on the income, the
total commission, if the estate remains in the office for 10
years, is equal to 7s. per cent, per annum on the value of
the estate, provided the latter brings in 4 per cent,
income ; if it remains in the office 15 years, the two com-
missions together are worth ss. 4d. per cent, per annum ;
it zu years, 4s. 6d. per cent, per annum. Probably the
average estate is wound up well within 10 years, so that
tne commission exceeds 7s. per cent, per annum on the
alue of the estate; and the difference between that and
tne actual expenses shown above is substantial enough to
make most financial concerns’ mouths water.

Kalgoorlie mine managers who have reduced
costs have had so much glorification that men now
announce reduced costs even :f they have to throw truth
down to the bottom level in order to doit. Whenever
reduced costs are mentioned, look first to see if extraction
hasn’t been reduced too. Even that can be faked bv
reducing the size of the “tons”; so look next to see if
the tonnage has suddenly increased. In many oases you
won’t have to look for nothing.
* * * *
In Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.), during the past
three years, directors’ fees and percentages have been
£6362, £14,800, and £14,800, or in all £35,962. As there
are six directors, they take £2OOO a j-ear apiece, which is
fairly good remuneration for men who sit on up to a
dozen different boards, and are mostly useless to put p
mine on its feet. With regard to the Oroya-Black Rangp
the same crew has allotted itself £l3OO a year fees, and
2i ptr cent, of net profits ! So they milk the one cow
twice.

(LIMITED)
SPRING ST.. SYDNEY
Kalgoorlie shows some fine results with high-
priced labor. At South Kalgurli mining costs are down
to 4s. 6d., and for treatment 3s. 2d. ; total, is. 3d. per ton
of ore. Yet the total costs are 225. per ton ; so labor
costs are veiy little over one-third of the total. Part or
the other two-thirds is London expenses; and there is
room for a cut there.
» * * *
North End, Kalgoorlie, after an ignominious
and checkered career, has come to the end of its tether,
and co. is to be reconstructed The form of reconstruc-
tion is peculiar. In the old co. 190,000 shares were
issued ; against these 95,000 are to be given, fully paid.
Then holders of old scrip are offered 25,000 contributing,
with 2s. called up, or 25 for each 190 held. This would
bring up the capital to 120,000 shares. But subscribers
of these contributing shares are offered a bonus of six
paids for every contributing allotted, a total of 150,000,
thus bringing the capital of the new co. to 270,000 shares.
The co. has been muddled, and no balance-sheet is avail-
able. The registered office is noiv being transferred to
Perth ; but, unless better management is introduced, the
concern is hopeless. 00. is said to have produced £9OOO
worth of gold, but there is next to none in sight now.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia South, Leonora (W.A.), has
been closed down for three months, pending the issue of
10,000 reserve shares. The mine was fully equipped about
a year ago, since when the main shaft has been sunk to
320 ft., and a lot of opening up done. There are two lodes
on the property, which immediately adjoins the big
mine; but, although rich and promising at times, they
have proved patchy and disappointing. Co. is in 50,000
shares, of which 24,000 were issued fully paid, and 16,000
cons, are now fully paid to 10s. It is proposed to issue
the 10,000 reserve "shares to try and raise another £5OOO
working capital. Mine may yet prove payable, but its
proximity to the Gwalia means nothing, as that co. holds
611 acres, which is enough to take in everything.
* ■» « «
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) should be in full
sw’ing on its new mine, the Goodenough, before the end
of the month. Developments are being pushed on
at 100, 180 and 280 ft.
* » * *
Augusta, Laverton (W.A.), during June
crushed 350 tons for 5650 z. Present holders bought it for
£2OO in Nov., 1904, since when they have crushed 2550
tons for 48150 z. This mine was owned by the Menzies
Golden Rhine, and was abandoned as worked out after
producing some very rich returns above 300 ft. The
ground then became very hard, and, without further
test, mine and plant were auctioned at rubbish prices.
Tailings are still untreated ; yet profits to date are in the
neighborhood of £12,000, most of which has been spent in
fully equipping the mine. An adjoining property, the
British Flag, once held by' Dr Laver, and sold by him to
a Bull co., is again being run by the doctor. Sixteen
tons from the dump averaged 7£oz. per ton, and a
further parcel of 14 tons from the mine has returned
68Joz. What sort of managers were employed by Bull
eight to ten years ago ?
* * * »
Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), can still
make a fair show on paper. True, its last half-year saw
it drift £4OO to the bad; but more than that was chargeable
to the drought in the early months. Workings show
nothing from which much can be expected ; but there are
more than expenses in view, and the co. has over £BOOO
up its sleeve. On a paid capital of £15,405, the Princess
has divided £116,000. But most of it was in ’O2 and ’O3 ;
the last three years have given only £12,000.
* * * *
The B.H. Prop, is rushing to increase its metal
output before the markets break. Preparations are
being made to work under the old fire-area in block 11;
and, among other things, the re-treatment plant at the
mill is being doubled, making its capacity 3000 tons a
week.
* * * *
The B.H. Proprietary’s ore body, though richer
in lead at 1200 ft. than at the upper levels, has pinched
considerably, and is now only about two sets wide. Still,
as a rule, these ore bodies are not continuous, but run
like a string of sausages.
-ft- * * *
The B.H. Central (Sulphide Corporation) is
making a big effort to recover itself. The main shaft is
being rushed down to 1000 ft., the intention being to have
everything triin and ready this side of Christmas. The
contractors are getting between £ll and £l2 a foot, and
work only six-hour shifts.
* # * *
B.H. Junction North shaft collapse obligingly
confined itself to a narrow strip at about 570 ft. The tim-
bers there are like matchwood, and one side has gone right
in and is leaning against the ocher. There is a cavity on
the broken side ; it is intended to fill this with mullock
and pack tight, and then sink through the broken portion
—a dangerous job.
* » * *
Opinion is gaining ground at Broken Hill that
Junction North, broken shaft and all, is too valuable to
be thrown in at the figure offered by the North
amalgamators. North’s northern ore body isn’t improv-
ing in size as it goes down ; and its southern and richer
body goes into the Junction North. Besides this,
the Junction North may possibly get the rich British
shoot in its lower levels.
* * * *
There seems little connection between B.H.
Junction mine developments and the recent jump in its
shares. A rumor was set going that it had sold its tail-
ings dumps. Directors wish they had. The big dump
(160,000 tons) is, however, reckoned too poor as yet.
Still there is a smaller dump of better stuff, and "the
slime dump holds about 40,000 tons. Then there are
8000 tons of zinc tailings, the residue from the treatment
plant, assaying about 18 p.c., and these last may be worth
as much as 6s. a ton.
* * * *
B.H. Block 10’s new winding engine is giving a
deal of trouble. This is one reason for present shortages
in mill output.
* * * *
The Consolidated, Broken Hill, was, in early
Barrier days, looked upon as about the best mine there-
abouts, bar the Proprietary. Zeb. Lane was in it, and
the Proprietary vainly offered him a good billet and some-
thingelse to letit in. Presently therewasa jumping case
and a Supreme Court action, and in the middle of it all the
bottom fell out of the boom, and nobody wanted the
Consolidated. Three years ago somebody else took up
the abandoned mine on the strength of a few
carbonate specimens, and the Consolidated got a
new lease of life. Men were put on bagging ore ; they
got it from an outcrop, and when that pinched out (which
didn’t take long) they bagged most of the stones for half-
a-mile .round ; and the new owner got a bill instead of a
cheque’ from the smelter. This led to the Consolidated
being again regarded coldly, until somebody else came in
with a barrow-load of trouble. Litigation started 12 months
ago, and has continued ever since. In the end all that hap-
pened was an order that the lease be thrown open on J uly 4
at 2 p.m. It was expected that the two opposing: parties
would be there to peg'. Only one party turned out, how-
ever, but it meant business. There were picktd pugilists
among' the crowd of 10, for it was expected there would be
a pitched battle at each peg'. The thing was done in
style. A referee—perfectly unbiassed—was appointed.
He gave the word to a youth with a gun, and as the
echoes of the explosion tumbled about amidst the sur-
rounding hills, they mingled with the sound of scraping
shovels and hammered pigs, and thus the “peggingout ”
was dramatically accomplished. A racing pony had been
provided to post in four miles to the courthouse and
lodge the application ; hut as there was no opponent, the
expected race was as big a disappointment as the hoped-
for fight. And now that the solitary applicant has got
the lease, he is probably w ondering why on earth he went
to all this trouble to get it.
•* * * *
Every little metal boom wakes the ghosts of
concerns that most people thought were decently buried
long ago. Back in ’99, or earlier, the Colonial Copper
Corporation was formed in London. It was just the sort
of poor little creature that the magnificence of the
name would suggest; and after wandering rather aim-
lessly over its 240 acres at Cow Flat (N.S.YV.) for not
quite two years, it gave in for want of breath. It just
had enough left to keep the office open in London, and it
has kept it open for five years. Now it is suggested that
the thing might be wound up, on the off-chance of some-
one being found who wants a copper show at Cow Flat.
But there isn’t much hope of a deal. Another English
co., the Lyndhurst, acquired the old Summer Hill near
there some time ago, and in 1903 spent money on it; but
the ore bodies were small, and tfie distance was great,
and the ore wasn’t what the Lyndhurst people wanted ;
and anybody who wants a mine at Cow Flat or there-
abouts can have that one also very cheap.
jp jp * #
George Lansell had a clear idea as to the value
of a mine. He also had an excellent knowledge of the
general public as gamblers. Hence, while in his will he
directed that all his real estate or residuary estate
should be sold, certain mines are to begot rid of by means
of a lottery, or lotteries, such as “ Tattereall’s,” to be run
in any country where lotteries are legal. Floating mines
is hard work, but raffling them would be easy. The
winners would have to shoulder the heavy work of flota-
tion. Think of some poor devil of a city clerk winning
the big Blue battery or the 180 mine. What an awful
joke to perpetrate by will 1
As expected, widow ,T. A. Wallace’s Stagorn
barge, at Allan’s Flat (Vic.), has closed down. Another
barge of hers at liow'd.v Flat has been let on tribute to some
Yackandandah people. Between his barge and bucket
dredge at the junction of Twist and Yackandandah
Creeks, Donald Fletcher has started a sluicing claim.
Years ago a reef was found there in an old pump shaft.
Thousands would be glad to see Fletcher, with his luck
and his pluck,turn fora whilefrom the nozzle and buckets
to the stamper.

The Bulletin, the other day, got a note from a
man who seems to have a history. Part of the note is an
extract from the Melbourne Morning Herald of June 9,
1851, reporting that “ YVilliam Aberdeen, a licensed
haw'ker, on Thursday last camped near some ranges at
the other side of Dr. Ronald’s run, in the direction of the
Sydney-road, and picked up some grains of a shining sub-
stance, which he has since ascertained was gold.” That
was about the start of Castlemaine. This Bullbtin’s note
comes from the same YVilliam Aberdeen, who has stuck
to the district for 56 years. “The first dredge ever
started in Castlemaine district was on my ground ; there
are 30 round here now.” The ancient battler, who is now
87, lately carried out arrangements to set two more
dredges going, and reckons there is plenty more gold
waiting thereabouts.

“R. 8.” on a field which even cautious Kidston
has decided ought to have railway connection straight
away :—“ Re Cloneurry (Q.). The work of the YVillcoeks-
cwm-Government parties (B. 7/7/’O6) does not represent
the efforts of the field to give itself ‘a fair run.’ The
Willcocks syndicate borrowed £2500 for 1 deep sinking ’;
and its deepest sink is little over 100 ft. Further, some of
the sinking is on the wrong side of the lodes. Meantime
Mt. Elliott prospecting shaft is down 180 ft., still in
oxidised ores, but sinking as hard as it can go. The ore
body is a bedded deposit in slate —showing on the surface
as silicious iron impregnated with glance, malachite, and
cuprite. The Hampden lode, alsoabeddeddeposit, has been
pig-rooted by tributors and pierced by shallow shafts and
costeens over a length of half a mile. Near surface
the copper occurs as seams of glance and cuprite in a
soft kaolinsed slate ; the deepest shaft (135 ft.) is full of
water, but the dump shows that the sink has reached the
sulphides, and A. E. Savage is said to have bulked the
sulphide values at 7’5 per cent, copper. Hampden is
a very big deposit, up to 200 ft. wide on surface; big
values and at) ideal smelting ore. C. S. Smith, one time
of the Vesuvius, is the new manager. Four new shafts are
now sinking, and should do something soon.
“The Cloneurry field is as big as that first known
as Cooigardie. There are big copper shows at Coolullah,
100 miles north of Cloneurry township : at YVest Leich-
hardt, 100 miles west; at Hampden and Mt. Elliott, re-
spectively 50 and 70 miles south ; and at Duchess and
Mt. Hope, 100 miles south-west. Between are the Duck
Creek and Malbon shows, the great stretch of country
known as the Oorella, and all the 30-year-old freeholds, in-
cluding the Argylla, which seems to be one of the best
of the bunch, and has never had a tap done on it by the
owners. Certain unauthorised persons have opened big
seams of red oxide at the western foot of the outcrop on
one of these freeholds, and have napped it ready for bag-
ging. Recently the owners of other freeholds wrote the
Government asking that the undeveloped ore should be
protected against possible thieves; but this was too
much, and Private Enterprise was advised to guard its
own property.
“Reported sale of the Duchess for £15,000 is
actual. Option in the first place was for three months
with £lO9O deposit, and half the time had gone before the
optioners’ manager arrived to develop. A month’s exten-
sion was granted on payment of a further £1000; and since
then (about 14th or 15th June) the balance of £13,000 has
been paid. YVhen I saw the show there were two shafts
sunk on the lode, 200 ft. from each other, and each about
60ft. deep. Midway between the shafts was an open cut,
60ft. long, Bft. wide, 10ft. deep, showing high-grade ore
wherever ground had been broken. At the bottom of
No. 2 shaft there was 2ft. 9in. of ore, probably worth 30 per
cent. There was no ore in the sink in No- 1 shaft, but it
was showing in the western end of the shaft, and drillings
showed it to be the same width and value as No. 2.
Alleged now that the lode has widened as the shafts
deepened, and that three other shafts sunk since I left
prove the shoot (so far as shafts prove the lateral con-
tinuity of a shoot) to be 600 ft. long. But that is not my
story.
“Near the Duchess is a line known as the I van-
hoe —apparently big and certainly continuous, and
showing good ore at intervals. This and the Pilgrim line
to the south were optioned by Moffatt and Linedale, who
also have an option over the YY’allaroo, YVee Macgregor,
Central, and YY’aratah, 10 miles south of Corella Springs.
The YVee Macgregor and Central are big and rich pro-
positions superficially ; but not a tap has ever been done
on them. The development syndicate which has taken
over the lot will get a good run for its money.
“The Orr-Schlapp syndicate has a good pros-
pect in the Mt. Hope shows. Very big quartz out-
crops, 40ft. high, and lialf-a-mile or so long. Eastern
side of these outcrops opened by prospectors (who were
all stockmen once, and now sneer bitterly at bullock)
yielded fine bunchy makes of red oxide and native copper
—bagging quality with a little dressing. The optioners
are sinking prospecting shafts in country, from which to
crosscut both outcrops.
“Commissioner Thallon says he can build the
Richmond-Cloncurry line at the rate of three miles a
week, which rate would send the loco, into Scotty YVere’s
bar within 60 weeks. Sleeper supply seems to be the
only thing likely to prevent the fulfilment of this esti-
mate. For 170 miles there is practically no grading to he
done, and only one bridge to build —that at the Gilliatt,
and unimportant, anyhow.”
* * * *
Peak Downs copper mine (Central Q.), now
taken in hand by a Brisbane co., has an interesting
history. Discovered by a wood-cutter, in the early ’,6o’s,
it was floated in Sydney into a £lOO,OOO co., unlimited
liability. One-third was subscribed capital ; rest went to
vendors. Capital being exhausted, there was a further
issue of shares. Mine was apparently developed down
to 180 ft. Smelting started 1864. in 1866 half-a-
dozen furnaces were going. Blister copper was pro-
dueed, teams took it to Rockhampton, over 250 miles dis-
tant ; then it was shipped for realisation. Dividends
amounted to £215,250. in the best .rear £lOO,OOO was
paid. But that £lOO,OOO was paid out of advances against
copper shipments. Transport was then slow, copper
slumped (the metal is still capable of that), shipments did
not realise advances, and the co. found itself heavily in
debt to its banker. It paid off the liability by ripping out
all the rich stuff; meantime exploration stopped, and
naturally liquidation followed. Still £63,000 was re-
turned to shareholders ; the mine was practically aban-
doned, being sold for what it would fetch. Then a small
co. was formed to take it on, and the small co., facing a
gutted mine, burst. All of this points to the Peak Downs
as a proposition of some possibilities ; and, now that the
railway is practically at its door, developments should
be worth watching.
If the records of Peak Downs are moderately
reliable, it is evident either that even 40 years ago there
were some boodling mining men and metallurgists about
who knew their business, or that the property was a
fabulously rich one and easily stood some knocking about.
If the copper market keeps up, the Brisbane co. which
now has the property—The Peak Downs Freehold Copper
and Coal Co., Ltd. —will probably be able to decide
which was the case. How much more it will be
able to do, one cannot say at present; but the
probabilities are that, unless it is very spry in
opening up, it will discover, just as a slump sets in, that
the old original co. left it only a lot of dead work and poor
ore. It may, however, be otherwise (a few old copper
shows are otherwise), and for this reason : J. T. Coates,
the new manager, has discovered that there are some good
values in the furnace-bottoms of the old smelters. He re-
ports that there are from 50 to 100 tons of bricks replaced
by matte, going as high as 27 per cent, copper, with
assays yet to come for precious metals. If in the days of
no railway the old co. was capable of leaving this behind,
question arises whether some ore was not also left behind ?
The thing looks attractive, and were the proposition in
hustling, experienced hands, would not be a bad gamble ;
but controlled by mournful Brisbane, it will probably
arrive too late. Coates, the manager, was one of mine-
manager Seale’s Mt. Morgan subordinates. All circs,
considered, his task is not light.

Central Queensland is feeling better. On top
of the good seasons in the West, mild booms are being
developed in copper, and Glassford Creek, Mt.
Hector, and Mt. Cannindah are used as an ex-
cuse to bring in a number of wildcat absurdities.
More money is reported to be available for exploit-
ing the coal deposits, and Willeoeks is said to be
taking a fist in this. (At latest advices Willeoeks was in
the North, seeing about putting the diamond drills
through Mt. Garnet. Nothing seems to come of this as a
zinc proposition, by the way.) The Orient Co. is reported
to have purchased wharfage sites at Gladstone, while the
P. and O. Co. is investigating Rockhampton in the inter-
ests of its cargo steamers. That Gladstone is a neglected
port is mainly the fault of Gladstone. It stupidly allowed
the Glassford Creek traffic to go to Miriam Vale, whence
Thallon’s railway takes the lot to Brisbane for oversea
shipment there. Said Glassford Creek traffic is for Glass-
ford Creek Co. not a very wholesome thing, and before it
or Mt. Hector or Mt. Cannindah can develop into big
things, a branch railway must go down.
•» * * •
The harnessing of the Barron Falls, N.Q., is
again before the State Government. An Australian
company is negotiating for the right. Kidston says that
the public will be well safeguarded according to the offer
under consideration. The scenic features of the attractive
spot will not in any way be affected. The proposal is to
sink a deep shaft at the top of the falls, and the water
tumbling on to a fan at the bottom will drive machinery
for the generation of electric power. The power will be
cabled to Cairns, where it is proposed to use it for driving
ore-treating machinery on a large scale. Some years ago
the Chillagoe Co. asked for somewhat similar concessions,
but the House didn’t like the way the co. put it.

Announced at the formal meeting of the Kauri
Timber Co. (Melbourne) that the co. would this month
wipe out £36,000 of debentures instead of the required
£13,700. Thereupon it is mentioned that “ the position
of the co. has much improved of late. The co. has paid
£23,400, or 4 per cent., regularly for some years, and it is
evident that the outlook is now favorable.” Quite true
that 4 per cent, has been paid ; but in order to do that
the net visible deficit has been increased from £171,800 in
1903 to £189,145 in 1905—that is, after writing off such
amounts from forests, etc., as the directors think proper.
Last year the co. made £23,065 ; the directors then wrote
off for depreciation £lO,OOO, leaving only £13,065 net out
of which to pay the dividend (£23,400). Either the
amount written off by the directors was ridiculously
excessive, or half of the 4 per cent, was paid not out of
profits but out of assets.
D. G. McFie, the manager of the Roper River
(N.T.) Concessions Syndicate, is a Scotchman with great
faith in the future of Australia. The syndicate has
secured a tract of four million acres of the Territory,
having a frontage of 50 miles to the Gulf, and is to settle
a large number of Scotch farmers up there. Rubber is
to be cultivated extensively. A Melbourne syndicate
has been given a right to carry on prospecting over the
whole of this enormous area. The manager of the colony
is to be Mr. Alfred Giles, w ell-known in pastoral circles.
Although the land is to be put under the control of
Scotch farmers, Italians are to supply the labor. Apart
from the wisdom of the S.A. Government allowing so
much country to pass into the hands of a syndicate,
the experiment will be of interest as showing w'hether
white men cannot live and work hard in the Territory.
McFie says that there is plenty of money available in
England and Scotland for enterprises in Australia which
are not over-capitalised, and he ought to be a judge.
“ The comments adverse to Australian legislation in Eng-
land,” he says, “ are made chiefly by Australians of certain
political brands, who sit in the clubs and say that Aus-
tralia is going to the devil. The question of greatest
interest to England is to know whether the States are
paying their way or not.”:

Apparently the Melbourne tram monopoly be-
gins to hear something coming. It lately reduced two more
lines—Richmond and Victoria-street—to 2d. fares for the
whole distance. But, presumably with a view to averring
that there w'as no increased traffic through the reduc-
tion, it quite omitted to advertise the change, and thus
lost some kudos. But a lot of representative citizens
kept on paying the same old 3d. till they learnt that
others had been stumping up only 2d. for a fortnight or
so ; then they wrote to the papers in a great rage and
anathematised the company heavily; so the octopus got
the publicity without the advertisement.
# * * *
“Quidam”: Will somebody kindly invent a
cheaper process for handling tailings for cyaniding—for
small plants? Anything over 4s. sand (excepting refrac-
tory and untreatable tack) is rapidly becoming unobtain-
able. And the working of small heaps of 3s. or 3s. 6d.
stuff on a profitable basis, though not absolutely impos-
sible, is nearly a fine art. So far, at any rate on small
heaps, where "costly systems of tramlines and trucks are
impossible, the prosaic horse and dray appears to be the
cheapest method of shifting tailings ; but the irreducible
minimum value below’ which this system cannot go is
rapidly being arrived at. The development of some
system of handling which would save even 3d. a ton over
the horse and dray, and at the same time be cheap enough
to instal on small plants, would bring into profitable use
an enormous number of sand heaps which are at present
just too poor to be of any service to anyone. When
handling, chemicals, supervision, smelting, and at any
rate part of initial cost of plant have to be supported by
(say) 3s. a ton stuff, it is obvious that there is not much
money to burn.

“ H.” : While there has been .much gaudy adver-
tisement about the progress of the Mount Sienna pigment
mine, near Adelaide, the Australians who support the
industry had better study the mystery of the deposits at
Black Flats (Vic.), only ten miles from Melbourne. The
Black Flats deposits comprise, in an area of a few hun-
dred vards, pigments of every color in commercial use.
Further, they mix freely with oil, and experiments prove
that the paint manufactured from them is as durable and
effective as the best imported. There are in the Vic.
Mines Department a numher of planks painted three
years ago with this material, and not the most delicate
tint shows the slightest sign .of fading. In the open air
the Black Flats paint proved its wearing qualities, and it
was estimated that a marketable article could be turned
out much cheaper than the imported . paints. If ever
there seemed to be a good proposition for Australian
capital and energy it was the Black Flats pigment
deposits ; yet to-day it is impossible to buy a pound of
the paint in the market.
Australia doesn’t seem to realise what money
there is in Pig. Minister Swinburne pointed out to Vic-
torian farmers the other day that the Yankee receives
£25,000,000 a a ear for exported Pig (at least, it’s mostly
pig, though some of it is wood, and some of it is man),
and the Dane £5,500,000. Britain eats £21,000,000 worth
of bacon in a year, and Canada helps to supply it by send-
ing away 1,250,000 cwt. of hog and sow annually. Don’t
despise Pig. #
The proposal of S. J. Mitchell, who represents
the Northern Territory in S.A. Parliament, to enforce the
carrying out of labor'conditions on N.T. mining claims
by European labor, where obtainable, is finding plenty of
backing among the white men on the spot. West Arm
and Horseshoe tinfield claim-holders and miners have
strengthened Mitchell’s hands by sending along forceful
resolutions passed at local meetings. For 30 years S.
Australia has let the yellow pig-rooter have the run of
the fields; and the N.T. is no forrarder. In fact, it is
the most backward part of Australia. If yellow labor
that is supposed to be cheap, but isn't, cannot prove its
virtues in 30 years, it seems a fair thing to assume that it
hasn’t any virtues to prove, and is simply hanging on, on
the strength of its vices.

Another factor in reducing the mining costs,
especially in large mines, is the general use of “Little
Wonder” Hand Hammer and “One Man” Compressed
Air Drills. Broken Hill mines use them extensively. The
managers favor these drills for their great strength and
simplicity having only one working part; also for
economy in air consumption as they require only about
25ft. free air per minute. Miners prefer them, as work is
made much easier; for tributors, “Wonder” drills are
the means of bringing in larger returns. Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, of Melbourne and Sydney, have de-
scriptive catalogues for those interested, and attention
is drawn to an exceedingly simple and ingenious paten*
water attachment for allaying dust.*

Electric haulage is one of the best paying in-
vestments for every mine in which the wash-dirt has to be
hauled from a greater distance than 2000 feet. The actual
saving effected at the Berry Consols Extended Mine,
Allendale, amounts to £3O per week at a distance of 3000
feet; at the Great Southern Consols Mine, Itutherglen, it
runs into £45 per week, at a distance of 5000 feet; in both
instances the necessary plant which was installed to
replace the manual labor has cost but £l2OO. Experience
has proved that at a distance of 4000 feet electric haulage
cost a quarter of that of horse traction and a tenth of hand
trucking. The Charlotte Plains and New Havillah Mines
at Moolort are the latest to adopt electric haulage, and
have again proved it to be highly satisfactory. The favor-
able results obtained with these installations are un-
doubtedly due in a great measure to the reliable and
smooth operation of the electric apparatus, especially the
locomotives adopted, which were all supplied and erected
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, Limited, corner
of Hanna and Kavanagh-streets, South Melbourne, agents
for Ganz and Company, Budapest, Hungary. This firm has
devoted special attention to mine haulage problems, and
during their 14 years’ experience have brought up their
mine locomotives and other haulage apparatus to the
highest degree of perfection.* [2.]

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Meplian Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and everv discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

The New Havillah Gold Mining Company has
just installed a high lift pump to make all necessary pro-
vision against any increase in the volume of water now
that they are opening up large areas of wash dirt. This
installation is in addition to the large three throw pump
which has given such high efficiency and such constant
service since its inception. The new pump is of the high
lift turbine tvpe, electrically driven, and is capable of
lifting 2,016,000 gallons of water in 24 hours against a
total head of 350 feet. The mechanical efficiency of the
pump is guaranteed at 71-5 percent, and all repairs and
replacements apart from undue wear are likewise
guaranteed for twelve months. The pump has four
runners and four vanes made from best bronze and the
shaft is attached by means of a flexible coupling to a
Ganz three phase motor 440 volts pressure, 60 cycles
capable of giving 207 h.p. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have executed this con-
tract. This Company has had the manufacture of most
of the big volume deep alluvial lead pumping schemes in
Victoria and has met in each with unqualified success.* [a]

* * * *
The Zinc Corporation has taken careful advice
in connection with the large plant required at Broken
Hill, and has ordered Babcock and Wilcox water-tube
boilers with superheaters and mechanical stokers. This
order has been followed by the great firm of mining
engineers, Messrs. Bewick, Moreing and Co., placing
orders with the same boiler company, in connection with
the South Bloeks, also at the Hill. These instances
further prove the assertion that, wherever economy in
fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. This company stock boilers at “Haymarket,”
Sydney.*

“ Janor ” : Among recent suicides in Vic.,
that of a gander is perhaps worth notice. A
neighbor for long kept a pair of geese. Recently
a vagrant dog attacked the goose in the river
and, despite a valiant defence by its better half’
kUied it. 1 rom that time the gander refused
food though tempted with all sorts of dainty
morsels, and died in a fortnight.

“Kangaroo”: Have now found out why
it is regarded as unlucky to go under a ladder
baw a perspiring young female try to rush under
a step-ladder, on which a man was standing-
attending to an arc-lamp. She did not notice the
rope across, and the crash resembled the noise of
a Frisco quake. The girl, the man, and the
ladder seemed to be practising jiu-jitsu amidst
the remnants of the arc-lamp, assisted by two
yelping curs which pranced round. The language
of the electrician was strong enough to run all the
lights of the city.

“L.G.J.” : In the ’7o’s, at M ‘Donald and
Smith’s Ban Ban station, near Maryborough (Q.),
Pero, a black boy, was one morning speared by a
1 raser Island native, whose lubra he had taken
away when in Maryborough with the wool teams.
.The spear was driven into the back with such
force that 2ft. of the head projected from the pit
of the stomach, and, after breaking off this part,
the manager with difficulty pulled the remainder
out of the back. The wound was a ghastly one •
and, although the bleeding was stopped by a
plentiful application of kerosene, the doctor, when
he arrived, pronounced the case hopeless. The
tnbal blacks of the Burnett, however, removed
the sufferer to their camp, and, after about six
months of their treatment, Pero was able to visit
the station, and in another four months was as
well as ever. Wonderful is the recuperative
power of our black brother Bill.
“ Werrimoo ” : Re “ Coo-ee ” (8., 5/7/’O6).
lhe word comes from the aborigines, who use it,
with slight variations, in nearly all parts
of uncivilised Australia. In a report on
the discovery and exploration of the
Uawkesbury in 1789, by Captain Hunter,
an officer under Governor Phillip, this pas-
sage occurs r In the woods we fre-
quently saw fires, and sometimes heard
the natives; in the afternoon we saw a
considerable number of people in the
woods, with many fires in different places.
v\ e called to them in their own manner
by frequently repeating the word * co-wee. ’’
which signifies ‘come here.’ 55 Some old
timers assert that, as used by the blacks,
it was imitative of the call-howl of the
dingo; while others say it w T as an imita-
tion of the far-sounding note of the wonga
pigeon. There is certainly no call known
in the bush, apart from the call of the
wonga and the dingo’s howl, that can equal
the penetrative power of the coo-ee. In
some parts, as South Australia, the “ ee ”
was not heard, the call sounding like coo-
°°n -Phonetically speaking, coo-ee is the
call of the bushwoman; the male notes
ar ® r? 10 , 1^ 6 lilce ca -a-w-whey—the first
syllable lingering and comparatively low;
the second loud, sharp, and abrupt—a
deep-liquid sound formed in the throat and
forcibly ejected—which is the secret of its
far-reaching quality. Perhaps the first
time it was heard Dowm Under was when
Mrs. Wheeler, honeymooning from this
country, lost her Bill near London Bridge.
Other means failing her, she put her
hands trumpet-like to her mouth, and
l* ogtown with a ringing coo-ee.
It pretty nearly got her ip for riotous behaviour
but it brought Bill-

The destruction of native birds is still
going on in Maoriland, the brown men being
mainly responsible. Kiwi feathers make fine
mats, which command high prices, and greed of
gold is responsible for the shooting of a great
many of these birds in the South Island. In the
•Worth, the tui is the great object of attention
and at one place alone (Parihaka, where the
decayed prophet Te Whiti hangs out), over 3000
tins are said to have been recently slain and
put down in fat, according to Maori custom. The
Government talks of suppressing the slaughter, if
possible.

Janor Have just shut down on a
prolonged and unsuccessful attempt
to tame a platypus. Captured the
joker with the bill before he was
weaned, in a shallow shaft hidden in
the creek bank. There was some
water in the shaft, which I baled with
prospecting dish, the mole making
desperate attempts to bore through
the bottom meanwhile. Soon as the
hole was dry he was my meat, and I
carried him home in the dish covered
with a flake of slate. Close to my
ancestral hall I excavated him a
home in the creek bank, and paled
and roofed it snugly. He’d nothing
to growl about as to the style of
his housing, but he was reai wild
about other things, and all my efforts
to alter his demeanor failed. I
lavished my best manner on the cuss
(I lectured for a Diorama years back),
but he always had urgent business at
the bottom of his well when I was
around. I gave him the choicest
morsels from the menu, but so diffi-
dent was he that he would only eat
m solitude. So after months of work
that would turn a lion-tamer green
with envy I resign. Not that lam
discouraged or that I’m afraid of the
brute with the fearful name (which
I cannot spell), but because he is
dead.

8.H..D. : I m satisfied ‘ ‘ The Prooshan ”
(B. 28/6/’O6) doesn’t know all the points about
crow shooting. Let him try running towards the
tree the crow is on, starting to run when about
100 yards off. Nine times out of ten he’ll get to
the foot of the tree before the crow remembers
the appointment he made with another bird of
the same feather on the next man’s farm. Learnt
the trick when shooting the wary bird in my
spare time for a Chow doctor. He paid 4d. a
head, and dried the fowl and ground it into
medicine.

FAIRFAX & ROBERTS
The Oldest Jewellery
House in Sydney,
23 Hunter St.
Time was when any Queensland horse or
cattle stealer was safe in the hands of a Roma
jury. One noted reiver was arraigned for lifting
600 head of cattle from a Western station. The
evidence was absolutely clear, and the odds were
■laid that the verdict would be “Guilty” without
the jury leaving the box, When they filed in,
after an hour’s deliberation, with a verdict of
“ Not guilty,” Judge Blakeney snapped out:
“Thank God, gentlemen ! that verdict is yours,
not mine.” Blakeney represented matters to the
Government in so unfavorable a light that Roma
was without a District Court for six years. De-
putation followed petition, and petition succeeded
deputation, and at last the Government
relented, and Judge Paul was instructed
to include Roma in the Western Cir-
cuit. At that time the railway had not
reached Bungil Creek, and the journey was finished
per buggy. The leading inhabitants of Roma met
the night before the Judge’s arrival to celebrate, in
champagne and more plebeian liquids, the moral
victory the town had won, and it was decided to
follow an old English custom by meeting the
Judge a few miles out and escorting him into the
town. When the time arrived for setting out, it
was a very unsober cavalcade of horsemen that
took the road to Blythedale. One man was chosen
as spokesman, because he stood his liquor better
than the rest. When the meeting took place he
gravely saluted, and ended up a welcoming speech
by saying : “ This may be regarded, your Honor,
as Paul’s visit to the Romans.” “Ahem,” drily
responded the Judge. “ Would it not be more ap-
propriate to call it Paul’s visit to the Titans.”
The tight ’uns tried to look amused and yet not
fall off.
* » * *
“ Itan ” : “Nulla” and “Gip” have
lately discussed the growth, in a wild state, of
Cape gooseberries and other fruit. All along the
scrub districts of the East coast, from the Clar-
ence River to Port Douglas, gooseberry bushes
will spring up in a great mass after the first burn-
ing-off. Also the cherry-tomato and small black-
berry bid for space with some success. After a little
cultivation the vigor of these plants seems to
wane. _ The yellow guava is now in great pro-
fusion in many of these districts, especially north
of Rockhampton. About Mackay there are
miles of it to be found, especially clustering
about the unused part of the railway or on aban-
doned land that was once cleared. At the back of
Cairns, where there is a break in the tropical
jungle, the luscious pawpaws and mangoes grow
wild. From a pawpaw tree in full bearing, one
could almost fill a cart. It is extraordinary that this
fruit does not gain in favor in the markets of Sydney
and Melbourne. Reverting to Cape gooseberries,
this fruit is commercially grown in some parts of
southern Queensland. The conserve made from
it is excellent. Once the Agricultural Depart-
ment of Bananaland undertook to market a part
of the crop, and to this day Minister Denham is
known as the member for Cape Gooseberry.

“Edyson”: Bulletin records many in-
stances of the giant labors of
single men in the bush and by
flood and field, and I notice that
the superhuman efforts are often
fruitless. YVhat of the case of
Quibell, a lone settler in the hills
of the coal country in Gippsland ?
Quibell has about 300 acres of ex-
cellent land, he has a fair-sized
orchard, a decent kitchen garden,
a few acres of spuds, and a drove
of goats. If he worked hard on
his holding he could make money,
but he prefers to follow his hobby,
and out of that he doesn’t get a
red cent. Quibell’s indiscretion
is artistic, but it does not even
bring him an audience. Near his
paling hut there stands a superb
white gum, straight, high, and
symmetrical. Many years ago
the settler started making carvings
on this tree, and he has gone on
carving and scorching until the
whole butt is a mass of fantastical
designs, and now the filigree, the
birds, beasts, flowers, and twid-
dling frivolities are running out on
the main limbs. Quibell’s art has
developed as it progressed, and his
material rise represents an increas-
ing artistic elevation. Down below
the designs are not much superior
to the efforts of the aboriginal
.Lindsays and Hops, but higher
up he gets in some excellent effects,
comic and serious, and George Reid
and his dog lend themselves to a
unique design in Australian fauna.
At first Quibell found that the gum
shed his carvings with its annual
change of costume, and he lost years
going back and patching up his job, but
now he knows how to do permanent
work, and that tree is a monument of
misdirected art and industry.
“ Quip ” : I know the Nambucca
(N.S.W.) a good bit, and there are so many
Mackays on the River that they are all nick-
named to distinguish them. Beside the four
Angus Mackays there are Black Bob, Red Bob,
Hong Jim, Uncle “Pot,” “Dooty,” also Baldy
Angus’s Angus and old Angus’s Angus.

“ Caber ” : That Chicago meat scare re-
minds me of one on a smaller scale that struck
some of us a good many years ago. A Jap was
missing from one of the Queensland sugar mills,
and it was given out that he had cleared for parts
unknown. Three days later, when draining off
The treacle from one of the large vats, they found
-the Jap. He had fallen in when it was boiling,
and made no remark about it. For seven years I
avoided treacle and scorned golden syrup. We
knew the economical habits of the sugar mills of
the period.
“ T.O’Fipp ” : Met with a curiosity in the
way of a patchwork bike, ridden by an antique
swagman, who was making a triumphal entry
with his implement into a Gippsland (Vic.)
township. The bike had lost a lump of handle-
bar, which was replaced by a crooked stick. A
number of spokes were conspicuous by their
absence, and those remaining were stayed from
one to another with pieces of wire that had once
belonged to wire-netting. The lop-sided seat was
strengthened with fencing-wire, and the torn
tyre bore patches of moleskin and greenhide. The
rider of the quaint art-object proudly informed
me that he could cover from 16 to 20 miles per
day, but he always got off and walked when he
struck a bit of bad road, as he didn’t believe in
asking too much off a good bike. He also advised
me to back him and his mount for the next
Bairnsdale road-race, when he meant to throw
prudence to the winds and “let ’er fly.”

“J.S.”: It was pay-night at the camp
between the two big cuttings. Pincher Bill had
come home drunk, and Mrs. P.B. started on him
with a piece of sapling, and P. B.’s roars were
awful. The new chum in our camp jumped up.
“My God, she’ll kill the man ! Hadn’t we better
interfere ? ” Nobody seconded the motion, and
the new chum, with a look of contempt, dashed
over and caught Mrs. P.B. by the arms, and
dragged her off P.B. Plainly the new chum knew
how to handle women, for she dropped the sap-
ling and squealed for help—and got it. P.B.
struggled to his feet, grasped the sapling, and
knocked the new chum out first hit. He came-to
half-an-hour later. On Monday, after pay, P.B.
came home from the cutting and found Mrs. P.B.
drunk and no dinner ready. Her squeals were
awful. “My God, he’ll kill her ! ” said one of the
“ batchers ” to the new chum. “ Hadn’t we better
interfere ? ” The new chum went on frying his
steak.
“Rio” : Meta Finn last week who had
contracted hydatids in Queensland, and endured
eight months’ suffering therefrom. He visited
six hospitals for treatment, and tried many
doctors, but continually got worse. A doctor at
Brisbane urged an operation, but the old chap
recommended him to try his skill at the slaughter-
house down the river. Finally he called on a
medico who had a noble thirst, and he said :
“Take no more food until I tell you. Drink a
bottle of good port wine, half a bottle of brandy,
and a few drops of turps every day. Take also
one dose only of the medicine I prescribe, and if
the hydatids don’t die something else will.”
After five days the hydatids were poisoned, and
there was just enough of Finn left to throw a
shadow. But he survived. A wonderful race are
these Finns.
“ THE LITTLE WET NURSE.”
Brisbane has got its floating island also.
The phenomenon was first noticed during the
flood of 1893, when a swamp at Chelmer kept its
trees and vegetation gaily on the surface. On
investigation it was found that a great mass of
vegetation was floating. Throughout the Great
Drought this swamp proved to be a perfect God-
send to local stockowners, as it maintained a
plentiful supply of pure water —the evaporation
of which was checked by the floating earth and
vegetation.

In a M.L. court the other day, two young
fellows were charged with stealing three calf-
skins. The “cops” reckoned they had a copper-
lined case from the start, and the defending
lawyers thought that their best chance was to rely
on the good characters of their clients and the
tender mercy of the First Offenders Act. They
had just got a strain on the quality of mercy when
the magistrate stopped them and began to ask
some questions. He learned that the prosecutor
was a farmer. That the accused were employed
by him to keep his land clear of rabbits. That he
paid them no wages, but gave them a penny
halfpenny a pair for skins, and their keep.
He kept the skins and the Sawbath day. He
gave them their grub—mostly boiled rabbit.
They helped to milk the cows night and morning
to show they were not afraid of work. Then the
beak started to calculate a bit while the crowd
below listened to the noises caused by the growth
of its whiskers. According to the court’s figuring
the accused were afoot from 5 a.m. till 9 p.m.
seven days a week, and the sum paid them for
rabbit skins averaged 2s. 6d. per week. Also
they got their boiled rabbit for meals. Then he
fined them £1 each, which was too much, though
it was a woeful disappointment to some strong
believers in Propputty who thought three months
was the minimum, In such cases there should be
some provision whereby the Law can take out the
fine in rabbits.
* * * *
“G.M.”: “ W.P.B.’s” suggestion (8..
21/6/’OG) that cats prefer their own music to any
other requires a little elucidation. Thomas does
not yowl because he likes music—his yowling is
merely the utterance of angry passions. Take the
common triangular love situation —a lady and two
Thomases. The cat is a natural coward, and will
not fight till he is sufficiently worked up. Thus,
each Thomas commences to tell the other he-eat
that his presence is unbearable; that the other he-
eat wasn’t invited ; that he is himself no inter-
loper, and knew Flossie first; and that if t’other
does not leave he will rip the coat off him and
strew the place with his whiskers. Then, when
one or the other is sufficiently maddened by his
own yells and the recital of his wrongs, he wades
in suddenly and without warning to get in a nasty
one under the belt. Music is the last thing he is
thinking of. He shouts at himself to keep him-
self from running away.
MILKIN’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a sunburned farmer who was leaning on a plough,
And talking to a traveller as he mopped his shining
brow ;
“ Can you milk?” the farmer queried, and the weary one
replied—
“ Never left a gill in any cow I ever sat beside.”
The farmer stood and pondered and he said, “ Well,
I might find
Some sort uv light employment fer a feller to me mind ;
While me son an’ me are ploughin’ (we must do it while
we can)
Some jobs maybe ’ll crop up fer a useful, all-round man.
‘ There’s some snaggin’ at the water-’ole —it’s time that
that was done—
And the missus wants wire-nettin’ fixed around ’er
poultry run;
There’ some cleanin’ round the stables, and there’s
milkin’, did I say ? ”
“ Yes, you spoke about the milkin’,” said the traveller ;
“ fire away.”
“ You can fill to-morrer mornin,” said the farmer, sure
and slow,
“ With a drain I’ve thoughter diggin’ fer this fifteen
month or so ;
There’s the pigs’ feed to be carried, there’s the ’orses to
attend,
And a heap of broken blacksmith’s tools that’s waitin’ fer
ter mend.
“There’s some fencin’ the contractor couldn’t finish,
bein’ sick —•
You’ll find ’is line of post-’oles over yonder by the crick ;
And towards evenin’ Sis an’ mother’ull be ready with the
cows ”
“ Aye, you menshuned there’d be milkin’,” said the man,
with gloomy brows.
“ The man I want,” the farmer said, “won’t loaf about
and yarn ;
You can ’ave the last bloke’s quarters in the corner uv the
barn ;
If your time should ’ang too ’eavy (when the milkin’ is
got through)
I’ve some old tarpaulin covers you could fix as good as noo.
“ I’ll wake you in the mornin’,” said the farmer, “ with
the boy ;
It’s gen’lly turn-out-early with the men that I employ.
And you’ll start the day with milkin’—have I spoke uv
that as yet ? ”
“ I believe I ’eard a w’isper,” said the traveller in a sweat.
“ Well, things is pretty quiet,” said the farmer. “ Seems
to me
Twelve bob a week ud pay you, if you git your tucker
free.
Th'ere’ll be fencin’, diggin’, mendin’, patchin’ things up
’ere an’ there ”
“ And amusemints, seeh as milkin’,” said the traveller
with a glare.
“ There’ll be Sis and me and Mother,” said the traveller,
growing red,
“ Ull be tuggin’ at them milkers when the sun ain’t outer
bed ;
And when the pale moon’s risin’, all the landscape fer to
kiss,
Sittiu’ achin’ in the cowshed ’ll be Mother, Me, and Sis.
“ When things get kinder lonesome fer the want uv work
to do,
We’ll git up milkin’ parties till the night is ’alf way through.
When the crimson sun is mountin’, or is sinkin’ in the
West,
I’ll be milkin’, ’long uv Mother, just to keep me ’eart at
rest.”
And the traveller muttered curses that were loud, and
likewise deep,
And he left the farmer standing like a man surprised
from sleep.
The farmer watched him plodding o’er the hillside’s
muddy brow —
“ Well, I told ’im there’d be milkin’,” he remarked, and
seized his plough.

JOE’S MISERIES, &c.
We are feeling the position acutely, and we desire to let the public know that we do feel it acutely. We feel that we are being misjudged—not only the
Government and Government supporters, but members on all sides of the House. We feel that we are being misjudged ; that the public mind is inflamed. It
is natural that members should feel acutely the position they are put in, and they feel the unfairness of the position they are made to occupy by a wrong con-
struction being put upon the actions of Parliament and the actions of the Government. — From recent wail by J. H. CARRUTHERS.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
That not-over-mild person, Vic. Premier
Bent, has for private secretary one Mr. Meek.
Judkins, who fusses so much in Melbourne
now, used to teach school at Creswick (Vic.).
Norman Lindsay was one of his pupils.
Jos. Symes, of Freethought fame, was
bequeathed £lOO by a recently demised Murchi-
son (W.A.) admirer. And he needed it.
Words of great men. Crick: “I have
occupied big positions in the past and will do so
again.” W. Nicholas Willis (before Justice
Owen): “It is incumbent on a man before all
things to preserve his honor.” Harry Levien :
“Crick is my friend, so I’ll vote for him. What
the devil do I care what he does ! ”
It appears quite likely that the fifth Minister
in S.A., should.the Council pass the Bill to
authorise his appointment, will be little Roberts,
editor of the Labor Herald.
“Koradgee” Chaplain, who left Brisbane
Courier a year ago to take up the post of Director
of Agriculture in W.A., has resigned, and it is
stated is returning to Bananaland. According to
accounts he did not agree with the experts in the
service.
S.A. University has just appointed its
second native-born professor in Dr. Jethro Brown,
head of the law school. The first native to attain
the distinction was E. C. Stirling.
The new Brighton (Melbourne) electric
tramway had its terminus at poet Gordon’s house.
But the old barn, broken down and weather-
beaten, has been now replaced by a shop, which
is painted a bright-green.
Melbourne attorney Croker, whose nego-
tiation of the new mail service brings him under
light, is a slim pale wisp of a man, dressed always
to the “nines.” He formerly partnered with Gil-
lott.
William Hodges, who recently died at
Norwood (S.A.), aged 84, was the last of the
pioneers who arrived by the brig Rapid in 1836.
Old Australian writer and dramatist, Gar-
net Walcli, spends the evening of his life quietly
among his flowers and books at Surrey Hills
(Melbourne). A daughter runs a prosperous
type-writing business in the city, and types MS.
for her father’s successors.
“H.M.C.”: Be children’s names. How
do the following strike you ? They belong to a
family of twenty-one down Alexandra (Vic.) way :
Gypsy, Caesar, Ruby Pearl, Jasper Bud, Claude
Silverton Silas, New Year Coolgardie, Silas
Nature, and Noel Maximum.
Died, H. M. Chomley, ex-Police Com-
missioner of Victoria, aged 74 years. Like many
other adventurers he came to Australia in the
early fifties, attracted by the gold discoveries.
After delving for a while he joined the police as a
cadet, and was present at the storming of Eureka
Stockade.
FLEMING, MELBOURNE'S GENTLE
ANARCHIST.
Died last week at Bendigo W. D. C.
Denovan, who, as digger, member of Parliament,
town clerk, newspaper editor and stockbroker,
had been connected with the city since 1853.
Denovan was a confirmed spiritualist, and in his
time wrote one or two books on the subject.
George Fowlds, M.H.R., Maoriland, re-
cently trod his native heath in Scotland to join
his brethren in celebrating his father’s hundredth
birthday. The centenarian, a hand-loom weaver,
still works the engine of his youth; for 90 years
he has been working at Farwick, near Kil-
marnock, where he was born.
Premier Price, who is also Education
Minister, told a gathering of Adelaide teachers,
the other day, that when he worked at his trade
as a stone-mason, he always did his best, and
turned a deaf ear to mates who urged that he was
“ s'poiling the job.” Price was an excellent trades-
man, as much of the mason work in the S.A.
House of Assembly testifies.
The doctor who was so nearly drawn into
the Melbourne boot-box tragedy of a decade back
has now got the best practice in a north-eastern
Vic. town. Of the other actors, boy Tod and
the elderly Madame Radalyski have disappeared,
while the masterful, handsome Thekla Regina
Dubberke was last seen at a recent market-
gardeners’ picnic.
Retired from business, after a clearing
sale, old Simon Kozminsky, jeweller, and plate,
coin and curio dealer, whose sign has adorned his
; shop-front, on the corner opposite Melbourne
G.P.0., for more years than most of us want to
remember. Simon knew a curio or a relic when
he saw one, and there were few Brummagem
articles of rertu in his shop. The old shop comes
down to make room for a skyscraper.
