W. Walker, M.L.C, still doing a
bit at law and law-making, was a prac-
tising solicitor four years before N.S.W.
obtained Responsible Government.
Mr. J. B. Turnbull, who died at Mada-
gascar in February last, was well-known at
Newcastle, where he was chief mine-manager for
the A.A. Co. Subsequently he superintended
mining operations at Lithgow and Seaham.
After leaving Australia he settled in South Africa,
and was in Madagascar on business when death
overtook him.
Mr. George Day, who was a Hawkesbury
(N.S.W.) native, born 1820, died last week.
Many years ago Mr. Day went to Albury and
established himself in the flour milling business.
In 1873 was elected for the Hume, continuing in
the representation till 1889. He was given a seat
in the Legislative Council by George Dibbs.
Mr. W. H. Duncan, S. Australian squatter,
who died at sea in May last while returning from
England, left an estate valued for probate pur-
poses at £84,000. The widow gets the real estate
during widowhood. Adelaide Hospital benefits
to the amount of £lOOO, and £5OOO is to be applied
to the erection and maintenance of cottage homes
for aged and infirm widows.
The old cottage of Johnny Fawkner, the
founder of Melbourne, still stands in the front
garden of S. Eardley-Wilmot in Launceston.
Wilmot’s house is built on the site of Fawkner’s
market garden. For years Johnny’s old cabin
was the residence of Wilmot’s gardener, but now
the place is untenanted and in decay. The local
A.N. A. wants to have it furbished up and made a
show place.
S. G. Mee, a compositor em-
ployed on the Queenslander (Bris-
bane), is aged 88, and, with the excep-
tion of a few brief spells, he has been
type-snatching for 74 years. Arriving
in Sydney in 1852, he was employed on
the Empire, and subsequently on the
Bathurst Free Press, Herald, and
Armidale Express. Next he went to
Brisbane, and for 46 years has been
comping for the Brisbane Newspaper
Company, first on the Courier, and
afterwards on the Queenslander.
Heaven knows how many tons of type
he has lifted.
Nicholas Lockyer, recently made
Assistant Comptroller-General of Cus-
toms, and lately I. S.O. is a kind of bene-
ficent despot at the Sydney Customs
House. He came from the Treasury
with a young, alert, and enthusiastic
mind, and sat him on the Collector’s
throne. He ordered a general hustle.
Then he searched the Commonwealth
for available young minds, took some
fresh blood from the schools, gathered
them all in a class in his room, and
taught them how to waylay the fraudu-
lent importer, and stalk the wild and
elusive invoice-fakir to his warehouse.
Then he ordered the youths back to
their rooms, and told them to bustle,
and the 1.5.0. conferred upon him
by King Ned will carry great weight
with the deceitful importers, whose
reverence for his Majesty is only ex-
ceeded by their determination to take
his Majesty down for his legitimate
revenue.
■The neatest thing that has yet
been said about the Kaiser is the re-
mark of H. G. Wells that he “ professes
a violent and grotesque Christianity,
with a ferocious, pro-Teutonic Father and a
negligible Son.”
Sydney City Council has changed the name
of Queen’s Place (near The Bulletin office) to
Dalley-street. Dailey is alleged to have been
born in a house still standing in Lower George-
street, opposite the Commissariat Stores. Henry
Parltes, in 1840, worked in Russell’s foundry,
then in Queen’s Place, and is said to have lived
in one of the quaint cottages in Queen’s Court off
that street.
Died, April 25, at Waco, Texas (U.S.A.),
Robert Hope Atkinson, aged 48 years. He was
the first representative of the Equitable Life
Assurance Society of New York, in Sydney. After
leaving the Equitable he established a remarkable
lottery for the disposal of some scraps of land,
which gamble got him into trouble. On emerging
from “ trouble,” after some months, he went back
to his native land, and what he has been doing
there since, this country knoweth not.
While George Watson, the V.R.C. starter,
was still grappling with illness, both the Argus
and the Age came out with a full account of his
death. Had it not been that old George was in
extremis —he died early on the morning of the
announcement—both papers would probably
have had to explain their breaking away before
the fall of the flag. As it was, no one noticed the
irregularity except the Herald, which,' in the
ordinary course, would have had the privilege of
first announcing the event.
Justice Cussen, of Victorian Supreme Court •
Bench, is a railway engineer. So was the late
Bryan O’Loghlen, who might have been a Supreme
Court Judge if he had kept out of politics, for
which he had no vocation. Ex-Judge Quinlan, of
Victoria (now living in England), kept a hay and
corn store in Melbourne before he went to the
Bar, and after he had gone there he married the
daughter of a wealthy ex-publican, whose house
was in Little Bourke-street.
FATE OF THE FALSE PROPHETS.
Dr. Rentoul gives the lie in England to the Stinking
Fish Party.
And it came to pass that Korah and his
associates were swallowed up.
If King O’Malley is left out of Parliament
at next Commonwealth elections he will, accord-
ing to a project on hand, be invited to float him-
self off as head of a new religion. The financial
success of JDowie and others shows what can be
done in that field. O’Malley, in early life, was in
the religious line, and it is said he still possesses
the rough notes of a Bible he then composed.
“ Quidam ” : Australians seem to be able
to stand pretty well any climate ever constructed.
Knew one young Rockhamptonite who went to
Klondike in its early days, and stood the climate
so well that after a visit to his native town (re-
puted to be the sultriest centre on the Eastern
coast) he returned undaunted to the Arctic Circle.
From cane-cutting or mining in Northern Queens-
land to the rigors of Klondike is a pretty far cry,
yet the white Australian can be found holding his
end up at either of those extremities.
How that weighty Tory, William Knox,
of Kooyong (Vic.), orates in the House of Repre-
sentatives :
I—er—repeat again, as—er— emphatically as anything
the Attorney-General has—er—said, that I for one—er—
believe—erUthat—er—we should be doing an injustice—
er—to—er—our Australian manufacturers here if we did
not—er, er—recognise this as a fundamental—er —prin-
ciple, underlying this measure, which many of us—er—
agreed not to vote against— er—on the second—er —read-
ing, if there is not to be somewhere in this Bill—er —if it
is to be of any use at all —er —some —er —means of pre-
venting—er, er—the unfair—er—or any—er—wrongful
competition with any—er—Australian—er, er —industry.
Er, er, er !
The most popular North Queensland pic-
ture post-card hasn’t got alligators or waterfalls
or canefields on it, but represents part of the
great unnatural resources of the country in the
shape of a local Chinaman and his four wives and
20 or so youngsters. At least there are only
three wives in the picture ; the fourth, repre-
sented by an empty chair, was probably away on
domestic duty. The yarn is that there are nine
more wives, hut they are left in China. Things
are seldom so bad that they couldn’t be worse.
The whole 13 might have arrived to hasten the
discoloration of the North. The frank polygamist,
who cheerfully owns to importing Chinese cus-
toms to these parts, and is the central figure of
many photographs, practises as a doctor.
“Spring”: Who and what sorb of a
human is this noble Senator who helps the Wren
to feather its nest? Has anyone ever read his
bumps? Has he the bleak eye of credulity, the
fan-like ear of the tip-taking juggins? Did he
learn his social politics in the two-up and hazard
school? Were his fellow-pupils decayed pugs,
sleeper-turners, thimble-riggers, and “workers”
of that sort? Does he spend his reading moments
absorbing penny-shockers—“ Three-card Bill, the
Dare-devil Crook,” and “Short-odds Dave, the
Hero of Rooks’ Retreat ” ? Take a young man’s
advice, noble Senator, and waste a night’s rest
thinking. Then get up unrefreshed, and have no
breakfast, dinner or tea. You’ve been in a steady
job too long, and eat, drink, and sleep so much
that your common sense has become clogged.
Stuart Alexander Donaldson, first Premier
of N.S.W., appears to have been a rainbow-hued
sort of politician. A description of him when
making his policy speech, from the Empire of half
a century ago :
Mr. Donaldson—rich, mellow, and pulpy as a melon —
came down to the House to give his “explanation ” in ab-
solutely splendid condition. His vest of cramoisy stuff —
scarlet, just fading—capacious, massive, and “heavy,”
made him look as much like a well-bred Minister as even
the Young England Party could have desired. His full
and affluent coat, thrown loosely open ; his neckcloth,
tied jauntily ; his flowing trousers ; and withal, his full-
toned form —big, stately, almost jovial—were sufficient
of themselves to strike terror into any new member, and
drive all thoughts of a maiden speech to Tophet.
And despite all that, his Ministry’lasted only two
months and nineteen days. Later, when he held
the office of Treasurer in the Ministry of Premier
Parker, he lasted 11 months and four days.
“Phoenix” writes:—
Tub Bullktix (28/6/’O6), referring to Senator J. C.
Neild, states : “He has been all his political life trying
to destroy Australian industries,” <&c. Exceedingly sorry
to say you are only almost correct. Once, and only once,
Neild waved his jawbone on behalf of the then growing
Protectionist cause, but he offended the Foreign-traders
of Paddington so badly by that act that they elected a
comparative outsider against him, consequently he had
to spend one Parliament without place, pay, or poli-
tical honor. I was at one of his meetings and
heard Jawbone, advocate the encouragement of our
local industries, and say that with adequate assist-
ance the products of 'local industries could replace with
advantage many shiploads of foreign, imports. That was
his political downfall—though he had once talked bunkum
for nine hours, the angry electors left him seventh on the
list, with 1806 votes. At the next election, however,
he announced himself astaunchsupporter of Foreign-trade,
and went on to speak of “the advantages that must
accrue by following the leaders of that great and glorious
doctrine, advanced by the greatest thinkers and states-
men of the old country, and so vigorously supported
by the ablest and most prominent workers for
the welfare of Sunny New South Wales.” (Have you
noticed how many of our politicians round off by using
“Sunny N.S.W. Abigail always did.) So the political
gentlemen got elected again, and he has discouraged
Australian industry ever since.
John Macarthur, about whom “ F.M.”
wrote (B. 5/7/’O6), was the first in Australia to
raise a legal question which even to-day suitors
occasionally touch on. John, the father of the
wool trade, had, it was alleged, smuggled into
Sydney a private still. Law proceedings followed,
but John, when cited to appear before the Crimi-
nal Court, set up the objection that Judge-
Advocate Atkins had borrowed money from him
and should not therefore be allowed to sit on the
Bench at his trial. Under the law of the time
there could be no Criminal Court without the
Judge-Advocate. Atkins, however, sat; but
before effect could be given to the court proceed-
ings, Macarthur had raised the standard of revolt
and deposed Governor Bligh.
“0.K.”: A recent Bulletin par com-
menting on the fact that the late W. P. Buckhurst,
of Melbourne, left less than £50,000 behind him,
erroneously stated that he was onetime a million-
aire but had been a heavy loser by land-boom
speculations. Buckhurst, so far from being a
rash gambler, was always scoffing at the foolishness
of Melbourne’s land-boom. He preached against
it, and refused to take any financial part in it.
Buckhurst was a niggardly man with a fixed
determination to avoid any excitement likely to
affect his health. For about twenty years before
his death he had ceased to work. He lived on the
income from his numerous house and land proper-
ties, travelled a good deal, and enjoyed himself in
his selfish, cautious way. The most non-boom-
some man that ever looked twice at a sixpence
before spending it.
The late George Watson would probably
have lived some years longer had it not been for
the invention of the bicycle and the motor car.
With him horse was more than a passion. It was
a religion. His whole life was horse, and he
brought up his children the same way. His boys
could ride as soon as they could walk, and his
daughter had her first riding habit at the age of
18 months. Whenever old George saw a man
riding a bicycle he would shiver, but console
himself with the idea that the rider was a poor
sort of creature, anyhow. It was when the
motor came along, and many people he had
known and respected as horsemen were seen out
in the degrading instruments of locomotion, that
he began to break down. His last public appear-
ance was at the Squires-Murphy fight, to which
he was wheeled in a chair.
WITH DANTE.
G UIDE: “ These are Australian artists, educated
on the continent, and now waiting for engage-
ments in London.”
MORTAL: “But why are they not engaged in
their own country ?”
G UIDE: “Oh, they are much too good for that.”
Another wearisome absurdity that still
remains in the House of Representatives (in spite
of a pressure of business so heavy that Prime
Minister Beakin has admitted that some Bills
will have to be sacrificed before the end of the
session) is the setting aside of certain occasions
as “ grievance days,” on which any member may
get up and babble on any subject he pleases.
Then Joe Cook froths at the Labor party ; bush
members talk about a post-card that went astray
at Boppy Mountain or out in the Mallee; and
every obscure Parliamentary fowl scratches round
in its own particular heap of dust. The dis-
cussions begin anywhere, wander everywhere, and
end nowhere, and all Parliamentary business is
hung up.

BUSINESS PURSUITS, which keep the mind more or less upon the stretch for
eight, ten, and even twelve hours daily, generally expend more nerve force
than can be generated by the over-anxious-aspirant to success. Excessive
nervousness and irritability, as every unfortunate wife of a hard-driven brain-
worker well knows, is a very common result of overwork, sometimes exhibited in the
gentlest form, a slight feeling of languor, a disinclination to rise to the occasion of
the usual brilliant effort, a headache, inability to sleep soundly, disturbing dreams,
&c. Constipation aggravates the evil, and the digestive organs fail to keep pace
with the additional strain on the nervous system. The dangerous brain-condition is
that in which the cerebrum has become so benumbed as not to feel the peril and
demand a halt. The brain derives its sustenance from the physical fitness of the
organs of digestion, consequently the organs of excretion need likewise to be in
perfect working order. Men and women can stand any amount of work as long as
the digestive and excretory organs are physically fit, and this condition can only be
relied on with unvarying regularity by means of Kutnow’s Powder.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
. Sydney, July 17, 1906.
My Dear Arini.—
Good old King Edward keeps his eyesight and
festivious taste in beauty. When Miss Eileen
Ward, daughter of the beknighted Joseph Ward
of Maoriland, was “presented” early last month
by the Countess of Crewe (one of the Rosebery
girls), the great Ned asked Earl Rosebery later
on in the supper room (since Victoria’s death the
Presented get some refreshment) about the young
Maorilauder and made some complimentary com-
ments.
Some three hundred ladies and gentlemen went
to Cabanta on Saturday to nurse the entente
cordiale and rejoice over the fall of the Bastille,
lo the French mind the Fall of Man is but a cir-
cumstance compared with the fall of their old
gaol. But the latter is a good excuse for a yearly
ou tmg and a champagne sparkle. Miss Rawson
and his Fx. ate some of the festive Frog at the
luncheon, and the latter complimented the French
Consul on the honor he paid the Great British
1 eople when he toasted the King before the
President. After that there was nothing for him
Garry) to do but to throw up his cocked hat
and wish every happiness and prosperity to the
Great JJrenoh Nation. Minister for Lands
Ashton then arose and also stroked the French
fur the right way. France had given the world
the greatest military genius since the days of
Julius Caesar (why drag in J. Caesar?); then he
got at short range and patted one of the guests
Dr. Danysz, who holds the fate of the Great
Australian Rabbit in the hollow of his glass tube.
Phis State will in August lay back its ears and
say Ida ! ida ! like the Biblical war horse. For we
are to . a Horse Show that month.
And the Show is to become an annual institution.
A country that worships the Horse as this
does will surely turnout m large round numbers
to make the show a success, and one of its side
features will inevitably be a Dress Parade. The
biggest Society function in New York is the
Horse Show. Now that our Spring Races have
been put back from September to October, we
would feel that we nad been too long in Spring
pickle if it were not for the promised Horse
Snow. One of the best judges of a neigh now
infesting Sydney is the lirst-ciass sport, Mr. John
laylor, of Quirmdi, who buys horses for the
Imperial Govt, and its allies, the Japs. He it
was who, after securing some ponies to Lady
Hopetoun s order last tune she was here, bluffed her
off fromseeing them until they were ready for show.
She commanded the presence of the animals. “ If
you saw them now you wouldn’t keep them ” was
the horse gospel according to John. “I can
make allowances,” haughtily replied the lady
lm not taking any chances,” said the horseman.
But when she did see them, she just fell on their
necks and stroked their long, soft ears.
Most stared-at pair at Rosehill races on Satur-
day was a brace of Collier’s “On The Quiet”
men. Iheir American overcoats took the eye.
Onecks like the lattice work on a summer-house
and suggesting a kimono as to make. Which
reminds me that a friend in Chicago says that
the correct thing in summer time for men
is the no : coat. No coat in the street, or house in
the evening. But the shirts, in their pretty pastel
tints, and box-pleated, have a blouse effect.
“America at Work” is stamped even on the
people who have put in a year there. Marie
JNarelle can t walk at the ordinary pace now she
sprints. .And as for little Taliaferro, she skims
along swiftly as a swallow, and it’s a comedietta
w* 011 li>'l6gged Australian man keeping up
Marie Narelle was back in Sydney for two
warbles after a prismatic success in country
towns, where the inhabitants made public holi-
days when she and tenor Fentress broke the
silence with song. Miss Narelle has an endless
string of k email stories, found mostly in America
and as yet innocent of publication. One of them
tells of an Irishman, just arrived in London who
meets a fearfully mutilated soldier seeking alms
money-box in hand. The beggar is minus one leg’
and retains but a stump of the other,’ both arms are
gone, and one ear and an eye are missing The
placard he carries says that his wounds were won
m the Boer war. The Irishman, after a pro-
longed stare, puts a half-sovereisrn into the
money-box, and earns the hysterical thanks of
the mangled warrior. Donor goes his way, but
returns in half-an-hour, and walks around the
beggar making another critical inspection ; then
he puts another half-pound into the box The
soldier, now weeping for gratitude, asks an
explanation. “ Faith, I’ll tell ye,” said Pat • “it’s
because you are the first English soldier I’ve seen
trimmed to me liking.”
Miss Narelle s triumphant home-coming is
darkened with sadness by the death of her
mother. Mrs. Ryan died suddenly last night, and
the soprano s country tour will suffer a short in-
terruption.
Beggars and begging letters are flies in the
amber of every Australian singer of note
who comes back in triumph to Sydney. Ada
Grossley was swamped with both. But for all
her graciousness and geniality, no one knew better
than Ada how to keep the salary-hook closed
over her profits She was not to be taken in by
the lady, the widow of an aristocrat, who wanted
a new horse and buggy to carry her vegetables to
market, the old trap being all tied up with rope
a “ d , tbe , horse m use bein S a Willie-goat. And
she didn t weep over the tearful party in rusty
biack who knew the contralto as a girl and always
paid to hear her sing. I think it was Amy Castles
who, while here got a letter entreating £5, the
refusal of which “would compel the writer to sell
her soul. Mane Narelle, overflowing with pity

with a herd of millionaires in America, she must
be wel inoculated with millionitis herself. Hence
she s always being taken down, and knows it
Sydney is frightfully dull, and for lack of news
a little sketch :
Tug Soul and the Casket.
At her first ball Isabel’s first dance was with a hand-
some aide-de-camp, rather spoilt by the admiration of
Women. “ That girl,” he told a friend the day after the
ball, “ looked so promising for a conservatory sit-out that
I programmed her for four dances at sight. Hair of sun-
shine, eyes of midnight, seventeen years, and such beauti-
fully cushioned elbows. How dare a woman belie her
personal appearance ! What d’ye think she tried to talk
to me about? ‘The following of Christ,’ and a person
named Loyola, who wrote the book. She’s a fish in cold
storage.”
The phrase got around among Isabel’s set as one that
fitted her without a crease. She went back to the station
where the South Australian wattle clump grew on the sun-
set side of a little hill, and dreamed and worked over her
verse-making. She used to sit under the wattles when
they were in flower, and showers of yellow spheres would
fall oil her and remind her of the shower of gold that fell
on Dauae. It was here, while looking at a page of states-
men’s portraits in an illustrated paper, that she first heard
the trees sing. Her glance rested a second of time on
each face until the Fifth was reached and that held her
eyes as a fixed bayonet arrests attention. It was then
the trees began to sing Mendelssohn’s Spring Song. She
looked up at them and saw them materialised into the
Spring Song—gold and green and grey.
At night, whilst Isabel slept, her Soul used to leave its
Casket and go out and wander in secret starlit ways,
meeting other souls, but never stopping for commune.
Never, until the night of the day she saw the Fifth Por-
trait.
“ You called me?” The soul of the original of the Fifth
Portrait was hovering by the side of Isabel’s soul.
“ Yes," was the reply. “ Your Casket and mine are
fated to meet. She is at last awake. By all the laws of
man, and the laws, men say, are Hod’s, these two should
stay apart. But—are even two air bubbles so much as
blown together against God’s will?”
“ We have known each other a long, long time,” said
the Fifth Portrait’s soul, looking with large eyes at
Isabel’s other self. “ Even if we would we could not put
off our Caskets’ meeting.”
“We have come up through many ascensions—we’ve
been through most of the jungle and forest beasts,
haven’t we ? ”
“ Except the pig. We never wallowed.”
“ No, we never wallowed. Do you recollect ages and
ages ago in this very region—our Caskets were then ring-
tailed opossums—the forest-fire that was started by vol-
canic fires, and we climbed to the summit of the giant
gum?”
“ * remember. You put your soft nose against my soft
shoulder; then the wide flame lapped us and the next
wind that blew over the blackened land scattered our
ashes. I want to tell my Casket that I have seen yours.”
The souls flew in by Isabel’s open window, but the
sleeper’s face was hidden in her long hair. “She always
sleeps like that,” said her Soul, apologetically. “It must
be a survival of the time of which we were speaking
when we used to sleep with our faces buried in our furrv
paWs.” J
The Fifth Portrait’s soul hovered off from where it had
been resting on the silver back of one of Isabel’s brushes,
and floated on to the wall, where it hung like a bat. It
seemed to be thinking. Presently it spoke : “ I get very
sick of Caskets. Don’t you ? How stupid they are, and
how vain ! Pin tired of all this donning and dotting. As
soon as death sets me free of one Casket, there’s another
waiting to be filled. I’m sick of the procession of Re-
turned Empties.”
Isabel’s soul hovered up close to the other and huno-
near it like another bat. “I am rather proud of my
present Casket,” replied the girl’s soul. VVe never speak
or think or act unworthily. Other Caskets call her the
‘Fish in Cold Storage.’ It’s their uncouth way of saying
she’s eclectic.”
“O, I’m not undervaluing my Box,’’said the man’s soul,
hastily. “It also harbors things fine and proud, and it is
as strong as chilled steel. But what are we, you and I
going to do about these two? lam fond of mv House—
you of yours. When they meet shall we barricade our
respective refuges against each other? Can we trust them
not to turn on us and try to throw us out ? ”
“ Have no fear. The vital spark in her will burst
into flame when your Casket clasps the hands of mine—
you remember how the volcanic fires set the cool, dim
forest ablaze—he will hold her in-his arms for One moment
we shall hear the seconds of the minute dropping liquid
gold ; then the ice avalanche of two thousand years of
Christian convention, two thousand years of Thou Shalt
Nots will sweep over her, and the fire'is smothered.”
“ That’s all very well for you,” murmured the soul of
the Fifth Portrait, “ for you are a female soul. The man
I inhabit takes mighty little stock of two thousand years
of Don’ts and Musts, tie’s rather given to making his own
laws. Not for nothing has he a jaw like the fender of a
locomotive. But he also obeys a helm, and I am the
helmsman—what is that growing through the window?
The rose of dawn 1 Adieu, adieu. My Casket is an early
waker.”
* * *
Isabel and the statesman met and exchanged common"
places. His smile had in it summer lightning and wild
beast, and by the time he discovered that her diffident
manner was a deliberate mask over the assurance of
social achievement, the day of their parting was there
They were two of a bright company at dinner, and she
was telling him that her taste in food had not been culti-
vated, for her preference was pea-soup.
“ She talks ‘ pea-soup ’ to him,” said Isabel’s soul to the
soul of the man. “These caskets would try to deceive
even us. Yet what I predicted did come about, and we
did hear the sixty seconds of that golden moment drop-
ping around us.” 1
“ Don’t try to pretend that we are not hurt,” said the
male soul. “ I’m going to look for balm for myself.”
Miss Rose Scott, who, now that there is no
female franchise to fight for, turns her gentle but
strong energies to advance other “Woman”
causes, invited a few friends to meet Miss Krout,
American journalist, at “Lynton,” last week!
Miss Krout has had a long experience of journalism
in America, ante-dating the inrush of “Yellow”
papers (for which she has a wholesome contempt);
and, in a short causerie, gave a clear picture of
past and present conditions. The giants of the
old days, Horace Greeley, Whitelaw Reid, and
the like, were described ; and then the modern
newspaper, with its eager sensationalism, its con-
tempt for sobriety of opinion, and its recklessness
in slander. It was a most interesting talk.
Miss Krout notes in American journalism a
marked tendency to make the newspaper a merely
business enterprise; side by side with this goes a
degradation of the journalists’condition. Where
before journalists were decently paid, now the
modern capitalist or Trust, owning a series of
papers, pays a high price for a few big names for
his posters, and gets the bulk of his work done by
cheap, unskilled lads and girls. Women jour-
nalists in America are suffering an unduly large
share of the results of decadence. Whereas in the
old order of things women were trusted with
almost any of the serious work of the newspaper
now they are mostly kept to dress reports and
society scandals.
In improving their premises recently David
Jones and Co., the fashionable Sydney drapers,
added a roof garden for employees; and there in
the heart of the city the saleswomen and others
may enjoy during lunch-hour a feast of fresh air.
It's a good example that other shops might
imitate.
The Yankee press tells a wild story to account
for one fash.enable English woman’s espousal of
Socialism. Years ago, when she was one of the
most beautiful girls in England, she was greatly
admired by the present King Edward. What
happened, nobody exactly knows, but he and the
lady didn’t play speaks afterwards. Now, accord-
ing to the papers of the U.S.A., she is animated
with a desire to get even with His Maj. for what-
ever he did or didn’t, and has taken up Socialism
in anticipation of bringing about a revolution
which will lump King Ned off his throne and
make him ready to come round to her backyard to
ask for a job gardening.
A London correspondent writes that the latest
thing in infant phenomena is Wauda Radford, an
Adelaide-born child ten years old (well-known in
Sydney, where her father is manager of the
A. 8.0. Cafe). She sings, dances, and enacts
scenes from the great tragedies like a miniature
Rachel. Roth Melba and Ellen Terry are giving
her the helping hand, and a crowd of rich Ger-
mans are taking a bulky interest in the little
Australian.
Same correspondent mentions that Sydney
pianist, George Royle, is a success in Berlin,
where Authorities have pronounced some of his
compositions “ masterly ” and “showing genius.”
Concerning other Australians abroad : Miss Nea
White, a daughter of the late “ Havilah ” White,
who went hence under the care of Mrs. Elood, was
married last month to Queensland squatter
Flemich.
As to fashions in London, the latest thing in
foot-gear runs to dainty buckled shoes with hose
to match, in tones of mauve, deep purple, blue, all
shades of foliage green, and biscuit shades. The
black hat is an epidemic. It is trimmed with
colors of course, but there’s a revulsion against
the rainbow hues of last year s straws; hence the
crow head-gear of this.
“ The automobile umbrella that one sees in the
Earks is like a doll s sunshade, but it has the
iuged stick, so that the dome-piece can be tilted
at any angle to shade the face. ’ (One of these
Pans notions was brought to Sydney from London
by Mrs. J. C. Williamson, some four years ago,
but her use of it in a brougham always brought
her an escort of humorous little larrikins, desiring
to know where she got it, and hadn’t she better
employ an umbrella-mender.) “As for men’s
fashions,” continues my correspondent, “the
swagger clubman wears pants so tight that the
mere woman wonders if he lias to be oiled before lie
getsintothem with theaid of ashoehorn. His gloves
are a bright canary-yellow, and glare from a
distance with a Rattle of the Boyne challenge,
and the violence of the shades of his blue, or
green, or mustard tie makes one wish one had put
on blinders before coming out.”

.Tohnnydom rise en masse from its Savoy meal and

Please note the subtle distinction between
people who gad round casually with a Governor
and people who gad officially with his Ex. His
Ex. is accompanied by So-and-So, but he is
attended by Such-and-Such.
The suffocatingly close corporation, the medi-
cal fraternity, which takes a top-lofty view of
the advertisement question, has broken out in
a fresh place at Wanganui (M.L.). In birth noti-
fications printed lately it is intimated that
“ mother and child are both doing well under the
care of Dr. .” This lets every matron in
town and up suburban lanes know who is attend-
ing Mrs. Birthrate, and a better advt. the good
dear doctah couldn't wish for. No woman ever
misses that column.
I see that J. W. Bottrell, president of the Vic.
Vegetarian Society, says that, instead of building
an Ifiebriates Retreat, the Stale, should save
money and effect a more certain cure by board-
ing out its inebriates to fruit-growers. He
alleges that the London Vegetarian Society has a
standing reward of £lO to anyone who will pro-
duce a bona-fide vegetarian drunkard, and the
reward has never been claimed. “ The man who
is fed on a vegetarian diet,” says Bottrell, “has
no craving for drink. ” But it seems possible that
it is simply because his craving and anguish for
meat obliterate his drink hunger. He must have
the meat first. And when he has it he has ceased
to be a vegetarian, and the Vegetable Society
then disclaims all responsibility for his drinking.
Engagement of Mr. Maurice Toohey to
Miss Constance H.iyes, a daughter of the
secretary of Sydney Exchange, has stirred the
Harbor waters somewhat. Bridegroom-elect
is son of the late brewer, J. T. Toohey,
and has some considerable thousands a year.
Mr. Toohey’s sisters and stepmother are in Lon-
don, bending their spines at Royal drawing-rooms,
and having a prismatic time generally. Their
Sydney home, up the North Shore line, is one of
the finest humpies in Australia. The Hayes live
close by, in another attractive home.
Ex-Premier John See has my permission to get
married. I wish he would,; then correspondents
would cease from troubling me with “ authorita-
tive statements” as to his bride-to-be. Latest
mate selected for him is a beautiful young party,
daughter of a deceased merchant. The knight
looks such a fine sport in the line of widowers that
Sydney fully expects him to marry again.
Sydney is once more clear of the sheep men with
their beards like the foliage of the she-oak. The
older ones are a fine, frank-looking lot of green-
hides with big voices and huge laughs.
“ Trixey ” writes from Melbourne
I dislike George Meredith for so cruelly exposing mv
sex : so I willingly went to hear the Australian Literature
Society (or something like it) dissect his works. I arrived
punctually at Glen’s Concert Room to enjov the opera-
tion. The first stages were very depressing. Two gloomy
persons with beards and intellectual-looking evening
clothes, in company with a caraie of water, a single
tumbler, and several cottage pianos, decorated the
platform. They—the men murmured some introduc-
tory remarks to the audience, which numbered about
twenty women and three of the other sex. Then there
was an impressive interval, during which a damsel with a
classic nose and alisp, undulated through the wilderness
of empty chairs, and presented me with a programme
Eventually a gentleman with a fluffy finger and an Ma'
degree drifted into the neighborhood of the water bottle’
He was the star turn. His criticism of the author under
ordeal was limited to frequent platitudes, uttered sonor-
ously, and with appalling dignity. He also “did"
fragments of dialogue from “The Egoist,” and
Richard Feverel.” The sprightly wit of Mrs. Mount-
Stewart Jenkinson, voiced by a seriously minded M \
became lugubrious and awe-inspiring. Sir Willoughby
however, became a living reality. Now, what have elo-
cutionary assaults on English novelists got to do with the
encouragement of Australian literature, and why do
newspapers give space or serious consideration to a show
whose aim seems exclusively mutual admiration?
A note from Adelaide :
Engagement is reported of Miss Sh.vlie Blue to Herbert
R.vnii.ll, both clotted cream, both notable golfers. Miss
Blue is the step-daughter of Adelaide’s only little baronet
and Chief Justice—her father was a country doctor—but
she does not believe in being tied to any apron-strings at
all. She’s a considerably joyful damsel, with a perfect
genius for having a good time. Benedict-to-be is a son of
old Henry Rymill, and of a very conservative stock. The
family has the motoring habit rather more developed
than any other in the train-horse State, and is ever so
rich. Marriage will take place about September.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENRHI.
Judkins and Woodfull make the sensible
suggestion that in Bent’s projected Licensing Bill
a conviction for Sunday or after-hours trading
should be placed to the discredit of the landlord—
i.c., the public-house—as well as the licensee. At
present, as a third conviction entails forfeiture of
the beer-seller’s license, his landlord changes
tenants on a second conviction being recorded,
the incomer starting afresh. But with the con-
victions attached to the house —well, there would
presently be very little Sunday trading or very
few licensed houses remaining.
How is it that the Sydney daily papers
preserved absolute silence over the recent open-
ing of an office in Sydney by the Pacific Cable
Board for dealing directly and conveniently with
the public? The Australian people own a big
share of the Pacific cable. The Pacific Cable
Board notified the opening with big advts., but
received not a paragraph of encouragement. A
private company would have received columns of
compliment and leaders of eulogy. Yet the
morning papers proclaim their public spirit and
devotion to the “ best interests of the country ! ”
But, of course, the syndicated cable news comes
by the rival Eastern Extension line, and that
accounts for the worm in the cocoa-nut
“F.”: You mis-read W. M. Hughes’
Anti-Trust amendment. Note it again, with the
context of the Hill :
Industry means any occupation in which persons are
enj>aged in the production, manufacture or distribution
in any State in the Commonwealth, or the introduction
from foreiyn countries into the Commonwealth , or from
one State to another, of anything having an exchange
value.
That means an effort to bring every calling under
the penal provisions of the Bill, and to make quite
sure that the importer doesn’t escape. Hughes
may be a Freetrader (he says he is), but during
the life of this Parliament he has been a consistent
Australian. He’ll soon strike a crisis at which
he’ll have to shed Australia or Freetrade ; and I
prophesy that Freetrade will go. [And The Bul-
letin accordingly apologises.]
“Loco.” : -Re Sydney tram-driver’s suicide
(B. 5/7/’OG, p. 12). I sincerely sympathise with the
tram-driver’s relatives. As a train-driver I have
many similar nerve-smashing experiences. An
instance. Going along with my train, half-a-mile
ahead is a level crossing with cattle pits. Coming
towards the crossing, on the road, is a horse and
cart, with the head of the driver just showing
above the body of the vehicle. I get nearer the
crossing. So does he. I whistle, as per regulations.
No effect. If I apply my brake I shall lose the
momentum I have striven hard to keep up for the
l ast two miles, and perhaps get stuck up on yonder
lin 50 bank. I’ll just about make a dead heat of it
to the crossing with the horse team. I am 200
yards off now. Still seemingly he does not see me,
nor hear my whistle. I must apply my brake
now. I do, and reduce speed enough to let the
cart get over in front of me Just then the driver
stops, sits up, lights his pipe and looks as if he
wonders what on earth the train is going so slow
for. If drivers of vehicles, and others, when
approaching railway crossings, would give some
indication that they see the train, and stop a
reasonable distance back, there would be a lot of
engine-drivers’ valuable nervous systems saved.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, July 17, 1906.
My Dear Yarrte.—
There are so many aides in the Northcote suite
that the comings and goings of any one of them
is of no concern much. Last year Captain Byng-
Stephens, one of the most painstaking of caddies,
set sail for the Cold Country. No one noticed
the departure. The other day he resumed his
efforts to promote vice-regal circulation, and no
one has yet noticed that either. Same way with
Captain Balmain, the most strenuous of the staff.
He went to the Fog city early last year without
comment, and returned minus any flourish. Not
till he married Miss Fairbairn did any special
notice attach to him. He has now left the staff
for good and all, and no one notices the gap or
misses his energy. It’s a redundant staff at
any time.
The social walk loses another stately prome-
nader in H. M. Chomley, ex-Chief Commissioner
of Police. The tall old man liked to sun
himself where he had once swaggered. Up to
a few months asro he was a venerable beau
of Flemington. He haunted the Terrace with
his Frenchily-dressed wife. He conducted his
social duties as solemnly as his brother
the Judge—conducts Bench matters, and paid
a compliment, when the exigencies of the occasion
demanded one, with the shy awkwardness of a
man who has the duty sense so strongly developed
that nothing short of hanging would keep him
from its doing. Last year H. M. Chomley and
his brisk little wife went travelling in search of
health for him. They returned without having
found it. The Chomleys are a big clan. Half of
Toorak will be put in mourning by his death.
A small—you might say short-coated—party
with music and claret-cup came to pass on
Monday evening at Stonington. It proved a
honKEopathic mixture of officials and socials, and
went down without a hitch. One of the im-
promptu hits of the evening was made by Miss
Winter-living, who played with telling effect.
The two other special features of the musical
hour were Madame Janson and Napoleon Boffard.
Among the favored few was Miss Buckley, of
Beaulieu, just back from a great gad that ended,
unfortunately, in mourning. At these soirees
Dame Talbot unbends somewhat, and passes her
smile so gladly round on the company that her
very greyness assumes a warm radiance.
The charmer hereabouts is wearing her skirt
briefer than wit. It's the result of the skating
craze, which requires extremely short garbs and
visible ankles. Miss Atherton, who has been
executing the frozen waltz largely, is an instance.
Her clipper style is a lesson in compact frocking.
It’s skimpy and economic in material, but there’s
a large bill in the sundries—the neck-furs and
muff, and so on.
Premature Spring brought the flutterers
forth in the gayest and frothiest of garb on the
final day of the Flemington meeting. The July
Steeplechase had never produced such a fine and
festive swarm of butterflies. Heads flaunted the
cheerfullest of feathers. In fact, an ostrich farm
seemed to have been let loose on the heads of the
mighty. One notable fashion-plate, who generally
perambulates at Flemington, this day had a few
yards of seaweed-green and lavender ostrich
boas swinging from the rim of her high-crowned
Welsh hat. The two vice-regal dames paraded
in royal purple and mauve tones sables
included in each impressive, instance. A
troop of aides tore their glance now and
then from the racing steeds to see if any ot
the vice-regals required a gamp closed, or carried,
or a yawn suppressed, or a spot removed from the
sun. It s interesting to observe a sporting aide
divide his attention between caddy fulfilments
and the nag he has parted all his tram-fares on.
Bigjohn Forrest had Austin Chapman—the re-
turned Federal Postmaster-General—in tow.
Roman conferences and such have wrought no
sudden change in the suave Minister—unless it
was that he smoked his cigar with excessive tran-
quility.
Mrs. Tom. Fairbairn and Miss Edie Smith,
both returned gadders, sported cream with
small garden-plots for top-gear. Miss Smith
was particularly chic. Mrs. Lindsay Inglis
Struck a check novelty—in purple and claret
velvet. A black hat of some pretensions topped
the rig. Mrs. Gordon Lyon beat all beauties in a
ficelle rig and race-coat, with a shrimp pink and
cardinal millinery triumph. Mrs. B. Bell sported
cream with a round hat, from which depended a
hyacinth-blue shaded feather of languishing grace.
Mrs. Billie Bell is a charmer of wool circles.
The Baltimore crew were at large this race-day.
Commander Stone and his blue-eyed greyish
dame headed the contingent. The officers were
in the tow of various Toorak philanderers. An-
other interest was the theatrical one. The “Squaw ”
stars were out —Miss Humphrey in a pastel-blue
corselet rig, a Lady Dedlock tragedy sort of hat,
flying chiffon and lace tails. She cut a dash, and
was a help to the Terrace picture. Miss Grace
Palotta looked fresh and lovely enough for any-
thing, but she had neglected her dress opportuni-
ties. Her plain rehearsal rig missed the mark.
Miss Eugenie Duggan wore the most wonderful
toilette in the gathering. It was a sooty glace,
with a crusade of silky trills from waist to hem,
and a top tier of still more curious fashion : a coral-
pink summery hat waved its terminal chiffons in
Spring frolicsomeness. Miss Clara Clifton, fresh
from Harborville, struck a cheery dress-note.
The big Saturday “Mexico” audience revelled
in a fresh novelty in the “Tamanee” hit. The three
stars of the co.—Miss Palotta and the two W.’s—
attired in rainbow-striped blankets and head-
mounts consisting of a circle of upended rooster
quills, took part in the wild Indian whoop and
dance, though the song itself fell to Grace. It’s a
screaming burlesque ditty and caper, and after, an
encore, comedian Ward shouldered the Indianess,
bolster fashion, and carried her off amid heavy
charges of applause.
The most sensational canvas in the Vic. Art
Galleries this year of show is Bernard Hall’s
decorative panel-subject, “Sleep.” There are
three figures, the one suggesting the title being
stretched nude at full length. It is too taut. That
rigidity alone mars a work rich in color and in
treatment. The marble of the bench, and the
mosaic floor of the classic lounge are both
finely depicted. But Bernard Hall’s formality of
mind mars any imaginative work. He wants
breadth and independence to put the requisite
feeling into his art. Blamire Young’s chief de-
corative panel is a deliciously warm, brilliant work.
A slight, dark-haired, woman stands naked to
the waist, with heavy claret draperies swirling
around her feet. A back-ground of deep electric
blue supplies a contrast. In the ambitious can-
vas showing a very long-necked Lady Franklin,
perched like a stuffed cockatoo at an alleged
reception of early Victorians at Fawkner Hotel,
Blamire Young is not so successful. The group-
ing of figures and the treatment of color is good
everywhere, but in the show of heads, the men
have sharp blade-noses, tooth-ache cheeks and
unnatural complexions., It's a big, expensive
canvas with only a little Young art spread on it.
Arthur Boyd, F. M'Cubbin, F. Hayward, and A.
Colq u houn are represented by meritorious canvases.
Among the she-daubers, Miss E. Rix strikes a
new bold note as a decorative paneller working
on Arcadian lines. Miss Helen Hambidge
has her name to a fine portrait of - a
chocolate-haired beauty. Miss Fabiola Tuomy,
Mrs. Muntz-Adams and Miss Amy Bosworth
haven’t to blush for their pigment effects.
The announcement that the Talbot Dame was
showing at the annual spread of Victorian Artists’
canvas proved a potent attraction. At the open-
ing one couldn’t see pictures for people. Dame
Talbot’s contribution to the collection is a most
curious memorial tablet, a silk tapestry design,
with a frame of wood, silver and mother of
pearl, which has the virtue of being Australian.
The design is of a gay fern-spreading Spring order,
the green of the fern being contrasted with a glad
blue ground. There is a text to the north of the
fine delicate needlework (which is really perfectly
done) and a musical scrap of hymn to the south—
the latter, notes and words, being effected in
beaten silver. The whole thing is rather too glad
to be a memorial tablet, but it s genuinely pretty.
Miss Grace Joel (a cousin of Mrs. 1. Isaacs) is
holding an exhibition of canvases on her own.
The Athenaeum Hall, voted the solemnest build-
ing about, is the scene. The gloom of the en-
vironment probably aids the thoughts in concen-
trating on the pictures. Miss Joel has had three
of her pictures hung on the Paris Salon walls of
1901, 1903, and 1905. The first attraction about
this returned artiste’s show is the poetic and fit
labels of her work. “ The Beaten Path of Years ”
is an instance, showing an old country road
bearing the indent of a long procession of waggon
wheels. The expressive back of a tired middle-
aged wife, seated facing the track, and a sugges-
tion of a homestead, provide atmosphere to a
well-treated subject. “ 'The Dying Year ”is an-
other thought. poetically rendered into paint.
“ The Sun Goeth Down” is a third trifle grace-
fully expressed in pigment. The most popular
canvas is “ Her Child,” an excellently rendered
picture of a mother gazing with wistful tender-
ness on the sleeping infant in her lap. The figures
are well drawn, the flesh tints are natural, the ex-
pression is adequate. This was a canvas eulo-
gised by artist Watts and other authorities at last
year’s Salon. “Paint Me” and “We Two” are
pictures of some humor and skill. As a portraitist
Miss Joel is most successful in her picture of John
Cockburn. The ex-Agent-General sits in a quiet
watchful attitude, as he would at a Board meeting.
There is a kindly gleam in his eyes. The vitality
of the man is fully expressed. It is a cleverly
caught likeness, and full of abundant vigor in
repose.
A wattle wedding broke through the monotony
of cafe sing-songs and picture shows last week.
This was the marriage of a squatteress in her own
girlish right—Miss Olive Ed wick to a martial
blade, Mr. Frank Stogdale, who once was a
dandy of the Rupertswood battery, but warriors
elsewhere now. The wedding was a very smart
affair, St. Peter’s the scene of the. ceremony.
The Grand Hotel was marked for the glass-
clinking and kindred felicitations. The reception-
room was a golden-bower in a blaze of wattle.
From a triple gateway depended wedding-bells.
Doves were about, and true love-knots had a say
in the poetic scheme. The wedding-cake was a
triumph of ingenious confection. It symbolised
the martial character of the groom. Silver
bayonets pointed up from the base ; silver cannon
lay about ; silver shot huddled radiantly on the
lower tier. And the interior, if consumed, would
probably prove more deadly than shrapnel. But,
fortunately, wedding-cakes are rarely consumed.
Miss Ed wick brided it in snowy chiffons, and
made a fair picture. She is pretty, and her style
of garbing effective.
A motor maniac leaves this Bent State for Ger-
many shortly, and quite a drove of the fair will
raise a dirgeful cry. His machine has whirred
glad parties all through Victoria. But in a
week or two it will whirr no more with him at the
helm.
Lady Le Hunte and her chick set sail for the
Cold Country this Spring bloom, and the vice-
regal residence will be given over to the moth and
rust , and Sir George for a year. His Ex. will
work out his grass-widowerhood in tours of the
land and in sitting for his photograph—a weakness
of his. .
Mrs. Kenric Brodribb gave a smart dance at her
East Melbourne mansion last Tuesday to launch
her youngest chick into the social world. The
said chick is a clever young Miss with russet hair
awave on a clever head. Her front name is Noel.
A Miss Bird, daughter of the surgeon, was pro-
claimed a grown-up and a person come to the use
of reason the same gay evening.
Miss Alice Mills has struck a new note in
miniature effects. It’s an attractive one ; and it’s
something of a trick. A faint photograph is used
as a groundwork, and on this Miss Mills employs
her clever brushes till the completed work repre-
sents either an excellent water-color portrait or
a dainty miniature. The effect is highly artistic,
and has the virtue of being true in likeness.
An Australian he-touris.fr complains that the
haunting cry in the Big Smoke is “conform ’ !
There is always something or someone you must
conform to. The cry never ceases. “To be in
it,’’ he writes, “one must conform in dress, in
manner, in speech, in thought, in face and feature.”
Dr. Frank Liddell has withdrawn his brass-
plate from Oollins-street. He intends toot-tooting
round the country in a petrol car of his own when
House of Heps, worries require not his exquisite
presence. The medico divides his interest equally
among pills, politics and petticoats,
Hugh Ward, who met Miss Leila Doubleday in
Sydney, and was much impressed with her
playing, shows his generous interest in the child
now. by arranging a matinee for her in the
Princess Theatre for the 26th inst. Willoughby
and Ward give the theatre free and provide all
services; Mrs. Hugh Ward (who rejoins her
husband in Australia this month) will contribute
to the programme, and as she is a musician of
distinction that will be a great “draw”; and
further, Grace Palotta and Hugh Ward will add
to the attractions of the entertainment. It’s a
real generous thing of “ Hugliie” to interest
himself so materially in the little Doubleday’s
success : the incident explains to some extent the
hearty grip that he has got of the affections of
Australian audiences.
Among the axe-wielders showing at Fitzgerald’s
Circus building last week was a young Tasmanian
about whom the few she-things who attended the
carnival went into ecstasies. He was an Apollo
six feet four inches high, with a pinky-brown com-
plexion, the features of a beauty-actor, a melting
brown eye, and a small, rosy mouth. Where the
older champion spat on his hands and assailed
his log in a rough-and ready fashion, the long,
brown Apollo gracefully sketched on the tree-
trunk the lines along which he intended the chips
to fly, and generally stage-managed his turn with
consummate art, while greedy female eyes lost not
a quiver of his beautiful muscles. And, my word,
Apollo could chop ! His beauteous young limbs
sent the lumps of wood flying with the wartiest
old champion of them all. He suggested a new
melodrama—where the stalwart backwoods hero
will chop down enough trees in five minutes to
make a bridge, over which the heroine will scuttle
gracefully out of reach of the flood, which the
villain has let loose on her home from a burst dam.
Miss Drummond, the intelligent young person
at present guesting at the greater Government
House, is the daughter of a very old friend of
Lady Northcote.
Something unique in the way of hops was given
in the Masonic Hall, t’other evening, by the
management of the Grand Hotel to its em-
ployees. All the staff turned out minus brass
buttons or cap and apron, to polka through a
lengthy programme. A gay band kept them in
action, and large mirrors reflected the glad scene
on all sides. It made one ponder on the levelling
effect of cloth and chiffon. The gathering had a
large share of good looks (these same being an
asset in the registry-offices), and was particularly
well-groomed and well turned-out. The Maid of
the Mop in decolletd rig is much after the pattern
of the Toorak fashionable ; and the hall-porter in
polka-tails is a finer specimen than many fashion-
ablemen. I saw a lift-boy two-stepa dainty maid of
the mop through a Lancers with far more grace and
ease than you find in any opening official quad-
rille anywhere. It should teach the wealthy
hotel-dwellers to conduct themselves, for -the
attendants evidently know how things should be
done; and do them when off duty with any
amount of empressement.
Madame Agnes .Janson bills herself for an
operatic performance on Tuesday, the 24th.
Sharing in the show are ’cellist Hattenbach,
Miss Barclay, and Harold Elvins.
The death of Mrs. S. Oliver, last Thursday
profoundly affected Toorak. The deceased lady
was very beautiful, a model of all the domestic
virtues, and, furthermore, was bright and inte-
resting. The woman who is all these things, and,
shining above all her contemporaries, yet stands
aloof from all questionable gaiety or frivolity,
becomes a paragon ; her happy, sunny nature and
frank, bright manner saved her from being a prig.
A husband and a daughter (Mrs. Stuart Murray)
are the saddest losers by her death, though several
sisters and a host of cousins are all in sincere
mourning.
The hat that is built while you wait has arrived.
It is on the fresh-air principle. You go to a
florist who has the millinery knack and buy a
crown of roses or clump of daffodils, and get it
pinned to your skull or the lining of your hair,
and there you are. You may have a straw rim
skewered around the head bouquet if you like.
That’s if you’re particular. Otherwise you just
go forth decorated with fresh flowers and foliage.
Presently we’ll be getting our frocks on the pound-
in-the-slot principle.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Reid speaks in Queensland of “when I
was Prime Minister.” And he was never any-
thing more than a vulgar fraction of a Prime
Minister.
Celebrations of the 12th of Juliars went
very flat this year. Is Australia getting wisely
sick of Orangeism ?
Vida Goldstein will be in the Victorian
field again for the Senate next election.
“ Squidge ” : No one seems to have yet
thought of altering the name of Australia to
Owestralia. [Better to alter the policy, and not
have an Owestralia.—Ed. B.]
The cool, calm Irvine, who is now court-
ing the Flinders (Vic.) farmers, has gone right
back on his one-time land-tax principles. Yet he
used to be the boss icicle of the Single Tax
League.
Hailstone Irvine, standing for Flinders,
Vic., announces himself “a moderate Protection-
ist.” The Age wants to know what a moderate
Protectionist is. That’s an easy one. A moderate
Protectionist is a moderate freetrader.
THE STRANGLE HOLD.
Catch-as-catch-can contest between Local Industries
and the Terrible Trust.
THE REFEREE: “Foul!”
Maoriland has rejected 47 white immi-
grants in three years. Forty of these were Dagos
of sorts who failed in the tongue test. Only
seven were Anglo-Saxons, but three of those were
consumptives and four were insane. All the con-
sumptives came from London.
Cameron, M.H.R., who alleged that he
held the fate of the Reid mis-Government in the
hollow of his hand a couple of years ago, and
made himself notorious solely by keeping his
mouth shut, is to be opposed by Dr. M’Call, an
ex-Chief Secretary of Tas., and a Protectionist
who undertakes to vote Protection.
Dismal Dick Butler, S.A.’s champion in
the whine industry, grizzled for two hours upon
the Address-in-Reply, almost entirely on the sin-
fulness of his deposition from office, which is the
one subject that fills his small mind. Everything
that Tom Price says aggravates the offence, and
the humorless shadow even complained bitterly,
in his opening speech, that Price had allegedly
claimed he could trace his ancestors back for gene-
rations.
At a Women’s National League meeting
at Heidelberg (Vic.) —a “drawing-room” meeting
—the lecturer told her “ pupils ” that all the in-
formation they required would be found in the
Aegus and (ye gods !) Melbourne Paunch.
In Canada, it seems, it’s a decent and
worthy thing to lie for one’s country. In Aus-
tralia the influential fashion is to lie against one’s
country. Better not to lie at all. But the Cana-
dian liar is far the more admirable sample of the
two.
Frank Tudor, M.H.R., Vic., praising
liis party at Malvern the other night—“ There are
more local preachers and Sunday School teachers
in the Labor party than in all the other parties in
I arliament put together.” Certainly that kind of
self-praise is no recommendation.
“Mr. Irvine’s limited Protection put him
in mind of a garden full of fine fruit protected by
a chained dog. The dog, if free, could protect
the gaiden, but chained, was no use. They
wanted not limited, but adequate and complete,
Protection.”— Hume Cook, M.H.R.
As much as £5 an acre rent is being paid
for first-class sugar land in North Queensland —at
Geraldton and other places. Presently the man
who is paying £5 a year an acre for land, that was
got freehold from the State for about 10s., will say
that he can’t afford to keep a white laborer.
What he really may not be able to afford is to
keep a white loafer.
In the Argentine just now State Enter-
prise is giving Private Enterprise a very rude
lesson. Private Enterprise couldn’t or wouldn’t
make boots for the army at moderate prices,
because of Trusts and other tilings, so, three years
ago, the State started a factory of its own, and so
well did it succeed that now it is going in for
making saddles and other gear for the police and
soldiery.
Maorilanders are becoming gradually im-
bued with the commendable notion that they can
make things quite as well as foreigners. Wel-
lington city council is spending another £7OOO on
tram-cars, and it is stipulated that they shall be
made locally; and Christchurch is adopting a
similar policy. It is unlikely that the Railway
Department will ever send away for more engines,
and its carriages have long been made in the
State.
‘ ‘ If industries cannot be established with-
out Protection, let the Commonwealth Govern-
ment establish them, because then we can be quite
sure of the condition of factories, and proper
wages being paid, and of decent hours being kept.
There would be no one to sweat, because there
would be no one to make.a profit.”— Anti-Socialist
Reid , crying out for Socialism at Toowoomba (Q.),
in 1901. (We have printed this before, but
reprinting just now is opportune.)
What the bursting up of big sheep and
oattlo walks, by means of a graduated land-tax,
would mean to Australia may be gauged by
statistics collected by the N.S.W. Railways some
years ago. Two areas, of 10,000 acres each, one
under cultivation, the other under sheep, and both
300 miles from Sydney, were selected as a means
of ascertaining the relative value of the two in-
dustries to the railways. The agricultual area
panned out £3lOO, the pastoral one £9O.
No sooner has Premier Bent expressed his
intention of making a high municipal rate more
advantageous than a low one, in the matter of the
municipal subsidy, than the swindling land-
owners begin to work points on him.
Hitherto the subsidy a shire received was
calculated on its revenue
from rates up to Is. in
the £ (the shire that
taxed itself at 2s. in the
£ got no more subsidy,
proportionately, than the
one that levied a rate of
Is. only). Bent proposes
to make the subsidy apply
only to shires that levy a
rate of Is. 6d. in the £, as
an inducement to the
farmer person to pay at
least half-a-crown him-
self before he asks the
State for two shillings.
The landowner noted
Thomas’s remarks —like-
wise his proposals to
increase the subsidy—
with pleasure, and rose
all over the country to
remark that it would be
easyto get more subsidy
by raising the rate and
lowering the valuation,
whereby the rate of Is.
6d. would cost him no
more than that of Is.
Bent threatened to ap-
point Government valuers, whereupon the ag-
gregate councillor person arose again in great
heat and remarked that “he was capable
of doing his own business, and that if Mr.
Bent went too far he would find that the
people would soon put him out of office.” But
Thomas knows the tricky, dishonest councillor
person by heart, and is weary of him, and the
chances are he will keep a stiff back this time.
From M.L. :
The undemocratic attempt to found a Seddon dynasty
—for that is what the move to foist 22-year-old Tom
Seddon on to the constituency that his late father repre-
sented amounted to—was successful after a heavy engage-
ment with deceased Seddon’s late lieutenant (one Michel).
Young Seddon is a likeable young fellow, with a good deal
of his father’s personal charm, and possibly some of his
ability ; but hitherto his political achievements have been
confined to debates in Victoria College (Wellington)
debating society, and his toil has been wholly at law
“ swatting.”
. For really first-class lying the she-politi-
cian takes the anna and the sapphires, and any
other cash and jewels going. Women’s National
League in Victoria commits itself to the state-
ment that the Labor party proposes to put a tax
up to 4 per cent, on the capital value of all estates
over £5OOO in value. And the proposed tax on an
estate of £lO,OOO (unimproved) would be £lO,
whilst 4 per cent, on £lO,OOO would be £4OO. It’s
a fine lie, and puts all male political records to
the blush.
“THE FAMILY—AS WE KNOW IT!”
Until the family—as we know it—disappears the ideal co-operative Commonwealth cannot be realised.—Altars.
The Queensland Employers’ Federation,
in a denial containing a large percentage of in-
dignant snort, has publicly declared that it did
not invite George Reid to Bananaland. Reid’s
half-hearted anti-Socialism doesn’t inspire confi-
dence in a crowd which wanted a fine old mouldy
remnant just out of the Ark. Or perhaps Reid
winked the other eye to the Employers’ Fed. to
give the denial. To go under the auspices of that
body in Q. is deadly.
An average yield of 10 bushels of wheat
per acre is fair for Australia. That yield produces
a big surplus for export, and it will be so while
our population-increase drags along at the rate of
some over-populated old country. The Australian
wheat producer has to accept for his export surplus
about lid. per bushel less than the quotation in
London. That lid. hits the cocky hard ; it is
equivalent to 9s. 2d. per acre when the average is
10 bushels, and it is a graduated burden on the
land as production is increased. It is because of
this fact that Protection, with the consequent
industrial expansion and increase of local popula-
tion, is a matter of urgent importance to farmers.
Premier Bent has an idea of starting a
tourist bureau on the lines of those of N.S.W.
and M.L. To this end, he has transferred from
the Railway Department a live young man who
has been collecting wheat freights, given him the
munificent rise of £lO a year, bringing his salary
up to £250, and sent him off to M.L. to investi-
gate the methods of that land. When he returns,
Tom intends to turn him loose in an office and
tell him to get to work. He is to have no other
assistance; but will write ads., design posters,
hustle into a uniform to personally conduct dis-
tinguished strangers round the Chow quarter,
and perform the mysterious mission known as
“organising the department” in his spare time.
“Two Seas” : Don’t know how the Shires
Bill is going to act in this State, but I do
know that if it operates anything like the
Tassy instrument, poor old Fat will drum on
his bingy like an enraged chimpanzee, and say
that capital is being driven out again. In
Tassy, when a would-be buyer of a farm or
grazing property goes to inspect, he usually
drops in at the local warden’s office to look
at the assessment books m which the capital
and annual values of the place are to be
found. The owner will probably ask at
least twice as much. On his return the buyer
will be way-laid by the genial warden (the
Tassies are a friendly people) and over a
whisky the warden will inquire: “By the
way, what is Blank asking for his property ?”
Buyer mentions a sum that makes the warden
grin. Result: Another indignant Fat writing
to the papers about increased assessments
and how capital is flying from the country
with its tail streaming in the wind.
The Victorian Lands Department is
wrestling with the demand of a bee farmer that
his legal position be defined in view of the fact
that a neighboring grazier has threatened to shoot
all his blooming bees. The person complained of
must have some confidence in his skill as a marks-
man. Possibly a close season for bees will be
do dared.
The appointments of secretaries by the
newly-appointed N.S.W. temporary shire coun-
cils show the prevailing wage to be £2 10s. per
week. A few are paying £3, and Queanbeyan
shire has shocked the frugal soul of Joey Carru-
thers by giving £5. Some, however, are giving
only £l, others £1 ss. One careful council is
giving £1 4s. 6d., while another has reached bed-
rock by offering £5O per annum.
The Victorian P.L.C. is discovering that
the gun it treacherously turned on its Liberal
friends—Higgins, Mauger and Co.—shoots back-
wards. Having invited J. C. Watson to do a
little organising work in Bent’s State, that gentle-
man curtly and properly declined, on the ground
that to do so would, either directly or indirectly,
injure those whom he had given his word should
not be opposed.
The Jap Government recently initiated
Government silk-grading, and the Australian im-
porter (amongst others) will now receive with each
length of cloth a Government guarantee as to
length, weight, and quality in each of three
grades. Strange to say, the Australian importer
hasn’t hastily risen to furiously denounce this
Government “interference with private enter-
prise.” So far, evidently, the enormity of the
said “interference” applies only when the Aus-
tralian Commerce Act essays to return the
Monkey (amongst others) as (compulsorily) honest
a deal as he (compulsorily) gives.
Vic. Premier Bent’s habit of bellowing at
large has just got him into a fair-sized difficulty.
Advocating his proposed Licensing Amending
Bill, at Brighton, lie stated that at the alleged pro-
hibition township of Mildura liquor could be freely
obtained at the chemists’ shops. There are liquor
clubs at Mildura, but it happens there is only one
chemist, who is a strict Rech'abite, and he has
been speaking firmly to Thomas and wanting to
know why he shouldn’t claim damages for slander
and injury to business and reputation and so forth.
Just Another case of Capital being scared
out of Australia. A top-hatted man, wearing a
coat of great area tailorwards, travelled down the
Queensland coast recently, and told each day that
he had come out to invest £1,399,829 6s. 6d. in
mining and railway enterprises, but found he could
employ nothing but white labor, and so was
leaving the country. A fellow passenger visiting
a hairdresser’s shop, the day after the boat touched
Brisbane, found Private Enterprise lathering a
customer in splendid style. Presumably he had
enough confidence in the country to invest his
£1,399,829 6s. fid. in shaving gear.
Now that Richard Seddon is dead, what
are the chances of Australian interests in the
New Hebrides receiving serious consideration by
Britain ? Spurred by Seddon during his visit,
Deakin joined in a request that the terms of the
proposed Anglo-French agreement should be
made public. Seddon made to a Presbyterian
deputation the grave statement that, “if the
terms of the draft agreement were made known,
it would be apparent how urgent the necessity
for the deputation was.” Now Seddon is dead,
and Deakin has received the reply. It is that
“ publication would impede the endeavors to give
effect to any alterations suggested by Australia
and Maoriland.” In this, Deakin has affably
acquiesced, and Seddon’s forcible views have gone
with him 6ft. underground.
AN OFFER.
Mr. Reid’s Bundaberg (Q.) meeting was held in the
local theatre hired from a theatrical co. for the evening.
News Item.
STAGE MANAGER: “ Sir , I feel bound to accept
your offer; but, at the same time, I cannot
help saying I would much rather that you
joined forces with us and gave your harle.
quinade as part of ode show, and split the
proceeds .”
No wonder Yic. Chamber of Manufactures
president Joshua, says “talk of the intended
political confiscation of the Labor party makes
him ill.” It was the Free trade party, that is
always blathering thusly, that confiscated his
living ; when Aloysius Fox lobbied the Parliamen-
tary Foreign-traders and some fool Laborites so
successfully that the old Victorian protection on
spirits was reduced almost to vanishing point, he
—acting for his employers, the spirit importers
—practically confiscated Joshua’s property and
business. And all the Foreign-traders did was to
dispute their agent’s bill.
G. H. Reid on White Australia at Towns-
ville (Q.): —
He could not promise that he would do anything to
reverse the “ White Australia ” policy, or to increase the
protection of sugar. If there was to be a change, it would
have to be brought about by public opinion, and the first
duty of those in Queensland who objected to the Federal
policy was to change the public opinion of the State,
and send to Parliament .men who would represent that
opinion.
He “could not” —not “would not,” be it observed,
G.H.R. hints plainly his readiness to turn
pro-nigger the moment there is safety in thq
wriggle.
VIC. TOPICS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Bent, in an ordinary tone, recently announced his
intention of spending over a million, and when the House
inquired in a faint gasp for details, he asked them, in a
gentle, reassuring voice, to wait a minute while he looked
it up.
This is the chorus Victorian, sung in a moment of leisure ;
This is the song of Bent Thomas —his prophets and seers,
Set in a very long-winded, Longfelonian measure
Written and blotted and chanted just as it appears.
Others less powerful wanted and wept and derided,
While others raved on the benches, or got in a stew,
Thomas B. quietly mentioned that he had decided,
In a spare moment, to lay out a million—or two.
Loud was the blast, but the yells of the members weie
louder,
Wherefore and whither and whatnot the mildest they
said,
Thomas B. smiling, and seeming, if anything, prouder,
Told them he’d tell them more later, and went home to
bed.
Scorn of the Detail Delicious! O Regal Magnificence,
really
Impressive and quite overwhelming, deserving the scone ;
Glamor and Glory and—one or two other things nearly—
But lam choked with emotion—l cannot go on !
VIC.
