SPORTING NOTIONS.
*
From Flemington Hill, on Steeplechase day :
The bookies were paying out on Decoration when cries
were heard, “ Look ! Look !! There they go. Look at
this one ! o,you brute ! Stop him ! Stopliim !” Faraway
on the Flat below* three or four welchers, spielers or
whatever they might be; were sprinting across the ground
to the bridge. Opposite the Stewards’ stand a scuffling,
fighting crowd surged, from out of which burst a big,
burly man in his shirt sleeves, with a white pith helmet.
Perhaps two score enraged animals followed him, one
waving the welcher’s scarlet coat. He dafted for the
punt, just across the bend ; but before the 300 yards were
covered some pursuers had caught him, while others
Chipped in on the way, just for the lust of a man hunt. He
battled by them, cleared the rails at a spring and crossed
the course. But his luck was out: the punt was at the
other side. Baffled, he turned, and, panting, bleeding,
blundered deviously across the course to the entrance
gates, where he might find troopers and safety.
The maddened mob tore after him. Children were
swarmed over and trodden on ; women showed bleeding
hands and ribboned clothes where the crazy crowd had
stamped on them, reckless of aught but the lust of blood.
The troopers were moving now—too late. One tired his
revolver over the seething heads—in vain. Others rode
straight at the swarm—their horses pranced against a
soft, but unyielding, wall. The victim still staggered on,
till one black-coated coward behind swung a tierce blow
on his right ear, and sent him plunging twenty yards
along on the grass. Then the crowd surged over him,
and kicked, and kicked. A few men tried to stem the
torrent—boxer Ted Nelson was one. As well they might try
to stem the rush of fear-maddened bullocks. Then a couple
of troopers steadily drove their horses to the centre,
where it lay, and presently four men carried away a
stretcher on which was 15 stone of flesh and blood,
bruised, battered, and mud-stained, with its neck broken.
All through the uproar Iltey on the Hill never turned his
face to look ; his raucous voice kept yelling “ I’ll pay
Decoration ; I’ll bet on the July. Six to one lted Streak,
ten to one others.”
The outrage would have disgraced a nigger-
roasting crowd of the southern states of U.S.A. The vic-
tim, Donald JVFLeod, was a welcher of the persistent sort,
it seems. He Collected bets, but informed backers of win-
ners that he was not in a position to pay out, and com-
promised by returning them their own money. The
money paid over by losers he kept. He had done this
several times on the last afternoon of his life, and on the
Grand National Steeple itself had booked 27 bets, of
which 10 were on Decoration, the winner. As Decoration
started at 9 to 1 on the lawn, at even half the price
M’Leod was obviously unable to pay out, even had he
Wished.
* ♦ * *
Notwithstanding the chronic complaint that
every time is a time of “dearth of good steeplechase
horses,” there was a general opinion among experts that
the field for last Saturday’s Grand National at Fleming-
ton included a much larger proportion of high-class stuff
than is ordinarily saddled up for a big jumping event.
And this estimate was borne out by the result. The
5-year-old Decoration, greatly improved of late, won the
race in record time, carrying a heavier burden than any
of the previous seven winners. Syringa and Beilis, who
followed him home, had both of them been there or
thereabouts in other years, and old Springfield as
placed fourth for the second or third time. These, with
The General, Cardinal, and Lamond, represented old
“form,” whilst Officer, Lothian, and Boisdale were ad-
mittedly promising candidates at their respective
weights. Decoration must have been a rattling good
’chaser last Saturday to finish lengths in front of a strong
opposition.
* ■* . * *
The Shamrock recompensed his backers for his
failure of the previous Saturday by getting away with the
Doutta Galla Hurdle Race. But for smiting one of the
obstacles in the Grand National the much fancied
Westralian horse would probably have finished second to
Realm ; yet his winning performance didn’t make him out
to have been quite a good thing for the race wherein he
failed.
* * * *
A father of racing, James Mayo, died in Sydney
last week. Randwick trainers, J. A. and F. Mayo, are
two of eight sons. Mayo pere raced Dagmar, Roulette,
Maid of Australia, Hypatia, Honeydew and Invader in the
old days, and he also trained, among other noted per-
formers, Cardigan, Erin, Stockwell, Locksley, and Crom-
well.
* * * *
The astute Mr. Albert Miller expressed his
opinion of the Grand National candidates by declining to
give old horse Error another chance to get beaten in the
25 furlongs race. The ancient started for the minor
handicap steeples on Wednesday and Saturday, and scored
a handsome win each time, which was better business
than reserving him for the more difficult event.
* * w *
A paying spec. Grand National Hurdles’
winner, Realm, was bought, when a three-year-old, for
£75, and, after gathering minor events as well as the big
cross-country race named, brought 650 gs. at auction last
week.
* * * *
Comes from Bananaland that turf matters there
are improving. Q.A.T.C.’s profit last year was £669,
which tops previous 12 months’ results considerably.
* * * *
After all, some good may come from the resump
tion of the strained relations between the Ascot Club and
the Kensington Racing ring. The correspondence which
both sides are heaving at one another in the press is cal-
culated to draw public attention, to the dimensions of the
curse. Racing in Sydney Metropolitan district has utterly
outgrown the “legitimate requirements” of even such a
horsey population as that of Sydney, and has now got to
the length of creating a class of people who do nothing
else, while they have the money, but follow these
wretched meetings scattered round the suburbs. Con-
sider the list of racing clubs that operate in the Metro-
politan district—A.J.C., Tattersall’s Club, City Tatter-
sail’s Club, Warwick Farm, Canterbury Park,
Rosehill, Moorefield, Kensington, Rosebery Park,
Epping, and Ascot Clubs. Some of these harpies
prey on the public twice a week. The punter who follows
the game goes to Kensington or Ascot on Monday, Rose-
bery Park on Tuesday, Kensington or Ascot on Wednes-
day, Epping on Friday, and Rosehill on Saturday. Then
there are regular meetings at Warwick Farm, Moorefield,
and Canterbury Park, just by way of variety.
• * * '*
Maonland takes top place in the weights for the
Caulfield Cup, with Menschikoff ; and also in the A.J.C.
(Sydney) Epsom and Metropolitan, with Achilles—a big
advt. for the blood stock of Maoriland.
* * * *
“ 0.K.” : The late George Watson, who figured
for so many years as the V.R.C. man with the flag, must
have owed his title—" Prince of Starters to the grovel-
some disposition of the local sporting press. I saw hun-
dreds of fields of horses sent off (and called back, in many
eases) by good old George, but I never saw' any special
evidence of his being peculiarly well fitted for the billet.
There was too much “ language ” in his method. He
weakened his moral control over the riders by
sw'earing at them, and he was not incapable of
favoritism. At one time or another he made some
shockingly bad starts, but it was treasonable to complain
of them. The Prince could do no wrong. He had the
right face and figure for the position. iHe looked the
part. There are various types of horsey men whom one
mentally puts into classes, and Watson filled the eye as a
first-class boy of the old school.
* * • *
“ The Sydney Club,” which is said to be owned
by Melbourne tote-man Wren, was again raided by the
police last week—the second raid within a few weeks.
Just, of course, since that is the law. But why does the
Sydney Club get a monopoly of this attention? There
are half-a-dozen similar institutions in the same street
that should be given a turn.
• • *■ *
The match between Auckland City and Glebe
District teams, at Sydney Cricket Ground, on Saturday,
attracted 30,000 people, who sat shivering in the cold,
hard wind that blew from the south. They were there
to see a football match, instead of which they found
themselves treated to a rough forward scramble in which
a startling series of accidents happened to the Glebe
players, till the game came to an end dramatically.
Riddle, of Glebe, and Little, of Auckland, simul-
taneously kicked at the leather, and their legs
crashed together with a report that was heard in the
members’ Stand very distinctly. With a yell, both men
sank on the ground. The game stopped instanter, and
the injured men had their broken legs put in splints,
after which each team carried its wounded off the field.
At St. Vincent’s Hospital it was found that, in addition
to his broken leg. Riddle also had two fibs broken. Play
was not resumed, and at the time the accident happened
the score was in favor of Auckland City by 11 to 8.
* * * «
When the Auckland City team had finished
butchering the Glebe niert, Sydney Cricket Ground was
handed over to the N.S.W. and Queensland intef-State
teams. The Cornstalks had the benefit Of the wind in the
first half, and turned it to the very best account, and by
judicious kicking kept the leather mostly in the Queens-
land 25. The northern men were not fkst enough, and
their backs had rtot mastered the art of picking up. The
ability to pick up cleanly and kick like lightning has
saved many a match, and ort Saturday Queensland’s
failure in that respect piled up points on the N.S.W. side
of the scoring-board. What with bad picking up and the
strong wind, it was obvioUs that Queensland had no hope
of scoring in the first spell, and it didn’t. When the
whistle blew, the score was N.S.W. 11 to nil. With the
southerly behind them the Queenslanders made some
desperate efforts, and the second half opened with the
appearance of being a more equal game. But there was
nothing in it. N.S. W. cleared the line every time it was
threatened, and carried play back to the Bananapatch,
where they scored again and again. The gante was so
utterly one-sided as to Ire devoid of interest—no one can
get up any enthusiasm over the spectacle of one team
running over another, and the general monotony of it
was only relieved by one brilliant six-man pass, which
took the ball from close to halfway, right over the line at
the eastern corner. Afr.er that Queensland just went to
pieces, and N.S.W. added another 11 points to its already
formidable score. Queensland’s only score was a goal
obtained from a penalty pick, and the game ended 25 to
three.
* * * *
The selection of A. J. Mackenzie, of the Uni-
versity, for the position of N.S.W. full-back in the match
against Queensland occasioned considerable surprise.
Mac is a good man, and as it happened, played his game
on Saturday without a mistake. 0. Hedley,' of the Glebe,
had been originally selected, but he begged to be allowed
to play for his own club against Auckland City, and the
request was granted. Well, there is absolutely nothing
to chose between Hedley, of the Glebe, and D’Alpuget, of
the Eastern Suburbs, and both of them are better men
than Mackenzie in the general estimation, and why
D’Alpuget should be passed over is a mystery.
* * * *
Under Association rules, the game of games in
and around Sydney on Saturday last was that between
Sydney and Rozelle. The teams were about as evenly
matched as possible. Rozelle was the faster and more
aggressive, but the Sydney team kicked with greater
judgment and force ; so the result of an irresistible attack
meeting an invincible defence was a draw without a score
on either side. At Epping racecourse Balmain scored
three goals to Glebe’s one, and at the Agricultural Ground
Pyrmont downed South Sydney by five goals to two. The
result of the round was to leave Pyrmont in the van of
the competition with 21 points, second place being filled
by Balmain and Rozelle with 16 each. Glebe has 14, and
after that there are only single figures. At this stage of
the competition single figures do not count.
* ■* » *
The crack game on Saturday in the Sydney
League matches (Australian football) was that between
Sydney and Redfern. It would have been a much better
game but for the fact that the public got so interested in
the struggle that it occasionally slopped over the line
among the players. Sydney, winning the toss, took ad-
vantage of the savage wind that tugged at the scalps of
the onlookers. The result was that the first quarter
ended with Sydney leading by 17 points to 1. Then Red-
fern got the wind, and settled down to equalise things.
But Sydney played desperately, and managed to score
8 points, but that wind told and Redfern lifted its score
to 19 points. By the time Sydney regained the wind it
was dying down, but they made the most of it, and ran
ahead to 46 points while Redfern added only 1 behind.
The final quarter was played in a comparative calm.
Redfern struggled gamely, but the Sydney kickers had
found the range, and went on ruthlessly bill at the bell
for close of play they had won by 58 points to 20. Redfern
had failed to add a single point during the last quarter.
Y.M.O, A. came along to see Newtown with a team largely
composed of substitutes, and, naturally, went under by
68 to 14 points. Nevertheless, the policy of giving the
colts an opportunity is a good one. Also, it is one rarely
abused in Sydney sports. East Sydney, with three
juniors in the fighting line, attacked and walloped North
Sydney by 45 to 37. Out at Moore Park, Paddington
simply crumpled up before Balmain, the waterside suburb
men winning by 88 to 27. In the competition Newtown
is leading with 18 points, Sydney follows with 16, Redfern
plods third with 14, Balmain fourth with 12, and East
Sydney next with 10. Below that is just mud.
Ideal weather and the prospect of an exciting
match drew some 25,000 spectators to Victoria Park on
Saturday, where, in Vic. League football, Colli ngwood
met Fitzroy. The display of football was first-class
during the first two quarters, which saw’Wood score 22
points to ’Roy’s 27 ; but in the third quarter last year’s
premiers rushed four goals, in almost as many minutes,
and from then to the end the case was hopeless for
’Wood, the board when the bell rang showing ’Roy 13
goals 10 behinds to ’Wood’s 6—B. There was excitement
in spots, certainly; as when two players exchanged
several willing blows close to the fence, the ball being far
away at the time, and when another sustained a drive in
the mouth that cost him a tooth ; but even a bigoted
barracker could hardly call that football. Similar
compliments were also passed between St. Kilda and
“Sowth,” but the umpire there was stricter and pro-
mised to stop the game if there we>'e any further hostili-
ties. “ Saints ” ultimately won by 35 to 28 points, but
the interest in these two clubs, with Geelong and Mel-
bourne, diminishes, it being pretty evident that the
finals will lie between Essendon, ’Roy, Carlton and
’Wood, with the odds on the first-named. In the Asso-
ciation the undefeated West Melbourne put up a good,
strenuous game against North at the “Glue-pot,”
scoring 37 points to 26; while the improving Richmond
team beat Footscray by 82 to 35. The clubs stand in
that order, with Williamstown clinging to the tail-board
of the van, in a manner of speaking. But the absence of
a single defeat this season begins to fill the “ West ” men
with that cockiness that goes before an upheaval, where-
fore they are advised to go slow and keep an eye on the
ground before them. Also they are recommended to pay
no heed to the blandishments of the local Lizzie ; she
is a bright little animal, but she won’t speak to them
when they get wallopped.
* * 9 -k
That the Australian game is fairly dangerous
may be gauged from ohe fact that, in Melbourne last week,
out of the eight League clubs, Carlton had four men in-
capacitated, Saints three, South two, ’Wood and Essendon
one each ; and those 11 are a fair average of Invalids out
of the 144 players. Of the ten Association clubs, Port had
five on the injured list, Williamstown four, West three,
Footscray two. Prahran one, and the unlucky Essendon
Town no less than eight. When, as happens a hundred
times in an afternoon, five or six strong:, lusty, young
men simultaneously jump four feet in the air from all di-
rections, their hands being engaged seeking the ball,
something is bound to happen frequently, and it
sometimes means broken arms, or legs, or ribs, or collar-
bones ; but mostly it is only severe bruises —a fatal acci-
dent is rare.
“Micawber”: How is this for stamina?
Speaking to one of the Auckland team, after the match
5/7/’O6, he commented upon the severe trip across and
the manner in which the various members were affected.
One player was particularly ill, and, for three days after
leaving Auckland, never left his bunk. On Friday, he
reclined, semi-unconscious, on the upper deck, and, pre-
vious to the boat entering the Heads, had almost
perished from exposure, as his presence on the deck had
been forgotten, and he was too weak to reach the cabin.
Yet this player put up a rattling good game against New-
town, and was one of the try-getters !
* * * *
George \V, Smith, the Auckland three-quarter
footballer, is perhaps the speediest player in all Maori-
land. In times gone by Smith was Australasian hurdle
champion, and holder of Australasian 120vds. and 440yds.
records. Then he retired from athletics altogether, but
subsequently drifted again into active football. Smith is
continually “ retiring ” —but somehow he cannot keep
out of the limelight.
* * «
Stanley Wickham is the “ veteran” of N.S.W.
Rugby football. He first “ represented ” against Banana-
land in 1895, and has been in the State team with scarcely
a break ever since, besides which he played against M.L.
in ’97, ’9B, 1901 and 1903, and against England in 1904.
Another old ’un in N.S.W. Rugby is Harold Judd—a
mighty forward who appeared for the State against
Rananalartd in 1900, and has kept his place ever since,and
also played against M.L. in 1903 and 1905, and England in
1904. Wickham and Judd have alternately filled the
captainship of the N.S.W. team for many years ; and on
recent form both are st'll as reliable as the younger
players.
* * » *
Melbourne Cricket Club lately, like Agag,
walks delicately. It has been practically kicked out of
the ground that it controlled for so many years, and
there are some enormous sums of money involved. Just
at present it seems open to anybody to start a suit in
Equity to make the Melbourne Club render a statement
of accounts for the revenues of the ground, and if anyone
should start that action there would be a lot of inter-
esting disclosures, not the least of which would be the
amounts paid to some of the gentlemen captains who
brought teams from England, and posed as lily-white
amateurs all the time, and are still posing as such. The
influence of the M.C.C. depends absolutely on having
control of the ground. So the club affects to regard the
trustees appointed by Tom Bent as persons illegally on
the premises, and it is understood will take legal action
to enforce its control of the ground. Even if the club
loses, it argues, it will take the trustees two years to eject
it, and in the meantime the club will have control. But
what is the use of fighting the master of many legions,
unless one has as many legions, which the club hasn’t?
Bent has Parliament behind him, and with one small Act
can strike the bumptious club utterly dead. And Bent is
just the man to do it if needs must.
* * * *
When Paddington (Sydney) Club came to deal
with the N.S.W. O.A.’s instructions to suspend Noble
and Trumper for the part they had taken in the trouble
with the Melbourne Cricket Club, the members found
themselves in a tight place. They had a feeling of loyalty
for the Association, and yet were dragged in the other
direction by their personal friendship for Noble and
Trumper, so they tried to get out of the difficulty by
a quibble. They endorsed the action of the Association
in suspending Noble and Trumper, but regretted that the
Club had no rule Under which it could suspend the two
players. The Association saw through the well-meant
deception, and suspended Paddington Club itself. Noble
and Trumper have since tried to undo the trouble by
resigning, but the Association is obdurate, and insists on
its orders being obeyed. In the meantime it has started
to arrange the programme for next season, leaving Pad-
dington out altogether. Once the programme is arranged
it is like the Law of the Medes and Persians—unchange-
able. So Paddington Club is likely to disappear igno-
miniously after a long and honorable career. Seems like
a case for a general meeting and the election of a new
committee.
* * * *
South Australia will now, unconditionally,
apply fora position on the Board of Control. Move is easy
of comprehension to those in the know. One particular
party who looks forward to being in an English tour or
two was wildly anxious about his profits, and, having dis-
covered that there was no dancer to his personal prospects
as far as the present Board of Control is concerned, sud-
denly realised that he was jeopardising those prospects
by opposing the Board. Of course, South Australia,
which is controlled by its players, will now agitate for an
immediate invitation to the Mar.vlebone Club to arrange
for a team to come to Australia next season. But there
are reasons why it should be disappointed- Victoria lias to
reorganise its cricket, for a totally new position has been
created there. The League players who are now joining
the Association under a system of district cricket must
be given a chance to play against the next English team
that comes to Australia, and also for membership
in the next team that visits England from
Australia. Queensland is in a similar position. It
has to work out its cricket on a new basis. Not only is it
undesirable for an English team to come to Australia
next season, but it is exceedingly doubtful if the game will
be sufficiently reorganised by the following season to
send to England a team really representative of all
Australia. The new Board of Control will be elected
somewhere about the middle of August and one of the
first things it should do is to emphatically announce that
there will be no English team invited to Australia till the
game is thoroughly established on the new basis.
Victor Trumper, having some idea of standing
as a delegate for his club to the Association, wrote asking
if he was regarded as a professional or as an amateur.
Considering that he himself wrote to the Association
stating that he would in future play under rule 19, which
provides for payment of professional players, the request
for information seemed hardly necessary. But the Asso-
ciation informed Mr. Trumper that, as he was and is a sus-
pended player, the Association had no jurisdiction over
him. In effect, he inquired : “ What class am I?” and
the Association replied, in effect: “You’re not in either
class—you’re just not.”
* * * •
George Towns is now training on the Parra-
matta River, but up till the time of writing had managed
to keep his progress fairly secret, and no one seems to
know exactly how the ex-champion is shaping. Stanbury
is reported as being very well, and in excellent fettle. His
superior strength and weight are a big handicap in his
favor. At time of writing £2OO of the £5OO side wager
was still unpaid, but is not due till July 25th. One
thing is certain, that it is a very different George Towns
to the one he beat before that Stanbury will find
pitted against him on Saturday week. On the other
hand, Stanbury seems to be a much better man than when
he defeated Towns. The struggle will be an intensely in-
teresting one, between the most graceful, artistic
oarsman in the. world, and the strongest sculler on the
planet. The race will take place over the old regulation
course, from Ryde bridge to Searle’s monument. The
winner of the impending struggle will most likely be
called upon to defend the title by Edward Durban,
Hanlan’s nephew, who is reported to have struck some
money, and to be coming to Australia to try for the
world’s championship.
What a day Saturday, July 28th, will be in
N.S.W. ! At Cootamundra, Holman and Norton will
clinch ; Towns and Stanbury decide the sculling cham-
pionship of the world on Parramatta River ; and the great
\V. N. Willis will arrive from S’Africa.
* * * #
Dick Arnst, the man who won the last Sydney
Thousand, arrived at Sydney last week, and immediately
made a bee line for George Towns’ quarters at Mortlake.
It is understood that a syndicate has sent the mighty
wheeler over for six months’ training, and if at the end of
that time he shows signs of championship prowess as an
oarsman, he will get a further course of training, after
which the syndicate will proceed to wipe the floor of the
world with him, and make much loot out of his victories.
Hick is the sort of man who would probably succeed at
anything—barring, perhaps, writing poetry. He doesn’t
show any signs of the poetical temperament. Up to
the moment he arrived at Mortlake, he told Towns, he
had never been in an outrigger.
• * * *
The match between Sawkins and E. J. Camp-
bell for the billiard championship of Victoria provided an
interesting contest, for the most part. The game was
5000 up, in six sessions. At the outset, both men played
poorly, the bonzoline balls coming strange to them after
their recent experience with the lighter and brighter
ehrystalate balls in Alcock’s tournament. Sawkins was
the more bothered of the two, and Campbell had a lead
of 191 when the first session closed. At the end of the
third session Sawkins was about 100 to the good, and
next night his “ form ” was very decidedly better than
Campbell’s best. Although he never quite succeeded in
making a break of three figures, he had an average of
20‘19 per innings, which wants a lot of beating on a
championship table. This put him 440 ahead, but in the
next session he had all the luck against him, and Camp-
bell reduced the lead to 84. On the last day a fresh set
of balls had to be substituted for the old ones, and both
men played unevenly until the game stood at 4514 to 4502.
Then Sawkins went on and won by 300 odd, scoring his
last 220 points whilst his broken-up opponent made only
35. In style, consistency and nerve the winner seemed
to have the advantage—not a great advantage, but enough
to swear by.
* * * *
There’s a lot of nonsense in the Melbourne
Beer trade’s offer to put up ten beer men against ten
total abstainers for a tug of war, as though the average
physical strength of the non-Reehabites and Rechabites
Could be fairly compared in such a contest. In the first
place the drinkers have the bigger population to draw
upon, and in the second place, a tug of war is not a
proper test of muscle and endurance. It is mainly a test
of weight. A huge, flabby man hanging to the far end
of a rope goes for more than a lean, healthy man of the
same height and bone structure. Fat is strength in a
tug, and “ knack ” is better than power of endurance.
All the same, it seems probable that the Vic. Cold Tea
party could put a champion ten into the field. Some of
the local professional muscle-makers, notably Clarence
Weber, are total abstainers and non-smokers. As No. 1
in a tugging team, the fifteen-stone Weber would be an
important consideration.
•* * * »
Sydney Summer Nights Amusements, Limited,
has announced that the usual November Cycling carnival
is off. In compensation, the money that is usually raced
for during November will be added to the prize list for
January, making the total amount then £O5O.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Cyclists’ Association, which is try-
ing to displace the League of Wheelmen, makes a modest
beginning in the road-racing line. The first event under
the new body is from Camden to Sydney on August 11th
for a prize list worth £6O. Of this, £25 is represented by
one of Henderson’s bikes, and a large portion of the
balance is made up of trophies, so the cash cyclist who
races under the Association doesn’t stand to collect much
filthy lucre.
* * * *
The contest for the Dunlop inter-club cycling
premiership reached the semi-final stage on Saturday,
when Enfield and Eastern Suburbs clubs qualified for a
start in the final. Enfield won the fixture last year, and
is generally looked upon as favorite for the final, which
has been postponed, on account of the sculling champion-
ship, from July 28th to an unfixed date about three weeks
off.
* * * •
The Pioneer Motor Cycle Club has cut adrift
from the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen, and at present
there is a movement on foot to unite in one general
motor league, the Automobile Club, the Pioneer Motor
Cycle Club, and the Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. A large
number of car owners are also boat proprietors, and the
same thing applies to the motor cycle section, although
to a less extent. A combination of the three clubs
would result in a very strong body which should be able
to organise some most interesting contests. But the
trouble is that neither car-owners nor boat-owners are of
a sportive nature. They keep their cars and boats for
pleasure, and would rather drift round pleasant places in
the afternoon sunshine, sipping tea and flirting with
pretty women, than hurtle through the air or water in a
frenzied rush to do the distance in a fraction less than
another man did it the week before. And theirs is the
better part.
* * * *
Veteran Rooty Hill (N.S.W.) coursing man,
Sam Bladon, won another Waterloo Cup at Moonee
Valley (Vic.), last week, with his crack youngster Bul-
wark, which only recently appropriated N.S.W. N.C.A.
Grand Challenge Stakes. Latest Australian Waterloo
Cup was tiie richest for some years. First money totalled
£225. Generally conceded that old man Bladon knows
more about the points of the greyhound and how to keep
and prepare him than all other Australian coursing men
together. Backing the right one of Bladon’s nominations,
in any coursing event worth troubling about, rarely pans
out badly.

Legal Notices.
yy ILLIAM SAWERS.
NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that a Petition under
the Presumption of Life Limitation (Scotland) Act 1891
has been presented to the First Division of the Court of
Session in Scotland Lord Mackenzie Ordinary by William
Clark Sawers Spool Polisher 98 George-street Paisley
and John Muir Coal Miner Border Shaft Alleghany
County Maryland United States of America praying the
Court to find that WILLIAM SAWERS sometime Box-
maker 316 Main-street Bridgeton Glasgow has disappeared
and that he was last known to be alive on or about 21st
November 1894 and that he must be presumed to have
died on 21st November 1901 being seven years from the
date On which he was last known to be alive.
The Petitioners’information as set out in the Petition
is as follows : “ The said William Sawers left this country
(Scotland) for Melbourne Australia in 1890. He arrived
in Melbourne on 13th August 1890. He was then 19 years
of age having been born on 30th June 1871. He was un-
married and a domiciled Scotsman. While in Melbourne
he worked as a laborer. After staying in Melbourne for
some time he went to Evandale Australia. At Evandale
he lodged with David Graham Master Bricklayer there.
Mr. Graham was his uncle by marriage being married to
atsister of his mother. He stayed at Evandale until 1894.
He then left and went to the old gold diggings in Aus-
tralia.”
On which Petition the Lord Ordinary has pronounced
the following Interlocutor. “ Edinburgh 24th May 1906
Lord Mackenzie. Act J. P. Smith. Alt- .” The
Lord Ordinary appoints the Petition to be intimated in
the Minute Book and on the walls in common form ;
further appoints intimation of the Petition to be made
twice in each of the following newspapers viz. : the
Weekly Scotsman the Melbourne Argus the Sydney
Bulletin and the West Australian in order that all
parties having interest may lodge answers to the Petition
by the 15th day of October 1906.
C. KINCAID MACKENZIE.
Any information as to Mr. Sawers’ residence if alive or
his death if dead should be forwarded to the subscribers
BRUCE KEIR & BURNS
W. S.
16 Hill-street Edinburgh
Scotland.
J. & J. HENDRIES
Solicitors
Union Bank Galston Scotland
June 1906. Petitioners’Agents.

ELIZABETH MARTHA WOOD, formerly the Wife of the

Order of the Honorable W. GREGORY WALKER, Pro-
bate Judge, made herein on the 28th day of June, 1906,
the Widow (if any) of the abovenamed John Wood, who
died on the 15th day of March, 1904, is, on or before the

to participate in the distribution of the Estate of the said
John Wood to the Curator of Intestate Estates ; other-

leaving a Widow. The address of the said Curator is
Queen’s-square, Sydney. Dated this 29th day of June,
1906. T. W. GARRETT, Registrar of Probates.

It was said that the Labor party wished to endanger

There seems to be a general impression that the
amalgamation of the Automobile Club of Australia and
the Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. will be carried by an
overwhelming majority of the members of each concern.
Which reminds that the Ocean Reliability race to
Newcastle is still a hazy fixture. In fact, it, is doubtful if
that race will come off till next summer. This portion of
the year, which is, as a rule, associated with calm seas,
has this season been unusually stormy, and there hasn't
been a day for ever so long when the committee would
have been justified in starting such a race.
* * * •
Another nasty display of black man’s wrestling
happened last week at the Cyclorama building (Melb.),
when Scotchman Peter M’Gregor, of Sydney, met Buttan
Singh in the catch-hold style, best two of three pin falls.
Peter’s first mistake was in trying for a standing arm-
hold, which failed, and left him on the mat with Singh
master of the situation. Then the dusky one practised a
series of holds that only eastern subtlety could devise.
He first applied a half-nelson and arm hold, Peter
spinning cleverly out, only to get into further difficulties
with a hannnerlock, from which he again managed to
free himself. Buttan, however, kept him moving, even-
tually securing an arm up the back, at the same tune
forcing the lower part of the leg up against the thigh,
what time the victim denounced him as a “ black
blanky cow.” After much wriggling, poor Mac
got out and assumed the squatting position. Buttan,
moving like a copper round a barrel, pulled his nan
down to the restful attitude and subjected him to a
second dose but with both legs forced up, at the same
time making an attempt to grasp the nose and eye.
Being cautioned by the referee lie secured an arm up the
back, gradually levering his man with the combined holds,
and getting both shoulders on the mat in 19 minutes 51
seconds. “Peter” limped to his corner from the
effect of the leg holds—which caused a stoppage
of the circulation —and though he got every
attention from his seconds he fell an easy
victim to the Hindu in the second bout. Unable to
withstand some more foot screwing and leg straining he
lay in a huddled mass on the mat, and was assisted away.
Buttan was top dog from the jump and could have done
the trick without applying holds that even a black man
ought to be ashamed of.
* * * *
“The Stork”: It is evident that Clarence
Weber, notwithstanding his chronic indisposition to get
angry, is the only athlete in these parts capable of
lowering Buttan Singh’s colors. Buttan defeated the
ponderous Pag'd by securing a leg hold, which caused
the huge German to cry enough. Fred Porter was
simply 7 played with and his nose rubbed on the mat, and
latest victim (M‘Gregor) was dealt with in similar fashion
to Pagel. Singh could never do anything with Weber’s
legs, for they are equally as strong as Hackenschmidt’s.
Buttan and Weber have had three wrestles. At their first
meeting Singh was, n the road to lose a tenner, but
managed to avoid the loss by strangling the “white”
after 13£ minutes’ work, just saving his bacon by l£min.
During the contest Singh could do nothing with Weber,
and as for obtaining a leg hold, he had no chance. The
w'hite bo.v merely Shook his leg free every time. In their
second meeting Weber threw the Hindu in 30sec., and
most folks are satisfied he w 7 as robbed of the second fall
after 9min. work by the had judgment of the referee,
who decided against him. The third contest took place
at Fitzgerald’s Circus building (Melb.), and resulted in a
draw after *2£ hours’ uneventful work, the Hindu being
afraid to attack and Weber not having the dash necessary 7
to achieve a victory. Weber has challenged his old oppo-
nent several times lately, but Buttan refuses to toe the
mark. Weber has therefore decided to give him another
chance to accept before claiming the championship.
* * * *
Possible that an Australasian amateur boxing
team will tour England and Europe under the wing of the
N.S.W. Sports Club. Everything depends on the State
and Maoriland Amateur Associations, which are being
sounded on the matter. The proposal lias its origin in
the full exchequer of the N.S.W. Sports Club.
* * i *
The endless duel between Sydney fighters
Montague Andrews and Robert Tin ner resumed at S.yd-
ney National Sporting Club last week. It went the full
20 rounds, and for gore was not in the same street as
Glebe’s match against Auckland City 7 Club. It was a
somewhat rousing spectacle on account of the cheerful
way Turner cut loose from all considerations of personal
safety 7 and rushed in with a whoop to shower blows over
Montague’s iron-clad mug. It looked as though both
men were getting a most fearful basting, but the fact
that they were both on their feet at the end of 20 rounds,
and neither of them at all seriously damaged, suggests
that there was a lack of weight about those whirling
blows, and that the fight was a feather-pillow scrap after
all. The verdict went to Andrews on points.
* * * #
“Snell”: The termination of the big Mel-
bourne battle, Arthur Cripps o. Ed. Williams, was a
pathetic demonstration of the extent to which the poor
pug is at the mercy of the referee. In my experience, I
have found referees quite as liable to lose their heads as
the combatants are, and in this instance I am convinced
that it was the referee who lost his nut, and not the
scrapper. The fight was one of the cleanest and cleverest
ever seen in Melbourne, and although it was all go from
gong to finish it never degenerated into a butchery. It
was marked by 7 clean, clever hitting and scientific
evasion throughout, and proved .young Williams to be
just within reach of the middleweight championship of
Australia. It was a vastly superior fighter who faced
Cripps to the wingy youth who had given Blackmore and
Johns a hustle for the money in Sydney rings, as Arthur
found out when straight lefts and sudden tricky rights,
coming from all parts, began to play on his dial. The
local boy was a lap ahead on points, although Cripps was
playing like a workman on his barrel almost throughout
the struggle. When in the 15th Arthur rushed Ed. in the
latter’s corner, Williams side-stepped, and got an arm
across with a bit of swing in it, Cripps’ feet skidded on
the wet hoards, and he fell on the ropes, rebounded, and
was spread on the floor, where he remained, like the
cunning tactician he is, pending a word from the referee.
Stokesberry said it was poultry, and Arthur’s luck was in.
He took the bigger thrashing, but he took the bigger
wedge of the gate too. The betting was 2tol on Arthur,
but if they meet again prices will run the other way, I
think.
The O’Neill’s scrap with Doc Morris, at Sydney
Gaiety, on Saturday night, moved a fairly large crowd in
a variety of manners. At the lead-off the O’Neill caught
Doc a few heavy ones between wind and water that were
calculated to bring on an attack of indigestion. The Doc,
however, although obviously uncomfortable, managed to
land a few at random that had a steadying effect, and at
last pulled himself together and waded in to knock off
O’Neill’s accumulation of points. But time was too
short, and his triumphal slog' was cut short by the arrival
of the 20th round, and a cold decision that there was still
a credit balance to O’Neill’s account.
* * * *
Road race men will be interested to learn that
the Dunlop Rubber Co. is making a special feature of
its road-racing detachable cover for the present road-
racing season. The cover is constructed of the finest
fabric, the walls of which are protected from cuts and
abrasions by a thin skin of pure rubber. On the heavy
tread is a light, compressed, non-slipping rubber band.
The tyre is absolutely one of the finest speed productions
the Dunlop Co. has yet put on the market, and is sure
to meet with the approval of the road cracks, on account
of its speed, lightness and strength. The covers weigh
3loz. per pair. It was on similar specially made covers
that the Arnst brothers put up such wonderful rides
in the Warrnambool-Melbourne road races.*

THESE marvellous “ RADIUM ” CIGAR and CIGARETTE
LIGHTERS are just about the most ingenious little contrivances
that have ever been invented. This wonderful appliance con-
sists of a small, highly-nickelled case measuring only 2J inches long,
as shown in the illustration. The igniting device is simplicity itself :
Some thin Platinum Wire Thread is fitted on to the inner cap, whilst
in the body of the Case is an ordinary Wick which is saturated with
Methylated Spirits. As soon as the top of the Lighter is removed, the
Platinum Thread, being susceptible to the Spirits, at once ignites the
Wick, which continues burning until the top is replaced again.
THE “RADIUM” CIGAR and CIGARETTE LIGHTER is certainly
a most useful article to everybody. Of course its advantages are not
confined to smokers. We all have to use matches frequently :we all
need this grand Pocket Ignition Device. It costs practically nothing
to keep ; just a little Methylated Spirits poured in when required.
The “ RADIUM ” CIGAR and CIGARETTE LIGHTER can only be obtained from us direct,
so you should send in your order now. THE PRICE IS ONLY 2s. 6d. ; 2d. extra for
postage anywhere. If Stamps are sent, you should add 4d. for exchange.
One of our TRICK CIGARETTE CASES is given free to every purchaser of J-dozen or more,
or we will send the two articles anywhere on receipt of Postal Note
for 3s. 6d f or Stamps 3s. 10d.

The Rexer gun has been earning a reputa-
tion in the present S’African war against the
Zulus. The Rexer isn’t a machine gun, but is
almost as good as a Maxim ; it isn’t a rifle, but it
can be carried and worked by one man. In future
battles the Rexer is going to take a big hand.
British Government seems to be genuinely
alarmed at the state of affairs in Egypt. Ihe
trouble there has been long brewing. It actually
began when the old prophet Muhamad lay dying
at El Medinah. Muhamad chose his father-in-
law Abu Bekr to lead the prayers, and then died.
It was held that the selection gave a sort of
shadowy title to represent the Prophet as his
Khalifa, or Vicar, and there was no objection to
Omar the Just succeeding Abu Bekr. Ali, husband
of Muhamed’s daughter Fatima, followed.
Then there were rows and rival Khalifas, and
a dynasty of “Fatimite Khalifas” reigning in
Cairo. That is the origin of the great Muhama-
dan schism between the Shiites, who hold
that Ali and his sons were the only legitimate
leaders in prayer, and the Sunnites. The
Fatimite Khalifas had a pretty good innings
before Saladin overthrew them at the end of the
12th century. In fact, the Druzes of Syria to this
day regard one of them as a divine incarnation.
The Persians, many Syrians, and practically the
whole population of Egypt, are Shiites. The
Turks are Sunnites, and ever since the Amunt
Mehemet Ali and his family have held sway in
Egypt the people have been sullenly discontented.
English rule has made the conditions better,
but they still regard the army as an appendage of
the Khedive, who is, at best, a foreigner, and a
pestilent heretic into the bargain. If the British
had the sense to pack the phantom Khedive off to
Constantinople or Ceylon, or somewhere, and
frankly run the country themselves, they would
have less trouble.
"Written Specially by “ Ralph the Rover."
Once more I grasp the caustic pen
To picture to the minds of men
A figure, neither gaunt nor coarse,
And introduce Miss “Argus ” Sauce,
With Saucy lips for Saint or Sinner,
A welcome guest at every dinner.
To City men on Hades’ brink,
Who cannot spare the time to think ;
To struggling Cocky in the scrub,
This “ Argus ” Sauce improves your grub ;
To all who live on beef and damper,
A bottle should be in your hamper.
Brave battler through the flies and dust,
When thoughts of tucker bring disgust;
With language livid, black and blue,
This Sauce is just the thing for you ;
It makes the rank ramstag merino
Go down like lamb in Riverina.
When further North in tropic zones,
Cold shivering ague rattling bones,
With Barcoo rot, and Barcoo spew,
This “ Argus ” Sauce will pull you through ;
A bottle puts you in the saddle,
The wild-eyed brumby colt a-straddle.
To far outsider, cattle thief,
It gives a zest to stolen beef;
For often have I eaten that
At “ Lingerandie ” and “ Bailgetfat,”
On Cooper’s Creek and Diamantina,
The Thompson River and Georgina.
On Western fields, where from the can
We eat our potted fellow man ;
For “ Argus ” Sauce we humbly pray,
To take the human taste away ;
Here Pharisee and good Samarian
Are equally human-eat-arian.
Now, in conclusion, man’s a muff
To patronise imported stuff ;
lie starves our Native industries
To fatten those beyond the seas,
And, though he thinks himself in clover,
He wins the curse of “ Ralph the Rover.”
If your storekeeper cannot supply “Argus”
Sauce, send us a post-card, and we will see that
you are supplied. Blogg Bros., Melbourne.

BEALE
in Australia.
PIANO*S
are made in Australia by Australians for Australia, under patented inventions which
you cannot obtain in any other Pianos. And they are expensively made—and, what is
more, they are most expensively made in the very parts which do not show. The
public demand the best that is reasonably obtainable; the merchant demands the
cheapest, always cutting out something that he thinks the buyer will not give him credit
for—things the buyer can neither see nor know. Our aim and object in selling direct
from factory to user is to give the buyer all possible points in his favor. Necessarily,
this costs money, and so, we repeat, BEALE PIANOS are expensively made. We use
only the very best materials ; we employ only the highest skilled artisans, each man a
specialist at his particular work. AH this means money. BUT, in selling direct from
factory to user in Australia, avoiding 20 per cent, duty, freight, exchange, European
agent’s charges, and the importer’s enormous profit, we make enough saving to cover
this expense, and the price to the consumer for absolutely the Best Pianos on the
market is no more than he would pay for second-grade imported goods.

• • Charlie ” McDonald, the new Chairman
~f Committees in the House of Reps., is said to be
the best authority on Parliamentary practice in
the Commonwealth. With him Parliamentary
law is more than a hobby —it is a passion. Years
when he was first elected to Queensland Par-
liament, his vigorous opposition to the party in
i tower led to his being cast out for disorder on
several occasions. He then resolved to read up
Parliamentary practice, with a view to getting
his sav in, or out, without falling foul of the rules
of the House. In a manner he has learned the
rules by breaking them, and holds the record as
a suspended legislator for Australia.
A long recess has its economical as well as
political uses for the heads of Parliamentary,
parties. Premiers nowadays are not so generous
as those of the past, but, however frugal they are,
a sitting House means more expense than one in
recess. In old times the things Ministers did in
the way of food and drink to keep an Assembly
together for a long sitting and a big fight were
memorable. Dibbs spent as much as £6O in a
night, and Parkes, one all-night sitting, owed
£BO for hospitality to his followers. Barton paid
£3O from time to time for the incidentals of a late
sitting, while Robertson spent about three-fourths
of his salary on the members about him.

There seems to be a settled conviction that
when Joe Ward returns from London and takes ■
oyer the Premiership (if Hall-Jones doesn’t change j
his mind in the meantime), the new captain will
promptly jettison dismal Jimmy Duncan, who at
present bosses the Lands, and “U.S.S. Co.”
Mills, who runs the Customs. Jimmy is expected
to go overboard quietly, but Mills is made of
sterner stuff, and will probably object to being
dropped into the whale.
Repdut o* the Australian Senate Standing
Orders Committee for 1905 embodies some inter-
esting decisions. It is not in order to describe a
Senator as “a political jelly-fish,” or his language
as “absolutely false,” or “ horrible,” or “dis-
graceful.” You mustn’t accuse him of cowardice,
or hypocrisy, or untruth. To describe the Premier
of a State as a “bandit ” is improper ; and so on.
N.S. Wales would break its heart under such
rules. Scarcely an Assembly sitting passes lately
without the use of language which ought to be
written down on paper and handed up to the
magistrate at the end of a pair of tongs.
Maoriland, in addition to having the
longest Premiership on record, has some records
in the way of short ones. It had two successive
Ministries which lasted 26 days between them
(Bell-Sewell 13, and Fox 13). It had two more
successive Ministries that lasted 18 days between
them (Stout-Vogel 12, and Atkinson 6). Two of
its shortest-lived Ministries were run by the same
man ; Atkinson who lasted 12 days on one occa-
sion and 6 days on the other. Also Maoriland
would have the shortest Ministry in Australasia
if Dawson, with his 6 days’ record in Queensland,
hadn’t tied with Atkinson. Tasmania’s best
record for brevity was Weston’s 17 days’ Premier-
ship. S. Australia is full of short-distance records
—Baker, 11 days; Torrens, 29; Waterhouse, 9;
Dutton, 11 ; Ayers, 13; Hart, 19 ; Ayers, 21;
Strangway, 18 ; and Sudden Solomon, 7~. Queens-
land had two sprint Premiers. Herbert, 18 days;
Dawson, 7 days. Neither N.S. W., Victoria, nor
Westralia ever produced a real short-distance
Premier.
“ Flap ” puts in a word of praise :
You have been giving it to Panton, P.M., lately, and
deservedly, but you go too far in demanding that he
should retire. You want to watch Panton over a series
of years, as I have, to judge him properly. Then you
would know that, although he is alleged by some to have
every fault a magistrate should not have, he is the best
man sitting on any Vic. Bench. This sounds paradoxical,
but it is true. A casual visit to Melbourne City Court
makes an onlooker leave horrified and indignant that such
injustice should be done. A dozen visits satisfy him
that, in spite of ail his glaring faults, the old man is a
mighty fine man for the billet. Panton’s faults are
shocking. He forgets the law in his haste. I have seen him
impose sentences in excess of those which the statutes
allow. He is full of strange and unaccountable preju-
dices. For a man to say he comes from Sydney appears
to be equivalent to a plea of guilty. He looks with an
over-lenient eye on thesassiety offender, and in nine cases
out of ten shows extra bias in the direction of accepting
police evidence. In the tenth case, he is even worse in
the opposite direction. There are some constables who,
in the opinion of police court habitues, could not obtain
a conviction before Panton were they supported by the
sworn testimony of the Angel Gabrial. One day, a ‘man
was arrested trying to sell gold watches in the street.
Taken to the watchhouse on suspicion, he was found to
have every pocket filled to the brim with second-hand
and unaccounted-for jewellery. Next morning’, the police
wanted a remand for further inquiries, but when the
accused said that he had got the jewellery from another
man, who asked him to sell it, Panton raked the police
fore and aft, and demanded why this reputable citizen
should have been dragged away from his honest employ-
ment. He was discharged without a stain on his charac-
ter, and the jewellery was restored to him. Investiga-
tions, which the police made for their own amusement
afterwards, showed that the reputable citizen had a
record in other States of half-a-dozen sentences. Another
weakness of Panton’s is that his sentences are dispropor-
tionate to his remarks. He is liable to compliment a man
who uses his fists for following the noble British fashion
of pugilism, and then send him to gaol for three months.
The Dago sailor who slices open his mate’s scalp may
receive a lecture upon the use of the knife in a British
country that makes him think he is going up for 10 years.
Then he may be fined ss. These are Panton’s faults. His
virtues are indescribable, but they are there. He knows
the criminals of Melbourne as well as he knows the detec-
tives. He is an excellent judge of character when he
doesn’t rush away in tow of a sudden prejudice, and very
seldom goes wrong in his estimate as to whether a wit-
ness is speaking truly. Panton’s rough-and-ready methods
result in better justice than would be administered by
many a better man. Only one better police magistrate
than Panton has adjudicated in Victoria of recent years
—G. 0. Morrison, now Under-Secretary.

Mr. Minges resides in Brighton, N.Y., and has
devoted the best part of his life to studying and

large company, composed of Brighton’s leading

Thus Harwood, of the Victorian House of
Rich Landlords :
It is not an unmixed good to raise our loans locally.
The country was never so prosperous as when id was
borrowing money abroad at 3 or even 4 per cent.
And the farmer is never so prosperous as when
spending the money for which he has mortgaged
his land and plant. And the tired man in the
burst boots and the pair of trousers made of a
stolen corn sack, who still wears a monocle and a
lisp, was never so prosperous as when doing-in the
boodle he got from the Jews on the strength of
his splendid expectations. The fact that the Jews
now possess the splendid realisations, and the
temporarily prosperous man possesses only the
aforesaid hessian trousers, doesn’t have any sig-
nificance to a person with a towering intellect like
that on which Harwood, M.L.C., balances his
sacred belltopper.
Cape Colony lias decided to give a tariff
preference to Britain and to any British possession
w hich will give Cape Colony a preference in
, . . , , . .
return. As an essay on the right way to give a
preference there are many points about the new
Cape tariff. Bor instance, the duty on candles used
to be Jd. per lb. all round. Now the duty against
British candles is per lb., and the preference
is arrived at by making the duty on imported non-
British candles about |d. In every case the pre-
ference to Britain is given, not by reducing the
duty on British imports, but by raising that on
non-British imports, and sometimes by raising
both of them, only giving the non-British the
larger rise of the two. If Australia ever goes in
for a preference system, that is the idea to
follow. It would make three gradations. First,
as the most favored person, the white British sub-
jeefc iii Australia; second, the white Briti. v
subject in the United Kingdom; third, the rest
of the globe.

Are you troubled with a nasty, irri-
tating, and often painful cough every time
the cold weather comes round ? A tickling
in the throat, tightness in the region of the
breast-bone, loss of voice on rising in the morning,
heavy and labored breathing, sometimes giving rise to a sense
of suffocation ? Do you instinctively fear the cold, damp,
foggy days ? Are you very susceptible to chill ? Are you
obliged to cough incessantly in church or at public meetings;
and do you often feel a desire “to clear your throat”?
If any of these things trouble you, Bronchitis has
claimed you for a victim.
Bronchitis drains the vigor of the strong and permanently
cripples the aged and weak. It kills off more old people than
any other single disease. Hence the importance
of speedily adopting some safe and certain treat-
ment possessing a direct influence on the actual
tissues of the throat and lungs, which are the
tissues affected.
This is essentially the treatment afforded by Peps.
As they dissolve on the tongue, Peps emit all the
rich pine fumes with which they are stored; and these
pine essences, as the breath is taken in, bathe the mem-
branes of the throat and bronchial tubes. Peps (unlike
liquid medicines, which are merely swallowed into the stomach)
thus bring a powerful antiseptic and healing agency into
direct contact with the tissues injured by the incessant
coughing. These pine fumes quickly subdue the inflammation
which is the beginning of Bronchitis, and repair the injured mem-
branes; while the accumulation of phlegm (which prevents the easy
passage of air between lungs and mouth) is more easily got rid of, and
the delicate air-tubes are strengthened and fortified against the perils of
these changeable, lung-chilling and feet-wetting days.
Peps bring relief in the most advanced cases of Bronchitis, and if persevered in
will so repair and strengthen the tissues that the disease is entirely dfiven from
the system. No longer are the lungs worn and torn by incessant coughing.
No longer is the breath obtained with difficulty; and no longer is there oppres-
sion, pain, or tightness about the chest.
Peps are an exceedingly effective medicine, safe for the most delicate adult
and the youngest child. They are a concentration of the richest pine essences
in combination with certain other pure medicine of great healing value in lung,
throat and chest disease. They may be taken at any hour and in any quantity,
and can only do good. A mother only does her duty when she administers
Peps to herself or children upon the first sign of wheezing, barking, hoarse-
ness, difficult breathing, or throat inflammation. No home should be
without a box of Peps.

lung-chilling—one moment warm, and humid the next.

The Inky Way.
The old-established London magazine. Good Words,
which used to be one of the few religious periodicals
worth subscribing to, has been taken over by the in-
satiable Harmsworth. Before it started on a gradual
career of degeneration, Good Words abounded with such
c mtributors as Jean Ingelow, Henry Kingsley, Gerald
Massey, William Black, and George M'Donald. But its
chief glory was the wonderful black-and-white work of
Sandys, Houghton, Lawless, Burne-Jones, Millais, Pinwell,
l’ettie, Walker, and other great illustrators. That, how-
ever, was a long time ago. Under the new management
the publication’s star author is the Rev. Joseph Shocking,
who has gained notoriety by turning out “ Flames of
Smithfield ” horrors, and some of the illustrations from
Harmsworth’s art-foundry are by a man who has exactly
three faces in stock —one for his men, another for
his women, and the third for any animal of the lower
creation which chances to be about.
* * * »
Took a first-class single for the Unexplored the other
day at Frankston (Vic.)—William Fisher, who for many
years bossed the Post at Devonport, Tasmania. Fisher
probably employed about half the journalistic population
of two States during his connection with that paper, for
it was only in rare cases that his reporters stayed with
him for more than a fortnight. He was bluff and blunt, and
would as soon have told the Seventh Ned to go to as
he would the office boys. He was a master in the use of
language, and could swear a bullocky speechless. Under
his reign the Post was counted the Toriest of Tory rags,
and the man who made the most headway with Fisher
was he who could invent the most extravagant account
of the doings of the “ Socialists.” Fisher was suc-
ceeded in the chair by H. O. Sexton, and since then the
paper has mended its ways.

Mr. W. S. Murphy writes thus from Croydon (N.Q.):
“In a recent issue of your paper, a correspondent,
referring to the destruction of the News office by fire,
and to the fact that the insurances totalled £BOO, asked,
‘ What was in the office, anyway ? ’ The insurance was
made up as follows: Building, £230; Ingle’s printing
machine, £100; Arab jobbing machine, £4O ; Otto oil
engine, £lOO ; guillotine, £3O ; type, £l2O ; stock, £lOO ;
furniture and fittings, £45 ; Albion press, £25 ; perforator,
stitching machine, etc., £lO. There were 250 cases of
type in the office. Your correspondent talked about the
class of printery on the Croydon goldfield thirty years
ago, but gold was first discovered here in ISB6 by
YV. C. Brown and the Aldridge brothers. Brown, who
made much money in the early days of the field’s history
and lost it again in mining, is still (at seventy years
of age) working a show here. My new plant has
arrived, and the News .will be running again in about
a fortnight.” [This correspondent seems to issume an
artificial lack of humor as an excuse to work in his
artful cheap advt. The humorous maunderings of
an alleged press patriarch are always liable to be
loose on dates, and are expected to be so. And the
off-hand manner in which the Croydon News man seizes
the opportunity to enumerate his stock, fixtures, fittings,
and his up-to-date plant generally, including the per-
forator, shows him to be a man of business. —Ed. B.]

“ U.”: To gain a profound disrespect for the average
church, one only needs to put in a term as advertisement
clerk on a daily paper, as I once did. The moneyed
church easily takes the bun for advertising meanness and
trickiness. Deacons of Bethels that pay the shepherd
about £25 a week come whining round the Waily View’s
premises until the manager agrees to insert their weekly
shilling ad. for sixpence. (The dailies accept almost any
meanness from a big church, and take it out of the
ordinary unsanctified advertiser.) Then Deacon Peck-
sniff makes all of the allotted number of- words
long ones, like Antinomianism and undenominationalism.
\\ r hen the churches are going to contribute portion of
the offertory to the Hospital Sunday fund some of them
have the brazen effrontery to ask for a further reduction
“ for charity’s sake.” And the big sanctuaries get it,
but the poor little suburban Ebenezer has to pay full
rates every time, unless it happens to have a rich im-
porter on its Board of elders.
* * * *
S.M. Herald has recently been keeping up the jubilee
of the Hunter-street office and giving some account of its
previous home in Lower George-street. The genesis and
the homes of the Herald are as follows : founded in 1831
by Stephens, Stokes and McGarvie, first home in Lower
George immediately opposite Essex-street, then known as
Gallows Hill (that building still stands though much alter-
ed.) In 1833, Stephens and Stokes (McGarvie, a bookseller,
retired) moved the Herald office to No. 5 King-street
East. In 1835 Stephens and Stokes brought the Herald
printing office to Lower George-street, opposite Queen’s
Place (recently re-named Dalley-street). In the early
forties the “ home ” was across the road in Underwood’s
Buildings, with Kemp and Fairfax as proprietors. In
1856 the S.M. Herald came to Hunter-street, and has
stayed there ever since.
“Billy” Donald, formerly of Bathurst Advocate (a
Lithgow boy), now managing editor and part proprietor
of the China Mail (Hongkong), and Lionel Pratt, another
Australian, the sub-editor of the same paper, have
brought out a “ Who’s Who in the East,” which is selling
well. Donald represented the Telegraph and various
English papers in Tokio during the early stages
of the Sake-Vodka war, but . could not get
away to the front. He wrote to the Sydney
paper, announcing his arrival in Japan, and stating
something to the effect that henceforth his path would
lie in the shadow of crossing swords. When the Japs
kept him out of the shadow for three months, he went olf
in disgust ; but later made a scoop in French China, when
he sent telegrams to the English press about how the
Baltic fleet at Ramram Bay was making holes in inter-
national law large enough to throw billy-goats through.
Pratt, more patient, got away eventually to Manchuria
with the Japs, and did some good stuff for the S.M. Herald.
* * * *
E. S. Cunningham, who succeeds David Watterston in
the editorial chair of Melbourne Argus, is a Tasmanian,
and began his inky career in 187(1 on the Hobart
Mercury, which has been a great nursery for journalists.
He joined Melbourne Age reporting staff a few years
later, and passed over to the Argus as leader of the
staff in 1884. When Vic. Irrigation Commissioners Deakin
and Dow went dredging for information among the
waterworks in the United States, Cunningham accom-
panied them and wrote up the damp pilgrimage for
S.M. Herald and Argus. Also, he represented the two
“leading Australian dailies” at the London Colonial Con-
ference in 1887, and “did” Queen Victoria’s Jubilee
junketting for the Argus in ’97.
* * * *
A story of N.Q. journalism : The editor, J. Smith, was
in the habit of gazing on the wine when it was rich and
rare. At one period of the paper’s existence, it didn’t
make its appearance for two consecutive numbers. The
proprietor found that his editor was very drunk, and
was lying in No. 3 room at the Diggers’ Doom Hotel.
The proprietor edited the next number himself, and
printed a long obituary column, containing various intel-
ligences about J. Smith. Two hereunder: —
“ We regret to learn of the death of our editor and
esteemed townsman, Mr. John Smith, who passed away
in room No. 3, at the Diggers’ Doom, at 4 a.m. to-day.
K.1.P.”
“ The friends of Mr. John Smith will kindly attend his
funeral which moves from No. 3 room at the Diggers’
Doom at 3 o'clock this afternoon. Gone, but not for-
gotten.”
He also printed the biography of John Smith, in which
it was stated that J.S. spent most of his latter years in
suffering a recovery in room No. 3, Diggers’ Doom Hotel.
John Smith recovered, so the proprietor printed an ex-
planatory par in the next issue.
“ We are glad to inform our readers that our editor and
esteemed townsman, Mr. John Smith, wasn’t dead (in the
proper sense of the word) after all, but was only dead
drunk in room No. 3, Diggers’ Doom Hotel. We must
apologise for disappointing the many friends of the
(alleged) deceased.”

“Ejayem”: N.S.W. Parliamentary jubilee revived
recollection of James 11. Palmer, the first official short-
hand writer in N.S.W. and probably in Australia;
for he was appointed in 1844. The ancient
came from the great Gurney office, which was
famous for its shorthand writers before Pitman was
even heard of. In the old days the Gurney men in
the gallery of the Commons were noted for their
mechanical accuracy; Dickens was one of the best
of them. The Vic. Government shorthand writers’ staff
was all Gurney. Hemming, Webb, and George Bell
(afterwards chief clerk at the Treasury) preceded present
chief H. E. Wade. All used the quill— ttiere are no thick
and thin strokes in Gurney, and, once acquired, it’s easy
to read. If one man gets a load of stuff, the others can
share the notes. Wade, after he became chief, did a lot
of this. About 12 years ago a retrenching Govern-
ment proposed to amalgamate the Goverment short-
hand writers’ office and Hansard. Of course,
neither of the two recording shops liked the idea.
Shortly after this squabble a vacancy occurred on Han-
sard, and George Octavious Webb (son of the former
chief), who was third man on the Government shorthand
staff, reckoned he’d get out of the austere officialdom of
the office, and find a more congenial atmosphere at Parlia-
ment House. But Parliamentary reporting is not a
matter of mere shorthand-writing. A man must be
trained at the graft. Webb, excellent in his
own line, was not only unaccustomed to the
menagerie business, but found (as almost all men
do in a new gallery) the greatest difficulty in learning.
So he got flurried and lost his head. E. B. Loughran, as
usual with a new man, took a check note. Webb after-
wards returned to the Gurney office. A somewhat simi-
lar unlucky experience befell Johnnie Briggs, who,
after he left the Age, went on Hansard under
Loughran. Both went down on “ nerve.” The
return of Webb to the Government shorthand writers’
office caused great consternation. The reputation
of the establishment, and of Gurney, was challenged.
Fowell Buxton Lincoln, second in command and a perfect
hammer- and-tongs note-taker, stepped into the breach
to do or die for Gurney. At this time a permanent
change was made in the Hansard accommodation, which
gave fair play to the new man—Hansard men were
allowed to take their notes at the table of the House,
and there Lincoln’s quills screeched and recorded the
proceedings with top-notch accuracy. Loughran was
good enough to sweetly congratulate Lincoln on
“ being able to do work up to our standard.”
It would be hard to say who was the best man turned out
of the Gurney office. George Bell was perhaps unsur-
passable. Even in hell-fire work he correctly placed his
dots, and in the days before the typewriter came into
vogue he would arrange his notes in three separate sets
and keep three longhand men industriously plodding
together at dictation, the connection with the notes
being maintained by the dictatee giving the catchword.
Wade, on a fairly decent job, made a copperplate
note, and denoted his periods by a small space—
a great assistance in transcribing. Lincoln made
a sort of unvarying corrugated iron note, and if the others
were perplexed over any of their own records, he could
pick them up and elucidate them with astonishing ease.
The late Judge Webb (brother of Hemming Webb) was
also a Gurney man. After he came to Australia, the
Argus held a competitive examination, so as to select a
first-class shorthand writer. He entered, and easily
beat all the others, but as a pressman he hadn’t the
original faculty. The worst that can be said against
the Gurney system is that it requires a greater physical
exertion to write than Pitman. Gurney “ breaks ” more,
and the arbitraiy phraseograms are not so scientifically
worked out as in the universal system created by “ Izak.”
Chief Justice Samuel Griffith carries to
the Bench his aloof and solitary habit of life.
When he was in Queensland politics Mr. Griffith
never haunted the lobbies to yarn with members ;
he strode from the House as soon as business was
over, and was off for a lonely walk or a solitary
ride on his white horse. In Sydney he is seen but
seldom, and is said to take most of his exercise
walking at night, when the push is abroad, and
the derelict is looking for stray valuables in the
ash-barrel.
“Bobby”: Turning over some old files
of a Tasmanian newspaper the other day, I became
aware that fifty years ago some men could have
their own private police force in that happy land.
In this old paper it was announced that Patrick
Michael Hooligan, or something, had been
gazetted special constable to the Roman Catholic
Bishop of Hobart. The clergy of all sorts had
good times then. The only R.C. priest at Hobart
in the twenties and early thirties was Father
Connolly, who was a paid Government chaplain.
The C. of E. chaplain was also a “ beak.” He
sent convicts to gaol, and then ministered to them
in the cells—a useful sort of Government instru-
ment. Well, Rev. Knopwood and Father Con-
nolly were on speaking and eating terms, and
when a man named O’Mulligan or Macßrady
came before his reverence and his worship for a
small crime he used to pass sentence like this :
“Three weeks hard labor in Father Connolly’s
garden.” And so Father' C. had his cabbages
planted, his ground manured, or his praties dug
just in the nick o’ time, gratis and free. Still,
O’Mulligan or Macßrady lived like a fighting cock
while he was at the presbytery, and was a trifle
sorry when he was turned out of gaol.
THE JOURNALIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Burton, who writes for the papers
(He’s written these thirty years),
Finds matter for fun in the capers
Of men—their laughter and tears.
He dwellS'on the latest rumor
That glides through the city street ;
And touches the hour with humor
Till it passes with shining feet.
He cellars the finest sherry
Of any man that I know—
The blood at its touch turns merry,
And it sets the soul aglow.
Burton’s a glorious fellow,
His jests just sparkle and shine ;
He’s fun—pure fun—and as mellow
As a drink of his own good wine.
But ask him to write a leader,
And mark how he changes then ;
He bows to my lord—his reader—
And writes with a reverent pen.
His wits that galloped at pleasure
Are slowed in their headlong race ;
His thoughts to a solemn measure
Troop by at a seemly pace.
lie writes for an august paper,
Whose grand, thought-shaping power
Is still like a vagrant vapour
That passes within the hour.
Yet while in a diction level,
He curbs his fancy-flights ;
He says that a lurking devil
Sits by him the while he writes.
While pleading for worthless causes,
lie hears its laughter rise ;
And when in thought he pauses,
It stares him in the eyes.
Burton is fond of joking,
He is brave, and yet I feel
His work is somehow choking
His manhood, true as steel.
To waste one’s soul for ever—
Its beauty and its youth—
To write and to write, and never
To write one’s inmost truth.
And yet the little devil
Who laughs at power and pelf
May lure him from the level,
Straight path—to write himself.
I picture the frenzied caper—
The antic weird and droll—
Of the owners of that paper—
Should Burton voice his soul.
N.S.W.

ONE YEAR OF MISERY
Was Ended by the Use of Only Three Bottles of
Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
WHAT A NORTH RICHMOND WOMAN
SAYS OF HER ILLNESS AND CURE.
The North Richmond Hotel, in Victoria-street,
North Richmond, Victoria, is presided over by
Mrs. Violet A. Cohen, a young married woman,
who is a native of Victoria. Before entering on
business life she lived in the country, had the
benefit of the fresh air and food which that life
affords, and was a hearty, healthy woman. After
taking up city life she fell ill of indigestion, and
suffered great pain and distress for qver a year.
Finally she was quickly and inexpensively cured—
by using Mother Spigel’s Syrup —as she tells in a
letter, dated November 15th, 1905.
She says : “For 12 months I suffered severely,
was always sick after eating, and had a sour,
bitter stomach. I became weak, and pined away,
losing 22 pounds’ weight in eight weeks. I was
never free from pains, and was very low spirited
and nervous. I tried medical treatment, but got
no permanent relief. Then I tried a well-known
blood mixture, and also took massage and Turkish
baths, at an expense of several guineas a week.
About six months back my husband persuaded
me to use Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and I obtained
more relief from it, within 24 hours than from
all the other things I had tried, and three bottles
quite restored me to my former good health,
condition and spirits.”
Mother Seigel’s Syrup has been known all
over the world for 35 years, and has cured
Indigestion, Biliousness, Constipation, and all
disorders of the Stomach, Liver, Kidneys and
Bowels, where all other remedies failed.
