Business, Robbery, etc.
•J-A.C. : I was surprised the other day. In
the simplicity of my nature I had not considered that
our laws were framed for the protection of swindlers. A
young fellow I know was engaged to drive a delivery cart
tor a purveyor of the stale and indigestible bun. He
worked from early morning till late at night, one
Saturday. That he performed his duties in a
satisfactory manner is evident from the fact that
he was told to return on Monday morning. Mean-
while “Buns” ran across a man he knew, and
en ff a ff e d him, so that when the youth presented him-
self on Monday he was incontinently sacked. He
asked for his wages, for the day he had worked, and “ Not
a condemned bean ” was all the satisfaction he got. Not
having any money to take out a summons, he applied in
forma pauperis to a chamber magistrate for one. The
O.M. refused to grant him a summons unless he got
a recommendation from a J.P. or a minister of
the gospel. I have reason to believe that the
C.M. was riding the high horse on this occasion, for I
have known instances where summonses have been
granted in similar cases without cavil or formality. In
any case, why a minister of the Gospel any more than a
butcher or a baker ; these latter are frequently more
respectable. However, the youngster got a recommen-
dation from a J.P., and returned to the magistrate, who
still refused the summons—this time on the ground that
the case did not come under the Master and Servant's
Act, but under the Factories Act, or Boiler Explosions
Act, or something equally absurd. Now, I want to know
why a concocter of legendary pies should be protected
and allowed to go scot-free while a vendor of the un-
assuming cabbage would probably have been mulct in the
amount claimed and ex’s.
* * * *
“Stony”: As to the American tinned-dog
“ scandals ” : I have had the savory experience of work-
ing in more than one U.S. A. packing-house, and 1 cer-
tainly saw plenty in those days to make me disinclined to
think deeply when eating wursts—or any other kind of
harmless sausage. The chief trouble was the lack of com-
plete cleanliness. One began working again where one
left off the day before, as it were. You find the same
thing with our Australian dairymen and their scalded
pails. I think there is nothing more dreadful than the
odor of a nominally “cleaned” milk-pail. The new milk
covers a multitude of odors. There are some pretty sights
in a packing-house. I remember that one floor—there were
a great many floors—was entirely devoted to the contents
and trimmings of various animals. These contents were
duly sorted out, and the more desirable portions were
sent down divers chutes to the floor below'. I have stood
near a chute and had a whole stream of bullocks’ livers
rain down upon the floor just beside me ; and in another
place hearts, and further along pigs’ trotters, and so on.
That floor w'as never quite, quite snowy-pure.
♦ * * *
Port Phillip is to have its first double-ended
screw boat in a few weeks time. The old paddle steamer
Gem, ferrying between Port Melbourne and VVilliams-
town, is to be replaced by a double-ended screw launch
now being built in Sydney. The Queen, which was sent
round from Sydney six years ago to run in opposition to
the Gem, is undergoing alterations. Its paddles are to
be taken away, and it is also to be fitted with a propeller
at each end. The Gem has easily earned the old-age pen-
sion, having been on the Port to Williamstown run since
1869.
* * * ■*
“ Americano While the police are gammon-
ing to look after Rockefeller, 1 take up a unique
book by a wonderful woman, Ida Tarbell. She gathers in
two vols., altogether 800 pages, her articles from M’Cmjrb
on the Standard Oil Trust. They are masterly, reminding
me of Chief Justice Coekburn’s enormous judgment on
the Tichborne case for the logical generalising and con-
sideration of such a bewildering array of facts. In 1860
the great Pennsylvania oil region was a wild forest,
from which the lumbermen floated their logs
on the Allegheny River to Pittsburg. It will be
remembered how J. O. Williamson came with the
play of “Struck Oil,” when the industry was
yet pretty young. Poor men were glutted and‘gorged
with fortunes far beyond the dreams of their avarice,
though miserable little ant-heaps compared to what the
bosses were to make. Professor Sillman, the eminent
chemist, reported favorably on the oil in 1855. This led
one Drake to put down the first bore at Titusville in 1859.
Then the furore began. The region was transformed
with derricks, towns and railroads, and ultimately came
those amazing pipes for conveying oil without the pre-
liminary trouble of casking or tinning. Rockefeller
early appeared on the scene. Ida Tarbell does not
know whether to compare his operations to those
of Napoleon or the Roman Catholic Church. She gives
the most striking and complete view on record of this
malign genius. Her work opens one’s eyes to the stu-
pendous difficulties which have been overcome by Rocke-
feller and his associates, and you perceive that a myriad
of first-rate men have been engaged. Cannot we per-
ceive, too, how the Trusts are shaping up to give a handle
for nationalisation ? Why should the oil in the earth, the
gold, the coal, the iron, belong to the men who only
bought the surface to scratch? If you buy a mile of land,
do you buy 4000 miles, right to the earth’s centre ? If so,
Spreckels and co. bought the earthquake.
* * * fr
A Melbourne tailor, whose customers are not of
the “ working ” class, surprised the Tariff Commission
somewhat the other day. Having assured his hearers
that Australian products were superior to imported,
price for price, he complained that the local cloth manu-
facturers handicapped him and themselves by refusing to
supply him except through a wholesale house —through
“ the Lane.” He could purchase direct from the biggest
mills 14,000 miles away, but he could not buy from the
mills at his own door. He wanted to do away with the
middleman. He was then asked: “Do you think the
mills could reach the public direct by doing their own
tailoring?” “Yes; but I believe the best solution of
the difficulty is that the State should control the indus-
try.” State Socialism finds friends in strange places.
When the N.S.W. Government announced that
it had purchased 4000 miles of wire-netting from British
manufacturers direct, the local importers were ex-
ceedingly wrath. Their idea was apparently to purchase
the same netting from the same makers, and sell it to
Carruthers for anything over £lO a mile profit that they
could manage to get. One individual announced, when
he heard the Government’s decision, that he and his clique
were going to give all their future orders “ to the Ger-
mans.” Remembering the enthusiasm of the mercantile
community only last Empire Day on the subject of
“loyalty to the empire,” it is a terrible spectacle to find
any members of that body deliberately proposing to
damage British industry, and support the industry of
Britain’s worst enemy, apparently just because British
manufacturers used their indubitable right as free citizens
to sell their goods to any cash customer who came along.

“ Tip ” : “ Mirumbeck ” (B. 21/6/’O6) sighs dole-
fully because of the decline of the watch-mending trade
since people took to wearing cheap tickers, which they
shy at a dog as soon as they stop going. What established
the popularity of the cheap time-keepers? The practice
of the watch-mender of charging 10s. 6d. for touching a
wheel with an oiled feather to remove a speck of dust
under pretence that all the works required cleaning, or
that the mainspring was broken. lam glad the trade of
the watch tinkerer is not what it used to be.
* * * *
The “brum” jewellery industry, which the
Commerce Act is designed to suppress, has had a long
run in Australia. So long ago as June 21, 1856, an action
was tried in Sydney to recover the value of certain
gewgaws described as “colored fine gold,” which phrase
was said to mean solid gold. By accident it came out
that the goods w ere really “ lead or composition, plated
with gold, so artistically enclosed as to defy detection
except by cutting.” As there was no Conmerce Act in
existence at the time, the luckless buyer lost his action
on a point of law, and had to carry' his wretched collec-
tion of “ brum ” away with him.

Mr. Hearne, Chemist, Geelong'.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago, in Sydney, I suffered from
a severe attack of influenza, and was confined to my room
for about a week, at the end of which time, feeling some-
what better, 1 got up and tried to transact my business
as usual. But 1 got up too soon, for the very next day I
had a relapse, and suffered tortures from what the doctor
told me was pleurisy and bronchitis. The pain from the
former in my chest and shoulders was frightful, and for
four long weeks I was confined to my bed, under the care
of a well-known Sydney doctor, and all the time his medi-
cine gave me but temporary relief. The landlady of the
hotel (the Cleveland) where I resided told me of a medi-
cine—Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure—from Victoria, which had
cured her of a bad attack of bronchitis and pains in the
chest, and begged of me to try it. I did so, and, in thanks
and gratitude to you, tell you that, after the second bottle,
my cough had ceased ; but, what is more astonishing, the
pains from pleurisy entirely left me, and, in about a week,
I was able to attend to my duties as usual. —Yours faith-
fully, J. BRAHAM,

A SATURDAY AT SPATS'.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f INHERE was a sort of dress parade on
the top flat at Spats’ every Saturday. On
ordinary days the Beauties came in their
second best, which was changed on the premises
for paste-stiffened skirts and bedraggled blouses,
any old thing being considered good enough to
work in. But on Saturday the pasters and folders
sported their glad rags, forgetting nothing that
would impress their enemies; and in the bitter
rivalry of dress there were no friendships. On
this day the new hat was flashed upon the factory
like a smack in the face, the unexpected frock
shocked the Beauties with its insolent beauty, the
nine-carat brooch or the glittering ring, set pro-
fusely with precious stones quite as good as
diamonds, was displayed for the first time with a
disregard for the feelings of others that was
simply vindictive. Every girl did her utmost on
Saturday, and, excepting in a few special cases,
-always with the fear that her best possible would
be outdone by a special effort on the part of some
more fortunate sister.
On this particular Saturday morning Kitty
■Coudray had no misgivings. She was wearing the
whole of her new summer’s goods for the first
time, and went up the front stairs, so that she
might have to walk the length of the flat to the
dressing-room. Every woman in an astonishing
frock is at heart a stage-manager. Kitty’s entrance
was a great success, her march down the stage a
triumph. She was radiant in blue and white
muslin, and a saucy little black hat with white
lace and feathers. The girls who had gone before
sighed over their little, faded glories, and meekly
abandoned all pretensions for the time being.
“Oh my, what a turn you’ve give me ! ” cried
the clerk, having fallen off his stool in blind
wonder.
Benno’s amazement, if facetious, was flattering,
and Kitty smiled on him very sweetly. The
packer barred her way.
“Break it t’ me gently,” he said in an awed
voice—“have y’ killed yer rich old uncle ’r
•embezzled the fam’ly di’monds?”
Kitty blushingly insisted that it was only a
simple bit of a dress for second best—nothing to
speak of.
“Yer a fair blaze iv glory,” Mills continued.
“Y’ kin do what y’ like with me. I’m done.
I’ll never get over this, I know.” He was walk-
ing round her, humbly inspecting.
“ Yah-h-h,” cried a derisive voice from a distant
board, “stick ’er in the bloomin’ winder, so’s give
ther town er charnce ! ”
Kitty stiffened at once, her nose went up, and
she walked on with an increase of dignity, but
not really displeased. That cry had come from
a stricken soul. It was really a tribute. She
would not have been wholly satisfied had the girls
taken it all quite kindly.
The Sal vation Army lass at the machine began
to sing a plaintive little hymn. The Army
had had a revival in the cheap suburbs lately,
and had been getting in some good Work amongst
the factory hands. There were two or three con-
verts in the room. They joined in the hymn.
It was the protest of the saints against the
vanities of the flesh. Spotty Corbet, the
machinist, was intensely “saved.” She came to
work in shabby regimentals, and spoke a three
minutes grace overa “crib ” of bread and jam that
it took her two minutes to eat. She was a squat,
sallow girl of about 10, sullen and slovenly, and
had a guilty secret. When not observed she oiled
her greasy dark hair with the machine lubricators.
Kitty Coudray had changed and taken her place
at the board, when Billy the Boy charged upstairs
•crimson with information.
“Wipe yer nose ’n’ -pull down yer pinny,
Mills,” gasped the devil, “ here’s her Excellency
makin’ a mornin’ call.”
TJp the stairs came a faint frou-frou. Feathers
nocked his ear. There was no mistaking it. It was
the musical rattle of silk. The packer ventured
to look over the rail. He blew a long, faint
whistle, expressive of profound emotion. He
beckoned to Goudy in the manner of a man bereft
of speech. There was an apprehensive hush, and
the sibilant call of a silk underskirt stole through
the factory. All eyes turned to the stairs.
“You’ve done yer dash,” cried Billy, addressing
the Beauties. “You kin all git orf the earth.
Twentyman’s got you beat.”
“Jimmy Jee!” whispered Mills, “it’s Bland
’Olt’s wicked woman straight from the halls of
-gilded vice, with all her clobber on.”
Feathers and the town traveller stood back
reverently, giving the newcomer a clear stage,
and as Sis Twentyman stepped on to the flat the
silence of the factory broke in a long gasp.
Nothing like this had ever before been seen at
Spats’. The most riotous imagination there had
never dreamed of such magnificence in a mere
“fact’ry ’and.” Sis was profusely clad in pink
silk, billows and billows of it, trimmed with
cream lace, and she wore a great black picture
hat foaming with feathers, and new glazed shoes
with steel buckles. Kitty Coudray, her brush
clutched in her frozen hand, gazed at this over-
whelming spectacle, her eyes wide, her mouth
-gaping, transfixed. It was a form of paralysis,
and she did not recover consciousness for three
minutes.
Sis remained talking with Feathers. It was
his duty to keep a record of late arrivals who
were fined according to the length of their trans-
-gression. Miss Twentyman made a great show
of excusing herself, but her late arrival was
premeditated malice, and Feathers understood
the situation. Sis was not merely completing her
triumph—she was “rubbing it in.”
“Won thirty thousan’quid in Tatt.’s, ’aven’t
yer?” said the packer, affecting the meekness of
the poor and lowly.
“ If you had let us know you were coming we’d
Lad the stair carpet down and the walls decor-
ated,” said the town-traveller.
Excuse yourself, Mister Goudy, ” replied Sis,
■with a ladylike air of hauteur.
“ Who’re v’ ruinin’, Sis?” asked Feathers.
“Not so familiar, Mister Mills, please.” Sis
gathered up her profuse skirt and swept away, and
half-a-dozen of the Beauties, driven from their
usual refuge of seeming indifference by such splen-
dor, forsook their work, and clattered after her,
shrill with admiration. After these went the
anguished foreman, with his feeble but insistent:
“Come, come ! Come, come, there. What’s
•this ? What’s this ?”
The girls buzzed about Sis, deaf to Fuzzy’s
-orders and his threats, and Spotty Corbet drove
her machine fiercely, and piped her hymn of pro-
test- once more.
Mills aired his philosophy of clothes to Goudy.
“Take it from the man under the bed, Scotty,
wimmin’s mostly clothes, an’ ther rest rings an’
-things. Man’s what he drinks; woman's what
she wears. Size up this bunch. On ther ordin’ry
mornin’s in their secon’ best baggin’, they’re sort
o’ subdood an’ moderate perlite, an’ you don’t see
’em buckin’ up, or playin’ ther frivolous ox. Look
at ther difference when they get inter ther rags,
Then they get down to it—they’re cobbers with
ther rats; an’ yer needn’t put much apast ’em.
But watch out fer Satudees when ther latest
season’s goods is out fer the air ; an’ they’re the
real pink, an’ yer must’n touch ’em with er prop
if yer don’t wanter get it cold. Blime, y’d think
Spats’ was er select seminary fer young lydies
iv gentle birth, an’ that ther dorters iv ther aris-
tocracy was here t’ get made fit in French, dancin’,
liaccomplishments, an’ perlite conversation. Mop
up what’s goin’ when Twentyman comes along in
her old stuff.”
When Sis came again, dressed for work, and
carrying her tub of paste, the packer said :
“ S’elp me shicker, Twenty, you was the on’y
pebble. Flemin’ton on Cup Day ! —don’ men-
tion. Ther Mayor’s ball ! —a blessed marine store.
Lovely, y’ was, straight ! ”
“ Gar’n ! le’ go me leg ! ” retorted Miss Twenty-
man, saucily.
“ What’d 1 tell yer ?” said Mills to Goudy.
The Beauties got down to work slowly and re-
luctantly that morning. There was a still later
arrival. Bell Olliver came in quite half-an-hour
behind time, wearing a new blue blouse, a blouse
that would have created some little stir on ordi-
nary occasions, but which was received with abso-
lute indifference, coming after the extravagance
of Kitty and the magnificence of Sis. Bell came
by the front stairs, radiant with expectation,
smiling a self-conscious smile, and blushing
brightly. By the time she reached the packer’s
board the smile had fled, and Bell’s face had
assumed the color of old dough. She turned down
the wing to the dressing-rooms, and as she did not
re-appear in the course of a quarter-of-an-hour, the
foreman went ambling down the flat, full of zeal,
and tripping over his own feet every few yards as
usual, in the agonies preceding an encounter with
a Beauty of Bell’s argumentative power and un-
certain temper.
Fuzzy found Miss Olliver seated behind a stack
of strawboards, wearing her drab working apparel,
her little tub of paste between her feet, weeping
like a wet day.
“ Come, come ! ” he cried, with tremulous valor.
“ Come, come ! Out o’ this ? ”
“Gar’n scratch ! ” retorted the young lady,
drearily.
“What’s this? What’s this? This won’t do,
you know. Get to work, ’r I’ll fine you a shillin’.’)
Miss Olliver jumped up and confronted Fuzzy
suddenly ablaze.
“You tork t’ me !” she screamed, “you, ’r fat
ole Pig’s Whiskers downstairs, ’r any iv yer rats.
Do it! Do it ! Tork t' me, monkey face, ’n’
I’ll”
The foreman recognised the symptoms. He
retreated precipitately, but Miss Olliver’s tub of
paste took him between the shoulders as he fled,
and made his back view absurd for the rest of the
day. Saturday morning was a trying time for
Fuzzy in many ways.
Bell came to her board when it pleased her.
She was an expert paster, and rival firms would
have been glad of her services. Fuzzy had to
rule her and many like her by a system of
judicious submission. For three hours order and
industry prevailed on the flat. Bell was in a fit
of black sulks; Kitty Coudray was sour and
insolent; the other girls had a thoughtful and
depressed air. Sis Twentyman alone was gay.
She was particularly affectionate towards Kitty,
and seemed quite stupidly blind and deaf to Miss
Coudray’s coldness and contempt.
* « * *
Naturally Sis Twentyman was the first in the
dressing-room at knock-off time. Her arrival
there was signalised by a series of shrieks that
stirred the establishment to its lowest depths,
galvanised the factory, and brought the printers
streaming upstairs. Shrieking was not uncom-
mon on the top flat, but never had such poignant
yells pierced its walls. Sis performed like a
woman in mortal agony. Feathers, heading the
rush, discovered her, standing in her petticoats,
holding the pink silk skirt out before her, and
screaming with all her energies in a stupid, blind,
settled way suggestive of madness.
“’Struth, Twenty, what’s bit yer?” gasped
Mills. He shook her as if expecting to re-arrange
a disorganised apparatus. “’Ere, ’ere,” he cried,
“ take a grip on yerself if yer ain’t fair off yer
almond.” But Sis only screamed again, and showed
the pink silk skirt, and now the situation became
comprehensible. The skirt was an utter ruin ;it
was torn this way and that, and the frills and the
lace hung from it in rags.
At this moment Sis Twentyman’s eyes fell upon
Kitty Coudray, in whose face was something like
a light of elation.
“ You ! ” she shrieked. ‘ 1 You—you—you ”
Her passion choked her on the suitable epithet,
and she dashed at Kitty with hooked claws, like a
ravening lioness. But Feathers and the Don
clung to the fury, and averted manslaughter.
The light of elation had fled suddenly from the
eyes of Kitty Coudray. It was succeeded by a
poignant apprehension. She darted into the dress-
ing-room, and the next moment her cries added to
the general confusion. She came forth carrying
her new blue skirt. It was split from waistband
to hem, six or seven times, and was a hopeless
wreck. Kitty glared at Sis in dumb rage for a
moment, and all a woman could possibly feel of
spite and anguish was in her face.
“She did it! ” she cried. “She did it, the
devil ! ” and it took two printers to keep her from
Sis Twentyman. The Salvation girl was singing
“Sound the loud timbrel o’er Egypt’s dark sea,
Jehovah hath triumphed, His people are free.”
Spats himself and other authorities had arrived on
the scene, but their influence was as nothing in the
face of a catastrophe like this. The Beauties
swarmed; their indignation was virtuous but
virulent. They cried aloud for vengeance on the
destroyer. A rush for the dressing-room revealed
the fact that no further damage had been done,
and slowly the factory simmered down. Sis and
Kitty, surrounded by guards, sat apart, and
sorrowed over their tattered garments, soaking
themselves in tears.
It was Feathers suggested the cat : “Ther
Tutsie’s erbout arf ez big ez a barrel,” he said,
“ ’n’ when he gets out after himself in high pressur
hysterics, he fair digs bricks outer ther buildin’.
I’ve seen him kick ther hessian off er bale iv paper
afore the day, ’n’ he ’ad er fit this mornin’. I
’eard him doin’ ’an’ springs ’n’ fizzin’ like er soder
factory back er ther lift. ”
The authorities retired to the depths, and
slowly the girls dressed and drifted down stairs.
Sis and Kitty had come together mutually for-
giving, as sisters in misfortune should be, and
they mingled their tears, pending the conclusion
of a consultation being held in the boss’s office.
Feathers brought the news. The foreman had
gone under cover behind his guillotines, over-
whelmed by the catastrophe, and knowing that
blame, unplaceable elsewhere, invariably fell upon
him.
“It’s rvbuck, girls,” said Feathers. “Yer on
velvet. Ther firm’s willin’ t’ accept responsibility
fer ther actions iv it’s dooly accredited cat, ’n’
pays compensation. Yer on’y ter put in a claim
fer damages ’n’ ther quids is yours.”
Sis and Kitty brightened up wonderfully at
this, but not sufficiently to imply that they had
wholly parted with their grievance.
“Spats has sent fer a ’ansom, si yer to be
wheeled ’ome et ther firm’s expense,” continued
Feathers. Then he cried to Spotty Corbet, the
Salvation girl, whom he had requested to stay,
pending orders, and who was stealing from behind
the bales towards the stairs. “Yer don’t do a
guy jes’ yet, Spot. This is where yer trouble
comes. Back in yer cart. I want ter chat yer.”
The girl came slowly towards them, lowering
under her battered Army bonnet, her sallow face
smeared with tears and dust, her brief, shabby
skirt falling short of its duty. She was an absurd
and pitiful figure. Feathers took her by the
shoulder: '
“Now,” he said, “cough it up. Why’d yer
tear them dresses ? ”
Sis and Kitty sprang to their feet, their eyes
blazed, and they made at Spotty Corbet with
bared, talons. “’Ere, ’ere,” cried the packer,
“keep good. Enough on it.” He put them back,
and addressed Spotty again, “I don't hear y’
talkin’,” he said. “Y’ know yer tore them
skirts, y’ can’t jolly me. Why’d yer do it? ”
Spotty dug a finger in her mouth, and squirmed
for a moment, and then her eyes gleamed and she
stood erect for a fine effort,
“They was offensive in the sight of the Lord ! ”
she said. Then she collapsed again, her finger
went back into her mouth, she began to cry, and
tucking her old skirt out with her knee, she said.
with stupid bitterness, “’N’mine is sichrags!”
“Hum,” said Feathers, philosophically, “I
thought I’d fitted yer. Now do a bunk.”
The packer held the two pasters while Spotty
shuffled to the stairs, and went down, snivelling
all the way. She did not reappear at Spats’.
“Why didn’ yer let me at her?” said Sis,
fiercely. “ I’d'a’scraped her bones.”
“Garn, Twenty, ain’t yer got a bit o’ sav’?
Make a bustle with her, ’n’ ’ave it orl over the
shop she done it, ’n’ ther cat’s bin wrongly
accused, ’n’ how does yer compensation come
in?”
“ Oh-o-o-o ! ” breathed Sis and Kitty in chorus,
very softly,
“ Oh-o-o-o ! ” breathed the packer in mockery,
more softly still.
Feathers was a true diplomat, and had a fine
contempt for accuracy. “’Onesty,” he said, “is
the bust policy.”
EDWARD DYSON.

The Pioneer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE stands beside the wind-swept verge,
The Flint Age just begun,
He sees the gray, incoming surge
Grow red beneath the sun ;
The primal seas befoie him hold
What danger and what fear,
This builder of the aeons old,
This Shipwright Pioneer ?
His knotted hands, with stone and fire
In cloud and shine have wrought
Through days of failure and desire
The rude inventor’s thought—
A tattoed chief, long ages sunk
In far, forgotten night,
Who chose the bole and felled the trunk
And paced its length aright.
Now auroch’s tooth and mammoth tusk,
And greenstone axe have done
Slow service in the falling dusk ;
And with the lifted sun
The sweat of toil, uncouth, sublime,
Has drenched his bare limbs o’er —
This workman in the dawn of Time,
This Titan by the shore.
A Fulton in a wolf-skin dress,
Whose fame no legends tell,
Through travail vast and hourly stress
He held his purpose well.
Bull-chested, savage, heavy-limbed,
Barbaric, past, remote,
His fame in fitting voice is hymned
From ev’ry storm wind’s throat.
The twin screw sings his work divine
Across a peopled sea ;
From Greenland to the circling line
Who worthier than he ?
The turbine’s shaft shall call his deed
Above the charted floors,
And, city-sown, they’ll grant him meed
Of praise, the thousand shores.
Homeric in the Dawn he stands ;
Wild triumph lights his face ;
The Key of Distance in his hands—
An Overlord of Space.
Eyes shaded by a palm of might,
Peering the level plain—
Out lrom the World’s Primaeval Night
He glares, and sinks again !
Builder was he ere Asia knew
Aught of Accadian tents ;
Builder was he ere Egypt grew
Skilled in the rudiments.
Master of Craft ere Rome and Tyre
Sprang from their swamp and fen.
Shaping with flint and bone and fir
The future sailor-men.
A gaunt Columbus in the weird,
Lost infancy of Man,
For him no sculptured stone was reared
By tutored sept or clan.
Painter and sculptor, caid and sage
Owe him neglected praise—
Lord of the Neolithic Age
And Captain of the Ways.
Burnt is his spark ; gone down, long sped
Into unheeded night ;
Dust are his hands, but still men tread
The vintage of their might.
Haply some student ship-yard looms,
Potent with force accrued,
And loud Invention clangs and booms
Where lay his dug-out rude.
Haply the strong cranes lift and bear
Masses of structure shaped
Hard by the salted margins, where
He hewed, and chipped, and scraped ;
Boiler and bulkhead, beam and mast,
Crank and propeller blade,
Swung to their place appointed fast,
All for the good of Trade,
Surely his spirit somewhere hears,
Out in the vastness wide,
Echoes faint of the Newer Years,
Borne o’er the roaring tide.
Surely the white electric gleam
Cleaving the darkness, yet
Lights with its slow, revolving beam,
A strange sea silhouette.
Framed by a night cloud drooping low,
Dark and obscurely dim,
Paddling his frail boat hard and slow,
Out at the dark sea rim—
Once in a hundred years perchance
A watcher by the sea,
One fleeting second’s space may glance
This ancient Mystery.
Bull-chested, bared to combat new,
Soaked with the driving spray.
Muscle and sinew, bone and thew
Fighting their onward way;
Holding the sure coast close inboard,
Hugging the solid shore—
Passeth the great Sea’s Primal Lord
Into the Nevermore.
Father of Builders—as he fades
Over the tither side,
A liner huge with whirling blades
Cleaves outward in her pride !
Father of Sailors—as his ghost
Mists at the rising moon,
Comes the sound of “ Washington Post ”
Played in her grand saloon !
N.s.w.
E. J. BRADY.
“ F.” : In all early colonial documents we
find what we now call “ creek ” designated by the
English term “rivulet.” Can anyone explain
how the “rivulet” merged into the American
“creek,” and when ?
Kwong Toe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LITTLE Kwong Toe came aboard the
Arrawatta, at Brisbane, accompanied by
an old Chinese doctor named On Yik.
Kwong was 12 years old ; he was on his way to
Townsville. There were no other Chinese aboard,
and the doctor asked me if I would keep in touch
with his pleasant-eyed little friend during the
trip North. Before taking his leave, On Yik
informed me that Kwong was the son and heir of
a world-famous mandarin of the hirst and Last
Button.
Looking at the young Celestial’s feminine com-
plexion, his shapely feet and ladylike hands, I
began to understand what people meant when
they spoke of the high caste Manchu.
The Duke of York’s baggage-room on board the
Ophir was a rag-shop compared with little
Kwong’s. His cabin looked as though manager
Williamson had piled the costumes of his last
nine operas around the walls. The luggage of a
European gentleman is always a blot and an
eyesore on shipboard—this includes his shoehorns
bootjacks, corsets and necktie-mounted braces.
The high-born Celestial’s wardrobe is an artistic
joy in 32 colors.
After his morning bath, little Kwong Toe
wrapped himself in two furlongs of flesh-white
silk. An hour was spent in manicuring his pink
toes and finger nails. Then he put on a coat of
elephant-colored pongee, clasped by a gold belt
and decorated with a silver dwarf and peacock.
Donning my Birmingham-made shooting jacket
and striped pants, I would accompany Kwong Toe,
aged 12, on his morning promenade along the
deck. Some ladies, belonging to the Government
House party, asked him to sit near them one day.
For a moment the fury of a little devil illumined
his eyes. Then he ran below and flung himself
weeping on his bed. He begged me to protect
him, in future, from similar insults.
If those ladies had belonged to his father’s
household, it is probable that an auctioneer or
executioner would have dealt with them immedi-
ately after tea.
Few Australians understand how Chinese chil-
dren amuse themselves. The old men fly kites;
the brats are supposed to do the hard thinking.
Kwong had about lewt. of toys. There were
duck-footed Mongolian birds that flew about the
cabin and pecked at the scattered rice. There
were bronze beetles which scuttled across the floor
and drove about 400 real cockroaches out of the
cabin for the rest of the voyage. The boss toy
was a brass volcano that emitted sparks and smoke
whenever he spoke to it. He showed me a model
earthquake scene among temples and pagodas. A
Chinese magician had made it for him. There
were rivers of glass and mountains of terra-cotta,
and when Kwong breathed the dreadful key
word, the mountains rent themselves asunder and
the temples broke in halves and ran down the
glass rivers. Kwong would then touch a single
block and the desolation and chaos would piece
itself together.
He kept the earthquake in a large wooden box..
Sometimes in the still hours of night it would
start like an alarm-clock and rattle the fittings
from the cabin walls. Kwong in his silk night-
shirt.would sit up in his bunk and address it in
violent Chinese ; only threats of the severest kind
could make it stop.
One day he exhibited a fight between two metal
dragons. They lived in brass pagodas facing one
another. One of them, Mik Mik, would snort
and wipe the atmosphere with his tail, and ask
the other dragon, Po Po, to come and help clean
the cabin floor. Po Po was a modest little dragon,
who had to fight against his will. I observed a
tendency on the part of Mik Mik to knock Po
Po’s head when he wasn’t looking. The fight
lasted about ten minutes. Mik Mik always won.
Kwong said they were not wound up. I ex-
amined Mik Mik carefully, and he bit my finger.
I left Kwong Toe at Townsville, after seeing
him safely housed with his relatives. Then I
started for the Towers. For three months I
hugged financial blizzards with both arms, and
when I retm ned to Townsville there was a lone-
some, feeling about my pockets, a desire in me to
put out to sea on a raft and burgle a man-o’ war.
Townsville is the place where the unemployed
die of thirst. ICis the place where the shipping
clerk works far hi to the hot midnight with a fear
in his heart that another fellow will swoop upon
his billet. A firm with a dawn-till-midnight billet
to spare offered it to me at 255. per week. I
didn’t refuse the job. I simply introduced the
manager to a Chow.
Sitting with the unemployed on Bobbie Towns’
bridge, one day, a moon-eyed Chinaman touched
my sleeve and asked me to follow him. At that
moment I would have followed a cannibal chief
to the bitter end. The Chinaman escorted me
into a large store, where another Chinaman, with
the face of a grindstone, pointed to an inner
room. Therein sat little Kwong Toe on a pile of
cushions.
Before leaving I was taken aside by the grind-
stone Chow. “ You welly hard-up, I see.
Kwong Toe ask me to lend you his cheque. Five,
ten, twenty pons; how much you want?” said
the Grindstone. His face was perfectly immov-
able ; his eyes were flint, his skin was harder than
the front of a pyramid. I wondered how much
pull little Kwong possessed to wring such an
arrangement from him.
“ Make it a tenner,” I said.
Two years later I sent the cash to Towns-
ville, but the Grindstone replied stating that
Kwong had gone back to Pekin. The money was
returned.
It seemed that the slight service rendered was
not to be soon forgotten. A month or so after-
wards I received a letter bearing the Hong Kong
post mark. After mentioning that his family
were in good health, Kwong begged me, as a mark
of honor, to accept the love of his sisters, Moo
Ying and Sisa Foo, aged four and six. He pro-
mised, at an early date, to send me a brass volcano
and the earthquake.
When the wind of adversity blows around the
office on pay-day, it is comforting to know that
one’s name is held in supreme veneration in cer-
tain parts of China.
Up to date the earthquake hasn’t arrived.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
